Hierarchy of Agnatic Heirs in Hanafi Law
Listing the hierarchy of agnatic heirs in order of priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi order of agnatic heirs from the son to the uncles of the grandfather and cite the Quranic proofs for the son's and father's precedence.

Core Idea
The son is the primary agnate; the father takes a fixed share when a son exists, but becomes agnate otherwise.

This lesson from Bahr al-Ra'iq delineates the hierarchical order of agnatic heirs in the Hanafi school. The author employs Quranic verses and rational reasoning to establish the priority of each category. By mastering this order, you will be able to correctly distribute the residuary estate after fixed shares are allocated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary agnate
Start with the son, who is the most entitled agnate. The proof is from Quran 4:11, which assigns fixed shares to parents only when a child exists, implying the child takes the residue as an agnate.
The son always excludes all other agnates.
2
Extend to the son's son
The son's son (even downward) stands in the place of the son and is given precedence over the father and all other agnates except the son.
This is based on the principle that the grandson represents the son.
3
Place the father and grandfather
After the son and his descendants, the father is next, then the grandfather (father's father) even if ascending high. The proof is from the kalala verse (4:176) which restricts sibling inheritance to cases with no parent or child, and from the consensus of the Companions.
The father excludes all siblings and their descendants.
4
List siblings and their descendants
After the grandfather, the full brother is next, then the consanguine brother, then their sons, then paternal uncles, uncles of the father, and uncles of the grandfather. Full siblings are prioritized due to stronger lineage (ties from both parents).
The order among siblings: full brother > half brother > son of full brother > son of half brother.
5
Apply internal ordering among uncles
Among uncles, the full paternal uncle precedes the half paternal uncle; if degrees differ, the higher degree (closer to the deceased) takes precedence.
The same ordering applies to uncles of the father and grandfather.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والاحق الابن ثم ابنه وإن سفل) وغيرهم محجوبون بهم لقوله تعالى يوصيكم الله في أولادكم للذكر مثل حظ الانثيين إلى أن قال سبحانه وتعالى ولأبويه لكل واحد منهم السدس مما ترك إن كان له ولد ﴾ [النساء : ۱۱] فجعل الأب صاحب فرض مع الولد ولم يجعل للولد الذكر سهماً مقرراً فتعين الباقي له فدل أن الولد الذكر مقدم عليه بالعصوبة. وابن الابن ابن وإن سفل كالابن على ما بينا لأنه يقوم مقامه فيقدم عليه أيضاً، ومن حيث المعقول إن الإنسان يؤثر ولده على والده ويختار صرف ماله له ولأجله يدخر ماله عادة إلا أنا صرفنا مقدار الفرض إلى أصحاب الفروض بالنص فيبقى الباقي على قضية الدليل وكان ينبغي أن يقدم البنت أيضاً عليه وعلى كل عصبة إلا أن الشارع أبطل اختياره بتعيين الفرض لها وجعل الباقي لأولى رجل. قال رحمه الله : (ثم الأب ثم أب الأب وإن علا) أي ثم أولادهم بالعصوبة أصول الميت وإن علوا وأولاهم به الأب لأن الله تعالى شرط الإرث للإخوة بالكلالة وهو الذي لا ولد له ولا والد على ما بينا فعلم بذلك أنهم لا يرثون مع الأب ضرورة وعليه إجماع الأمة، فإذا كان ذلك مع الإخوة وهم أقرب الناس إليه بعد فروعه وأصوله فما ظنك بمن هو أبعد منه كأعمامهم وأعمام أبيه والجدات ألا ترى أنه يقوم مقامه في الولاية عند عدم الأب ويقدم على الإخوة فيه فكذا في الميراث وهو قول أبي بكر الصديق وابن عباس وعائشة وأبي موسى الأشعري وأبي الدرداء وأبي الطفيل وابن الزبير ومعاذ بن جبل وجابر بن عبد الله وجماعة آخرين منهم رضي الله عنهم وبه أخذ أبو حنيفة . قال رحمه الله : (ثم الأخ لأب وأم ثم الأخ لأب ثم ابن الأخ لأب وأم ثم ابن الأخ لأب ثم الأعمام ثم أعمام الأب ثم أعمام الجد على الترتيب) وإنما قدموا على الأعمام لأن الله تعالى جعل الإرث في الكلالة للإخوة عند عدم الولد والوالد بقوله تعالى وهو يرثها إن لم يكن لها ولد ﴾ [النساء : ١٧٦] فعلم بذلك أنهم يقدمون على الأعمام لأنهم جزء الجد، وإنما قدم الأخ لأب وأم لأنه أقوى نسباً من الجانبين فكان ذا قرابتين بني وكذا الأخت لأم وأب تقدم إذا صارت عصبة على الأخت لأب لما ذكرنا ولهذا يقدم في الفرض فكذا في العصوبة. قال رحمه الله : (ثم الأعمام ثم أعمام الأب ثم أعمام الجد على الترتيب) أي أولاهم بالميراث بعد الإخوة أعمام الميت لأنهم بعد ذلك جزء الجد فكانوا أقرب وقد قال الحقوا الفرائض بأهلها فما أبقت فلا أولى رجل ثم أعمام الأب لأنهم أقرب بعد ذلك لأنهم جزء الجد ثم أعمام الجد لأنهم أقرب بعدهم. وقوله «على الترتيب» أي على الترتيب الذي ذكرنا في الإخوة وهو أن يقدم العم لأب وأم على العم ثم العم لأب على ولد العم لأب وأم ، وكذا يعمل في أعمام الأب يقدم منهم ذو قرابتين عند الاستواء في الدرجة وعند التفاوت في الدرجة يقدم الأعلى.
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And the most entitled is the son, then his son, even if downward). And others are excluded by them due to His statement, 'Allah instructs you concerning your children: for the male is like the share of two females,' until He, exalted, said, 'And for his parents, for each one of them is a sixth of what he leaves, if he has a child' [4:11]. So He made the father a fixed-share holder with the child, and He did not assign a prescribed share to the male child; thus the residue is allocated to him, indicating that the male child is given precedence over him by agnation. And the son's son, even if downward, is like the son as we explained because he stands in his place, so he is given precedence over him as well. And from the rational perspective, a person prefers his child over his father and chooses to direct his wealth to him; for his sake, he usually saves his wealth, except that we have directed the amount of the fixed shares to the fixed-share holders by text, so the remainder follows the indication of the evidence. And it would have been necessary to give precedence to the daughter as well over him and every agnate, except that the Lawgiver nullified his choice by assigning a fixed share to her and made the remainder for the nearest male relative. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (Then the father, then the father's father, even if upwards). That is, then their agnate descendants are the roots of the deceased, even if upwards, and the foremost among them is the father, because Allah conditioned inheritance for siblings by kalala, which is one who has no child nor parent, as we explained. Thus it is known that they do not inherit with the father necessarily, and upon this is the consensus of the ummah. If this is the case with siblings, who are the closest people to him after his descendants and roots, then what do you think of those farther than them, like their uncles and the uncles of his father and grandmothers? Do you not see that he stands in his place in guardianship when the father is absent and is given precedence over siblings in that? So likewise in inheritance. This is the saying of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, Ibn Abbas, Aisha, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Abu al-Darda', Abu al-Tufayl, Ibn al-Zubayr, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Jabir ibn Abdullah, and a group of others among them, may Allah be pleased with them, and Abu Hanifa took it. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (Then the full brother, then the consanguine brother, then the full brother's son, then the consanguine brother's son, then paternal uncles, then uncles of the father, then uncles of the grandfather, in order). And they are given precedence over the uncles because Allah made inheritance in kalala for siblings when there is no child or parent, by His statement, 'And he inherits from her if she has no child' [4:176]. Thus it is known that they are given precedence over paternal uncles because they are a part of the grandfather. And the full brother is given precedence because he is stronger in lineage from both sides, so he possesses two kinship ties. Similarly, the full sister is given precedence when she becomes an agnate over the consanguine sister for what we mentioned; hence she is given precedence in the prescribed share, so likewise in agnation. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (Then the paternal uncles, then the uncles of the father, then the uncles of the grandfather, in order). That is, the foremost in inheritance after siblings are the paternal uncles of the deceased, because after that they are part of the grandfather, so they are closer. And he said, 'Attach the prescribed shares to their people, and whatever remains is for the nearest male relative.' Then the uncles of the father, because they are closer after that, since they are part of the grandfather. Then the uncles of the grandfather, because they are closer after them. And His statement 'in order' means according to the order we mentioned regarding siblings, which is that a full paternal uncle is given precedence over a consanguine paternal uncle, then a consanguine paternal uncle over a full paternal uncle's son, and so on. Similarly, with the uncles of the father, preference is given to the one with two kinship ties when equal in degree, and when different in degree, the higher degree is preferred.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the order of agnatic heirs in the Hanafi school. It begins with the son as the primary agnate, then the son's son (even if many generations down), then the father, then the father's father (grandfather), then siblings (full brother first, then half brother), then their sons, then uncles (first full, then half), then uncles of the father, then uncles of the grandfather. The author provides Quranic proofs: for the son's priority, the verse giving parents fixed shares only when there is a child implies the child takes the residue; for the father's priority over siblings, the kalala verse indicates siblings inherit only when no parent or child exists. The text also affirms that the father's precedence is established by consensus and the practice of the Companions, including Abu Bakr and Ibn Abbas, and that Abu Hanifa adopted this view. The commentary also explains the internal order among siblings and uncles: full siblings precede half siblings because of stronger lineage, and within the same category, precedence is given by extra kinship ties or by proximity.

Key Terms
كلالة (kalala): Deceased leaving no parent or child; siblings inherit in this case.
الابن (al-ibn): Son; the primary agnatic heir.
ابن الابن (ibn al-ibn): Son's son (grandson) who stands in place of son for agnation.
الأب (al-ab): Father; takes fixed share when child exists, else becomes agnate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, who is the first agnate after the son's son?
A) The father
B) The grandfather
C) The full brother
D) The paternal uncle

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi order of agnatic heirs is derived from Quranic structure and consensus, with priority given to the son, then his descendants, then the father, then grandfather, then siblings with preference for full lineage, and then uncles.

The Nature of Wala': Is the Right of Patronage Inheritable?
Explaining inheritance by wala' and the dispute whether wala' is inheritable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on the inheritability of wala', identify the intra-madhhab disagreement, and apply the ruling to a simple inheritance scenario.

Core Idea
Wala' is a legal relationship like lineage, not property — it cannot be inherited by the manumitter's heirs.

When a slave is freed, a bond of patronage (wala') is established between the manumitter and the freed slave. This bond gives the manumitter the right to inherit from the freed slave if the latter has no blood relatives. But what happens when the manumitter himself dies? Can his heirs step into his shoes as inheritors from the freed slave? The Hanafi school is divided on this question, with the majority holding that wala' is not inheritable. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of wala'
Understand that wala' arises from emancipation, not from contract or inheritance. It is a legal bond created by the act of freeing a slave.
The hadith 'الولاء لمن أعتق' establishes that wala' belongs to the manumitter.
2
State the Hanafi majority view
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that wala' is not inheritable. It remains with the manumitter; his agnates inherit from the freed slave by being his closest agnates, not by inheriting wala'.
This is the zahir al-riwaya position.
3
Recognize the dissenting view
Abu Yusuf holds that wala' is inheritable, dividing it among the manumitter's heirs like property, with the male receiving double the female's share.
This view is also attributed to Ibn Mas'ud in one narration.
4
Compare through a practical example
Consider: freed slave dies leaving a son and a daughter; then the manumitter dies leaving a son and a daughter. Under the majority, only the manumitter's son inherits from the freed slave. Under Abu Yusuf, both the son and daughter inherit with the son getting double.
This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
5
Analyze the proofs
The majority relies on the hadith 'الولاء لمن أعتق' and the analogy of wala' to lineage (لحمة). Abu Yusuf analogizes wala' to other inheritable rights. The text argues that wala' is not property and cannot be sold or gifted, strengthening the majority view.
Tarjih is based on the explicit text and the nature of wala'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ثم المعتق) لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الولاء لحمة كلحمة النسب وهو آخر العصبات لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لمن أعتق عبداً مات ولم يدع وارثاً كنت عصبة له قال في التعصيب من جهة النسب فهو نوعان: مولى استحقاقه العتاقه ومولى الموالاة. أما الكلام في مولى العتاقة فنقول : تكلم المشايخ في سبب الإرث قال بعضهم شبيه الإعتاق والنص يشهد له قال عليه الصلاة والسلام «الولاء لمن أعتق» وقال بعضهم : شبيه الملك على المعتق وهو الصحيح ألا ترى أن من ورث قريبه حتى عتق عليه وكان ولاؤه له ولا إعتاق ههنا وفي المضمرات: لا يباع الولاء ولا يوهب لأنه ليس بمال. وفي الزيادات ومن الناس من أجاز هبته والصحيح ما قلنا ويكون لأقرب الناس عصبة من المعتق حتى لو مات مولى العتاقة وترك ابنه وبنته ثم المعتق فميراثه لابن المعتق ولا شيء لبنت المعتق، وكذلك إذا مات مولى العتاقة وترك أباً وابناً ثم مات المعتق كان ميراثه لابن المعتق ولا شيء لأبيه لأن الابن أقرب العصبات إليه . فالحاصل أن الولاء نفسه لا يورث بل هو للمعتق على حاله ألا ترى أن المعتق ينسب بالولاء إلى المعتق دون أولاده فيكون استحقاق الإرث بالولاء لمن هو منسوب إليه حقيقة ثم يخلفه فيه أقرب عصبة كما يخلف في ماله فينظر إلى موت المعتق إذ مولى العتاقة لو كان حياً في هذه الحالة ومات من يرثه من عصباته وهو أقرب الناس إليه فيرث ذلك الشخص من المعتق وفي الذخيرة: وهذا الذي ذكرنا أن الولاء لا يورث ظاهر الرواية عن أصحابنا، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يورث ويقسم بين الابن والبنت للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وهكذا روي عن عبد الله بن مسعود في رواية وبه
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (Then the freed slave) due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Wala' is a relationship like the relationship of lineage.' And he is the last of the agnates due to his statement, peace be upon him, to one who freed a slave: 'If he dies and does not leave an heir, you are his agnate.' He said regarding agnation from the side of lineage: there are two types: a master whose entitlement is by emancipation and a master by alliance. As for the discussion of the master by emancipation: the scholars (mashayikh) spoke about the cause of inheritance. Some said it is similar to emancipation, and the text bears witness to it; he said, peace be upon him: 'Wala' belongs to the one who frees.' Others said it is similar to ownership over the freed slave, and that is correct. Do you not see that someone who inherits from his relative until he becomes free (i.e., the relative is manumitted) and his wala' belongs to him, yet there is no emancipation here? And in al-Mudmarat: wala' cannot be sold or gifted because it is not property. In al-Ziyadat: some people permit gifting it, but the correct is what we said. And it belongs to the closest agnates of the manumitter. So if the freed slave dies leaving a son and a daughter, then the manumitter dies, his estate goes to the manumitter's son and nothing to the manumitter's daughter. Similarly, if the freed slave dies leaving a father and a son, then the manumitter dies, his estate goes to the manumitter's son and nothing to his father, because the son is the closest agnate to him. The conclusion is that wala' itself is not inherited; rather, it remains with the manumitter in its state. Do you not see that the manumitter is attributed by wala' to the freed slave, not to his children? Thus entitlement to inheritance by wala' is for the one to whom it is truly attributed, and then the closest agnate takes his place, just as he takes his place in his property. So consider the death of the manumitter; if the freed slave were alive at that time and someone from his agnates who is the closest to him dies, that person would inherit from the manumitter. In al-Dhakhira: what we have mentioned — that wala' is not inherited — is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) from our companions. And from Abu Yusuf it is inherited and divided between the son and daughter, for the male like the share of two females. And similarly it is narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud in one narration.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of inheritance by wala' (the bond of manumission). The matn states that after all agnates of lineage, the freed slave (more precisely, the manumitter of the freed slave) inherits as an agnate. The commentary explains that wala' is a relationship akin to lineage, and cites a hadith that the manumitter is the last agnate if the freed slave has no blood heirs. The author then explores the legal nature of wala': some scholars view it as resembling emancipation, while others view it as resembling ownership over the freed slave; the latter is deemed correct. Wala' cannot be sold or gifted because it is not property. The key ruling: when a freed slave dies, his estate goes to the manumitter's closest agnates, with preference to males. For example, if the freed slave leaves a son and a daughter, then the manumitter dies, the manumitter's son inherits from the freed slave, not the daughter. This shows that wala' itself is not inherited; it remains with the manumitter, and his agnates inherit by virtue of being his heirs, not by inheriting the wala'. The relied-upon view (zahir al-riwaya) is that wala' is not inheritable. However, there is a transmission from Abu Yusuf that wala' is inherited like other property, divided among the manumitter's children with the male getting double the female. This is also attributed to Ibn Mas'ud. The commentary affirms that the majority and correct view is that wala' is not inheritable.

Key Terms
المعتق (al-mu'tiq): The manumitter; the one who frees a slave and thereby becomes entitled to inheritance by wala'.
الولاء (al-wala'): The bond of patronage arising from manumission, giving the manumitter inheritance rights from the freed slave.
مولى العتاقة (mawla al-itaqa): The freed slave; also the manumitter, but here it refers to the freed slave in context.
مولى الموالاة (mawla al-muwalah): A master by alliance; a type of wala' contracted by agreement, not by emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what happens to the wala' when the manumitter dies?
A) It is inherited by his children like other property.
B) It is canceled and the freed slave has no heir.
C) It remains with the manumitter; his agnates inherit from the freed slave.
D) It goes to the state.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority holds that wala' is not inheritable; it remains with the manumitter, and his closest agnates inherit from the freed slave by substitution.

Order of 'Asaba: Agnatic Heirs in Hanafi Inheritance
Order of 'asaba heirs with reasoning from Quran and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct order of 'asaba heirs in Hanafi inheritance and identify the Quranic proof for sibling priority over uncles.

Core Idea
The closer the agnatic relation, the stronger the claim; full siblings precede half, and all siblings precede uncles.

In Hanafi inheritance law, after the Quranic fixed-share heirs receive their portions, the residue (if any) goes to the 'asaba—the agnatic male relatives. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its explanation in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes a clear hierarchy among 'asaba heirs. Understanding this order is essential for correctly distributing the inheritance, especially when multiple male relatives survive.

Guided Path
1
List the Categories
The passage enumerates the 'asaba in a specific sequence: full brother, half-brother (from father), son of full brother, son of half-brother, then paternal uncles (full and half), then paternal uncles of the father, then paternal uncles of the grandfather, and finally the emancipator and his 'asaba. Write out this list to internalize the order.
The order is strictly hierarchical; each category excludes more distant ones.
2
Identify the Proof for Sibling Priority
The commentary cites Quran 4:176, which in the context of kalalah (no child or parent) grants inheritance to siblings. The Hanafi school uses this to argue that siblings are closer than uncles because they are 'part of the grandfather' (i.e., directly descended from the same ancestor). Recognize that this is a qiyas: the verse's ruling is extended to priority over uncles.
The dalil is Quranic but its application to this hierarchy involves ijtihad.
3
Understand Full vs. Half Preference
The full brother (from both parents) is given precedence over the half-brother (from father only) because he has two blood ties (both sides), making his lineage 'stronger'. This same logic applies to sisters when they become 'asaba bi-ghayrihim (with daughters).
Half-brothers from mother are not 'asaba; they are fixed-share heirs.
4
Apply the Same Logic to Higher Uncles
The phrase 'in order' means that within each category (e.g., paternal uncles), the same hierarchy applies: full uncle over half uncle, and closer degree over farther. For example, the paternal uncle (full) precedes the paternal uncle (half), and the paternal uncle of the father precedes the paternal uncle of the grandfather.
The 'closer' refers to proximity to the deceased by lineage.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i View
Note that this order is specific to the Hanafi school. The Shafi'i school, for instance, gives the grandfather precedence over siblings. Recognizing this ikhtilaf helps in understanding the Hanafi logic and its practical implications.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both schools have strong proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علا ثم الأخ لأب وأم ثم الأخ لأب ثم ابن الأخ لأب وأم ثم ابن الأخ لأب ثم الأعمام ثم أعمام الأب ثم أعمام الجد على الترتيب ثم المعتق ثم عصبته على الترتيب
العلات
الله : (ثم الأخ لأب وأم ثم الأخ لأب ثم ابن الأخ لأب وأم ثم ابن الأخ لأب) وإنما قدموا على الأعمام لأن الله تعالى جعل الإرث في الكلالة للإخوة عند عدم الولد والوالد بقوله تعالى {وهو يرثها إن لم يكن لها ولد} [النساء : ١٧٦] فعلم بذلك أنهم يقدمون على الأعمام لأنهم جزء الجد، وإنما قدم الأخ لأب وأم لأنه أقوى نسباً من الجانبين فكان ذا قرابتين بني وكذا الأخت لأم وأب تقدم إذا صارت عصبة على الأخت لأب لما ذكرنا ولهذا يقدم في الفرض فكذا في العصوبة.
قال رحمه الله : (ثم الأعمام ثم أعمام الأب ثم أعمام الجد على الترتيب) أي أولاهم بالميراث بعد الإخوة أعمام الميت لأنهم بعد ذلك جزء الجد فكانوا أقرب وقد قال الحقوا الفرائض بأهلها فما أبقت فلا أولى رجل ثم أعمام الأب لأنهم أقرب بعد ذلك لأنهم جزء الجد ثم أعمام الجد لأنهم أقرب بعدهم. وقوله «على الترتيب» أي على الترتيب الذي ذكرنا في الإخوة وهو أن يقدم العم لأب وأم على العم ثم العم لأب على ولد العم لأب وأم، وكذا يعمل في أعمام الأب يقدم منهم ذو قرابتين عند الاستواء في الدرجة وعند التفاوت في الدرجة يقدم الأعلى.
Then the brother from father and mother, then the brother from father, then the son of brother from father and mother, then the son of brother from father, then the paternal uncles, then the paternal uncles of the father, then the paternal uncles of the grandfather in order, then the emancipator, then his 'asaba in order. The reason: (Then the brother from father and mother, then the brother from father, then the son of brother from father and mother, then the son of brother from father) and they are given precedence over the paternal uncles because Allah made inheritance in kalalah for siblings in the absence of child and parent by His statement {and he inherits from her if she has no child} [An-Nisa 176]. So it is known that they are given precedence over the paternal uncles because they are part of the grandfather. And the brother from father and mother is given precedence because he is stronger in lineage from both sides, so he has two relationships of kinship. Likewise, the sister from mother and father is given precedence when she becomes an 'asaba over the sister from father for what we mentioned, and for this reason she is given precedence in the prescribed share, so likewise in 'asabah. He (the author) said: (Then the paternal uncles, then the paternal uncles of the father, then the paternal uncles of the grandfather in order) meaning the first in inheritance after the siblings are the paternal uncles of the deceased because after that they are part of the grandfather so they are closer. And he said: Attach the prescribed shares to their people; what remains goes to the nearest male. Then the paternal uncles of the father because they are closer after that because they are part of the grandfather, then the paternal uncles of the grandfather because they are closer after them. His statement 'in order' means in the order we mentioned for the siblings: that the paternal uncle from father and mother is given precedence over the paternal uncle, then the paternal uncle from father over the son of the paternal uncle from father and mother, and likewise it is done for the paternal uncles of the father: among them the one with two relationships is given precedence when equal in degree, and when differing in degree the higher is given precedence.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, explaining the order of 'asaba (agnatic heirs) after the deceased. The order is: full brother (from father and mother), then half-brother from father, then son of full brother, then son of half-brother from father, then paternal uncles (full then half), then paternal uncles of the father, then paternal uncles of the grandfather, then the emancipator (mu'tiq) and his 'asaba. The commentary provides the reasoning: siblings are preferred over paternal uncles because Allah specifies inheritance for siblings in the verse on kalalah (An-Nisa 176). Among siblings, full brother is preferred due to stronger lineage from both parents. This same logic applies to sisters when they become 'asaba (e.g., with daughters). For uncles, the order follows the same principle: closer degree and stronger connection through both parents take precedence. The phrase 'on the order' indicates the same hierarchical logic applies within each category.

Key Terms
عصبة ('asaba): Agnatic heirs who inherit the residue after prescribed shares. They are male relatives through the male line.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi order of 'asaba in this lesson, who inherits first after the siblings?
A) Paternal uncles
B) Sons of brothers
C) Grandfather
D) Emancipator

Key Takeaway
The order of 'asaba in Hanafi law is based on closeness of agnatic relation: full siblings then half siblings then their sons, then uncles, following a consistent hierarchy that prioritizes lineage strength and degree.

Cause and Nature of Wala' al-'Itaqa
Opening of the section on wala' al-'itaqa: causes and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the cause of inheritance by wala' al-'itaqa and explain why wala' is not considered property in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Wala' is a cause of 'asabah, not property; it cannot be sold or gifted.

This lesson examines the foundational principles of wala' al-'itaqa. We explore the scholarly discussion on its cause and clarify its legal nature as a non-transferable bond of clientage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions on the cause
Some scholars liken the cause to emancipation itself; others to ownership over the freed slave. Determine which is preferred and why.
The text gives preference to the ownership view.
2
Examine the textual evidence
The hadith 'al-wala' li-man a'taqa' is cited. Understand how it supports the cause of emancipation.
The hadith is ahad, so it gives zanni certainty.
3
Understand why wala' is not property
The text states wala' cannot be sold or given as a gift because it is not mal. Reflect on the implications for inheritance.
A minority view allowing gifts is rejected.
4
Distinguish wala' from property rights
Wala' is a personal bond of 'asabah, not a transferable asset. This affects who inherits and how.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ثم المعتق) لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الولاء لحمة كلحمة النسب وهو آخر العصبات لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لمن أعتق عبداً مات ولم يدع وارثاً كنت عصبة له قال في التعصيب من جهة النسب فهو نوعان: مولى استحقاقه العتاقه ومولى الموالاة. أما الكلام في مولى العتاقة فنقول : تكلم المشايخ في سبب الإرث قال بعضهم شبيه الإعتاق والنص يشهد له قال عليه الصلاة والسلام «الولاء لمن أعتق» وقال بعضهم : شبيه الملك على المعتق وهو الصحيح ألا ترى أن من ورث قريبه حتى عتق عليه وكان ولاؤه له ولا إعتاق ههنا وفي المضمرات: لا يباع الولاء ولا يوهب لأنه ليس بمال. وفي الزيادات ومن الناس من أجاز هبته والصحيح ما قلنا ويكون لأقرب الناس عصبة من المعتق حتى لو مات مولى العتاقة وترك ابنه وبنته ثم المعتق فميراثه لابن المعتق ولا شيء لبنت المعتق، وكذلك إذا مات مولى العتاقة وترك أباً وابناً ثم مات المعتق كان ميراثه لابن المعتق ولا شيء لأبيه لأن الابن أقرب العصبات إليه . فالحاصل أن الولاء نفسه لا يورث بل هو للمعتق على حاله ألا ترى أن المعتق ينسب بالولاء إلى المعتق دون أولاده فيكون استحقاق الإرث بالولاء لمن هو منسوب إليه حقيقة ثم يخلفه فيه أقرب عصبة كما يخلف في ماله فينظر إلى موت المعتق إذ مولى العتاقة لو كان حياً في هذه الحالة ومات من يرثه من عصباته وهو أقرب الناس إليه فيرث ذلك الشخص من المعتق وفي الذخيرة: وهذا الذي ذكرنا أن الولاء لا يورث ظاهر الرواية عن أصحابنا، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يورث ويقسم بين الابن والبنت للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وهكذا روي عن عبد الله بن مسعود في رواية وبه أخذ ابراهيم النخعي وشريح القاضي. وإذا مات المعتق ولم يترك إلا بنت المعتق فلا شيء لها في ظاهر الرواية عن أصحابنا ويكون الميراث لبيت المال وحكي عن بعض مشايخنا أنهم كانوا يفتون في هذه المسألة أن يدفع المال إليها لا بطريق الإرث ولكن لأنها أقرب إلى الميت من بيت المال كيف وأنه ليس في زماننا بيت المال، وإنما كان كذلك في زمن الصحابة. وإذا دفع ذلك إلى سلطان الوقت أو القاضي لا يصرفون إلى مصرفه هكذا كان يفتي القاضي أبو بكر وصدر الشريعة. وذكر الإمام عبد الواحد الشهيد في فرائضه أن الفاضل عن سهام الزوج والزوجة لا يوضع في بيت المال بل يدفع إليهما لأنهما أقرب إلى الميت من جهة النسب وكان الدفع إليهما أولى من غيرهما، وكذلك الابن والابنة من الرضاع إذا لم يكن للميت غيرهما يدفع المال إليهما وعصبة المعتق ترث أما عصبة الورثة لا يرث مثاله: امرأة أعتقت عبداً وماتت وتركت ابناً وزوجاً ثم مات المعتق فالميراث لابن المعتق لأنه عصبتها، ولو كان الابن ثم مات وترك أباه وهو زوج المعتقة لا يرث لأن أب الابن ليس عصبة المعتق. وإذا أعتق الرجل عبداً ثم أعتق المعتق الثاني عبداً ثم مات المعتق الثالث وترك عصبة المعتق الأول لا غير يرث منه. ولو أن امرأة اشترت أباها حتى أعتق عليها ثم مات الأب وترك هذه المشترية وبنتاً أخرى فميراث المعتق أثلاثاً وكان الثلثان بينهما على السوية بحكم الفرض والثلث الآخر للمشتري بحكم الولاء وكثير من هذا الفصل قدم في كتاب الولاء. وأما الكلام في ولاء الموالاة فنقول : تفسير ولاء الموالاة أن يسلم الرجل على يد رجل فيقول للذي أسلم على يديه أو لغيره واليتك على أني إن مت فميراثي لك. وفي شرح الطحاوي: إن مت ولم يكن لي وارث لا من جهة الفريضة ولا من جهة العصبة ولا من جهة ذوي الأرحام فميراثي لك، وإن جنيت فعقلي عليك وعلى عاقلتك وقبل الآخر فهذا هو تفسير ولاء الموالاة، فإذا جنى الأسفل جناية فعقله على عاقلة المولى الأعلى وإذا مات الأسفل يرث منه المولى الأعلى، وإن مات لا يرث منه المولى الأسفل ولا تثبت هذه الأحكام بمجرد الإسلام بدون عبد الموالاة، وإذا مات الأسفل فميراث الأسفل لأقرب الناس عصبة إلى الأعلى كما في ولاء العتاقة، ولكل واحد منهما أن ينقض عقد الموالاة وليس له أن يجعل الولاء إلى غير فإنه لو قال جعلت ولائي لفلان لا يصير له والأسفل له أن يتحول بالولاء إلى غيره فإن له أن يوالي مع آخر مع الأول، وإن والى مع غيره ينتقض الأول، وإن كان الموالاة مع غيره بغيبة الأعلى. وفي الذخيرة ووالي الموالاة يخالف ولاء العتاقة من وجوه : أحدها أن في العتاقة يرث الأعلى من الأسفل ولا يرث الأسفل من الأعلى، وإن شرطوا ذلك في ولاء الموالاة يعتبر شرطهما حتى لو شرطا يرث كل واحد منهما كما شرطا والثاني أن ولاء الموالاة يحتمل النقض وولاء العتاقة لا يحتمل والثالث أن ولاء العتاقة مقدم على ذوي الأرحام ومولى الموالاة مؤخر عن ذوي الأرحام المولى الأسفل إذا أقر بأخ أو ابن عم ثم مات فميراثه المولى وينقض العقد
He (the author) said: (Then the emancipator) because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Clientage is like lineage in flesh.' And it is the last of the 'asaba because of his saying, peace be upon him, to one who emancipated a slave who died leaving no heir: 'You are his 'asaba.' He said concerning 'asabah from the perspective of lineage: it is of two types: a client whose entitlement is by emancipation, and a client by contract. As for the discussion on the client of emancipation, we say: the scholars have spoken about the cause of inheritance. Some said it is similar to emancipation, and the text bears witness to it: he said, peace be upon him, 'Clientage belongs to the one who emancipates.' Some said it is similar to ownership over the emancipated one, and this is correct. Do you not see that one who inherits from his relative until he is emancipated, his clientage belongs to him, and there is no emancipation here? And in al-Mudmarat: clientage cannot be sold nor given as a gift because it is not property. And in al-Ziyadat: some people permitted its gift, but what we have said is correct. And it belongs to the nearest 'asaba of the emancipator. So if a client of emancipation dies leaving a son and a daughter, then the emancipator dies, his inheritance goes to the son of the emancipator, and nothing to the daughter of the emancipator. Similarly, if a client of emancipation dies leaving a father and a son, then the emancipator dies, his inheritance goes to the son of the emancipator, and nothing to his father, because the son is the nearest 'asaba to him. The conclusion is that clientage itself is not inherited, but it remains with the emancipator as it was. Do you not see that the emancipator is attributed by clientage to the emancipated one, not to his children? So entitlement to inheritance by clientage is for the one who is truly attributed to it, then the nearest 'asaba succeeds him in it just as he succeeds in his wealth. So one looks at the death of the emancipator: if the client of emancipation were alive in this situation, and the one who inherits from him among his 'asaba dies, and he is the nearest person to him, then that person inherits from the emancipator. And in al-Dhakhira: what we have mentioned, that clientage is not inherited, is the apparent narration from our companions. And from Abu Yusuf that it is inherited and divided between the son and daughter, with the male taking the share of two females, and thus it was narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud in one narration, and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and Shurayh al-Qadi took it. And if the emancipator dies leaving only the daughter of the client, she gets nothing in the apparent narration from our companions, and the inheritance goes to the public treasury. And it is narrated from some of our scholars that they used to give fatwa in this issue that the money should be given to her, not by way of inheritance, but because she is closer to the deceased than the public treasury, especially since we have no public treasury in our time; it was only like that in the time of the Companions. And if it is given to the ruler of the time or the judge, they do not spend it in its proper channels. This is how Qadi Abu Bakr and Sadr al-Shari'ah used to give fatwa. And Imam Abd al-Wahid al-Shahid mentioned in his Fara'id that the surplus over the shares of the husband and wife is not placed in the public treasury but is given to them because they are closer to the deceased by lineage, and giving it to them is better than to others. Likewise, the son and daughter of breastfeeding, if the deceased has no others, the money is given to them. And the 'asaba of the emancipator inherit, but the 'asaba of the heirs do not inherit. Example: a woman emancipates a slave and dies leaving a son and a husband, then the client dies: the inheritance goes to the son of the emancipator because he is her 'asaba. If the son then dies and leaves his father, who is the husband of the emancipatory woman, he does not inherit because the father of the son is not an 'asaba of the emancipator. And if a man emancipates a slave, then the second emancipated one emancipates another, then the third client dies leaving only the 'asaba of the first emancipator, they inherit from him. And if a woman buys her father until he is emancipated by her, then the father dies leaving this buyer and another daughter, the inheritance of the client is by thirds: two-thirds between them equally by virtue of prescribed share, and the other third for the buyer by virtue of clientage. Much of this section has been presented in the book of Wala'. As for the discussion on clientage by contract: its interpretation is that a man accepts Islam at the hand of another, and says to the one at whose hand he accepted Islam or to another: 'I contract clientage with you on the condition that if I die, my inheritance is for you.' And in Sharh al-Tahawi: 'If I die and I have no heir from the prescribed shares, nor from 'asaba, nor from uterine relatives, then my inheritance is for you; and if I commit a crime, my 'aql (blood money) is upon you and your 'aqila.' And the other accepts. This is the interpretation of clientage by contract. Then if the lower one commits a crime, his 'aql is upon the 'aqila of the higher client. If the lower one dies, the higher client inherits from him. If the higher dies, the lower does not inherit from him. These rulings are not established by mere Islam without the contract of clientage. When the lower dies, his inheritance goes to the nearest 'asaba to the higher, as in clientage of emancipation. Each of them has the right to rescind the contract of clientage. He does not have the right to assign clientage to another; if he says 'I make my clientage for so-and-so,' it does not become his. The lower one has the right to transfer clientage to another; he may contract clientage with another along with the first. If he contracts with another, the first is rescinded, even if the contract with the other was in the absence of the higher. In al-Dhakhira: clientage by contract differs from clientage of emancipation in several ways: first, in emancipation the higher inherits from the lower but the lower does not inherit from the higher; if they stipulate that in clientage by contract, their stipulation is considered, so if they stipulate that each inherits from the other, it is as they stipulated. Second, clientage by contract is subject to rescission, but clientage of emancipation is not. Third, clientage of emancipation takes precedence over uterine relatives, while clientage by contract is delayed after uterine relatives. When the lower client acknowledges a brother or paternal cousin, then dies, his inheritance goes to the client, and the contract is rescinded.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on wala' (clientage) from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It covers two types: wala' al-'itaqa (clientage of emancipation) and wala' al-muwalah (clientage by contract). For wala' al-'itaqa, the author explains the cause of inheritance: some scholars say it is due to emancipation (based on hadith 'al-wala' li-man a'taqa'), but the preferred view is that it is similar to ownership over the emancipated slave, as seen when a relative is inherited and becomes free without direct emancipation. Wala' itself is not property and cannot be sold or given as a gift, per the relied-upon view. The entitlement to inheritance by wala' goes to the nearest 'asaba of the emancipator, not to females of the 'asaba. The passage then discusses the dispute: in Zahir al-Riwayah, wala' is not inherited; Abu Yusuf held that it is inherited and divided between sons and daughters like property, based on a narration from Ibn Mas'ud. However, the fatwa is on the Zahir al-Riwayah position. When the emancipator dies leaving only a daughter of the client, she takes nothing in the dominant view, but later scholars (like Qadi Abu Bakr) gave fatwa to give the money to her due to the absence of a proper public treasury. The passage also provides examples clarifying that the 'asaba of the emancipator inherit, but not the 'asaba of the client's heirs. For wala' al-muwalah, the definition is given: a contract between two free men where one agrees to be the client of the other for inheritance and blood money liability. The rulings differ from emancipation wala': the lower inherits from the higher if stipulated, it is revocable, and it is subordinate to uterine relatives. The passage ends with a note that acknowledging a closer relative cancels the contract.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-wala'): A legal relationship of clientage that causes inheritance and liability for blood money, either by emancipation or by contract.
مولى العتاقة (mawla al-'itaqa): A client by emancipation, i.e., a freed slave whose inheritance goes to the emancipator's 'asaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, can wala' be given as a gift?
A) Yes, if the emancipator agrees
B) No, because it is not property
C) Yes, but only to a relative
D) No, because it belongs to the state

Key Takeaway
Wala' is a cause of 'asabah, not property, and cannot be sold or gifted.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
10 min
Inheritance by Wala' al-'Itaqa: Rules and Disputes
Detailed rules of inheritance by wala' al-'itaqa, including the intra-Hanafi dispute and exceptional cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that inheritance by wala' goes to the nearest 'asaba of the emancipator and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether wala' is inherited.

Core Idea
Inheritance by wala' goes to the nearest 'asaba of the emancipator, not to all his children equally.

This lesson dives into the practical rules of inheritance through wala' al-'itaqa. We examine who inherits, discuss the crucial dispute with Abu Yusuf, and see how later scholars handled exceptional cases.

Guided Path
1
State the rule: inheritance goes to nearest 'asaba of emancipator
The text gives examples: if client dies leaving a son and daughter, then emancipator dies, the son of emancipator inherits, not the daughter.
The 'asaba are determined at the time of the client's death, not the emancipator's.
2
Understand the principle: wala' itself is not inherited
The text insists that wala' remains with the emancipator; only the right to inherit through it passes to his 'asaba.
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf held that wala' is inherited and divided like property among the emancipator's children, male and female.
He based this on a narration from Ibn Mas'ud.
4
Analyze the practical difference
Under the relied-upon view, a daughter of the emancipator does not inherit through wala' if there is a son. Under Abu Yusuf's view, she would.
5
Consider the exceptional case of no 'asaba
If emancipator leaves only a daughter of the client, the dominant view gives nothing to her, but later scholars gave the money to her due to absence of a proper bayt al-mal.
This shows practical adaptation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ثم المعتق) لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الولاء لحمة كلحمة النسب وهو آخر العصبات لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لمن أعتق عبداً مات ولم يدع وارثاً كنت عصبة له قال في التعصيب من جهة النسب فهو نوعان: مولى استحقاقه العتاقه ومولى الموالاة. أما الكلام في مولى العتاقة فنقول : تكلم المشايخ في سبب الإرث قال بعضهم شبيه الإعتاق والنص يشهد له قال عليه الصلاة والسلام «الولاء لمن أعتق» وقال بعضهم : شبيه الملك على المعتق وهو الصحيح ألا ترى أن من ورث قريبه حتى عتق عليه وكان ولاؤه له ولا إعتاق ههنا وفي المضمرات: لا يباع الولاء ولا يوهب لأنه ليس بمال. وفي الزيادات ومن الناس من أجاز هبته والصحيح ما قلنا ويكون لأقرب الناس عصبة من المعتق حتى لو مات مولى العتاقة وترك ابنه وبنته ثم المعتق فميراثه لابن المعتق ولا شيء لبنت المعتق، وكذلك إذا مات مولى العتاقة وترك أباً وابناً ثم مات المعتق كان ميراثه لابن المعتق ولا شيء لأبيه لأن الابن أقرب العصبات إليه . فالحاصل أن الولاء نفسه لا يورث بل هو للمعتق على حاله ألا ترى أن المعتق ينسب بالولاء إلى المعتق دون أولاده فيكون استحقاق الإرث بالولاء لمن هو منسوب إليه حقيقة ثم يخلفه فيه أقرب عصبة كما يخلف في ماله فينظر إلى موت المعتق إذ مولى العتاقة لو كان حياً في هذه الحالة ومات من يرثه من عصباته وهو أقرب الناس إليه فيرث ذلك الشخص من المعتق وفي الذخيرة: وهذا الذي ذكرنا أن الولاء لا يورث ظاهر الرواية عن أصحابنا، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يورث ويقسم بين الابن والبنت للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وهكذا روي عن عبد الله بن مسعود في رواية وبه أخذ ابراهيم النخعي وشريح القاضي. وإذا مات المعتق ولم يترك إلا بنت المعتق فلا شيء لها في ظاهر الرواية عن أصحابنا ويكون الميراث لبيت المال وحكي عن بعض مشايخنا أنهم كانوا يفتون في هذه المسألة أن يدفع المال إليها لا بطريق الإرث ولكن لأنها أقرب إلى الميت من بيت المال كيف وأنه ليس في زماننا بيت المال، وإنما كان كذلك في زمن الصحابة. وإذا دفع ذلك إلى سلطان الوقت أو القاضي لا يصرفون إلى مصرفه هكذا كان يفتي القاضي أبو بكر وصدر الشريعة. وذكر الإمام عبد الواحد الشهيد في فرائضه أن الفاضل عن سهام الزوج والزوجة لا يوضع في بيت المال بل يدفع إليهما لأنهما أقرب إلى الميت من جهة النسب وكان الدفع إليهما أولى من غيرهما، وكذلك الابن والابنة من الرضاع إذا لم يكن للميت غيرهما يدفع المال إليهما وعصبة المعتق ترث أما عصبة الورثة لا يرث مثاله: امرأة أعتقت عبداً وماتت وتركت ابناً وزوجاً ثم مات المعتق فالميراث لابن المعتق لأنه عصبتها، ولو كان الابن ثم مات وترك أباه وهو زوج المعتقة لا يرث لأن أب الابن ليس عصبة المعتق. وإذا أعتق الرجل عبداً ثم أعتق المعتق الثاني عبداً ثم مات المعتق الثالث وترك عصبة المعتق الأول لا غير يرث منه. ولو أن امرأة اشترت أباها حتى أعتق عليها ثم مات الأب وترك هذه المشترية وبنتاً أخرى فميراث المعتق أثلاثاً وكان الثلثان بينهما على السوية بحكم الفرض والثلث الآخر للمشتري بحكم الولاء وكثير من هذا الفصل قدم في كتاب الولاء. وأما الكلام في ولاء الموالاة فنقول : تفسير ولاء الموالاة أن يسلم الرجل على يد رجل فيقول للذي أسلم على يديه أو لغيره واليتك على أني إن مت فميراثي لك. وفي شرح الطحاوي: إن مت ولم يكن لي وارث لا من جهة الفريضة ولا من جهة العصبة ولا من جهة ذوي الأرحام فميراثي لك، وإن جنيت فعقلي عليك وعلى عاقلتك وقبل الآخر فهذا هو تفسير ولاء الموالاة، فإذا جنى الأسفل جناية فعقله على عاقلة المولى الأعلى وإذا مات الأسفل يرث منه المولى الأعلى، وإن مات لا يرث منه المولى الأسفل ولا تثبت هذه الأحكام بمجرد الإسلام بدون عبد الموالاة، وإذا مات الأسفل فميراث الأسفل لأقرب الناس عصبة إلى الأعلى كما في ولاء العتاقة، ولكل واحد منهما أن ينقض عقد الموالاة وليس له أن يجعل الولاء إلى غير فإنه لو قال جعلت ولائي لفلان لا يصير له والأسفل له أن يتحول بالولاء إلى غيره فإن له أن يوالي مع آخر مع الأول، وإن والى مع غيره ينتقض الأول، وإن كان الموالاة مع غيره بغيبة الأعلى. وفي الذخيرة ووالي الموالاة يخالف ولاء العتاقة من وجوه : أحدها أن في العتاقة يرث الأعلى من الأسفل ولا يرث الأسفل من الأعلى، وإن شرطوا ذلك في ولاء الموالاة يعتبر شرطهما حتى لو شرطا يرث كل واحد منهما كما شرطا والثاني أن ولاء الموالاة يحتمل النقض وولاء العتاقة لا يحتمل والثالث أن ولاء العتاقة مقدم على ذوي الأرحام ومولى الموالاة مؤخر عن ذوي الأرحام المولى الأسفل إذا أقر بأخ أو ابن عم ثم مات فميراثه المولى وينقض العقد
He (the author) said: (Then the emancipator) because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Clientage is like lineage in flesh.' And it is the last of the 'asaba because of his saying, peace be upon him, to one who emancipated a slave who died leaving no heir: 'You are his 'asaba.' He said concerning 'asabah from the perspective of lineage: it is of two types: a client whose entitlement is by emancipation, and a client by contract. As for the discussion on the client of emancipation, we say: the scholars have spoken about the cause of inheritance. Some said it is similar to emancipation, and the text bears witness to it: he said, peace be upon him, 'Clientage belongs to the one who emancipates.' Some said it is similar to ownership over the emancipated one, and this is correct. Do you not see that one who inherits from his relative until he is emancipated, his clientage belongs to him, and there is no emancipation here? And in al-Mudmarat: clientage cannot be sold nor given as a gift because it is not property. And in al-Ziyadat: some people permitted its gift, but what we have said is correct. And it belongs to the nearest 'asaba of the emancipator. So if a client of emancipation dies leaving a son and a daughter, then the emancipator dies, his inheritance goes to the son of the emancipator, and nothing to the daughter of the emancipator. Similarly, if a client of emancipation dies leaving a father and a son, then the emancipator dies, his inheritance goes to the son of the emancipator, and nothing to his father, because the son is the nearest 'asaba to him. The conclusion is that clientage itself is not inherited, but it remains with the emancipator as it was. Do you not see that the emancipator is attributed by clientage to the emancipated one, not to his children? So entitlement to inheritance by clientage is for the one who is truly attributed to it, then the nearest 'asaba succeeds him in it just as he succeeds in his wealth. So one looks at the death of the emancipator: if the client of emancipation were alive in this situation, and the one who inherits from him among his 'asaba dies, and he is the nearest person to him, then that person inherits from the emancipator. And in al-Dhakhira: what we have mentioned, that clientage is not inherited, is the apparent narration from our companions. And from Abu Yusuf that it is inherited and divided between the son and daughter, with the male taking the share of two females, and thus it was narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud in one narration, and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and Shurayh al-Qadi took it. And if the emancipator dies leaving only the daughter of the client, she gets nothing in the apparent narration from our companions, and the inheritance goes to the public treasury. And it is narrated from some of our scholars that they used to give fatwa in this issue that the money should be given to her, not by way of inheritance, but because she is closer to the deceased than the public treasury, especially since we have no public treasury in our time; it was only like that in the time of the Companions. And if it is given to the ruler of the time or the judge, they do not spend it in its proper channels. This is how Qadi Abu Bakr and Sadr al-Shari'ah used to give fatwa. And Imam Abd al-Wahid al-Shahid mentioned in his Fara'id that the surplus over the shares of the husband and wife is not placed in the public treasury but is given to them because they are closer to the deceased by lineage, and giving it to them is better than to others. Likewise, the son and daughter of breastfeeding, if the deceased has no others, the money is given to them. And the 'asaba of the emancipator inherit, but the 'asaba of the heirs do not inherit. Example: a woman emancipates a slave and dies leaving a son and a husband, then the client dies: the inheritance goes to the son of the emancipator because he is her 'asaba. If the son then dies and leaves his father, who is the husband of the emancipatory woman, he does not inherit because the father of the son is not an 'asaba of the emancipator. And if a man emancipates a slave, then the second emancipated one emancipates another, then the third client dies leaving only the 'asaba of the first emancipator, they inherit from him. And if a woman buys her father until he is emancipated by her, then the father dies leaving this buyer and another daughter, the inheritance of the client is by thirds: two-thirds between them equally by virtue of prescribed share, and the other third for the buyer by virtue of clientage. Much of this section has been presented in the book of Wala'. As for the discussion on clientage by contract: its interpretation is that a man accepts Islam at the hand of another, and says to the one at whose hand he accepted Islam or to another: 'I contract clientage with you on the condition that if I die, my inheritance is for you.' And in Sharh al-Tahawi: 'If I die and I have no heir from the prescribed shares, nor from 'asaba, nor from uterine relatives, then my inheritance is for you; and if I commit a crime, my 'aql (blood money) is upon you and your 'aqila.' And the other accepts. This is the interpretation of clientage by contract. Then if the lower one commits a crime, his 'aql is upon the 'aqila of the higher client. If the lower one dies, the higher client inherits from him. If the higher dies, the lower does not inherit from him. These rulings are not established by mere Islam without the contract of clientage. When the lower dies, his inheritance goes to the nearest 'asaba to the higher, as in clientage of emancipation. Each of them has the right to rescind the contract of clientage. He does not have the right to assign clientage to another; if he says 'I make my clientage for so-and-so,' it does not become his. The lower one has the right to transfer clientage to another; he may contract clientage with another along with the first. If he contracts with another, the first is rescinded, even if the contract with the other was in the absence of the higher. In al-Dhakhira: clientage by contract differs from clientage of emancipation in several ways: first, in emancipation the higher inherits from the lower but the lower does not inherit from the higher; if they stipulate that in clientage by contract, their stipulation is considered, so if they stipulate that each inherits from the other, it is as they stipulated. Second, clientage by contract is subject to rescission, but clientage of emancipation is not. Third, clientage of emancipation takes precedence over uterine relatives, while clientage by contract is delayed after uterine relatives. When the lower client acknowledges a brother or paternal cousin, then dies, his inheritance goes to the client, and the contract is rescinded.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on wala' (clientage) from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It covers two types: wala' al-'itaqa (clientage of emancipation) and wala' al-muwalah (clientage by contract). For wala' al-'itaqa, the author explains the cause of inheritance: some scholars say it is due to emancipation (based on hadith 'al-wala' li-man a'taqa'), but the preferred view is that it is similar to ownership over the emancipated slave, as seen when a relative is inherited and becomes free without direct emancipation. Wala' itself is not property and cannot be sold or given as a gift, per the relied-upon view. The entitlement to inheritance by wala' goes to the nearest 'asaba of the emancipator, not to females of the 'asaba. The passage then discusses the dispute: in Zahir al-Riwayah, wala' is not inherited; Abu Yusuf held that it is inherited and divided between sons and daughters like property, based on a narration from Ibn Mas'ud. However, the fatwa is on the Zahir al-Riwayah position. When the emancipator dies leaving only a daughter of the client, she takes nothing in the dominant view, but later scholars (like Qadi Abu Bakr) gave fatwa to give the money to her due to the absence of a proper public treasury. The passage also provides examples clarifying that the 'asaba of the emancipator inherit, but not the 'asaba of the client's heirs. For wala' al-muwalah, the definition is given: a contract between two free men where one agrees to be the client of the other for inheritance and blood money liability. The rulings differ from emancipation wala': the lower inherits from the higher if stipulated, it is revocable, and it is subordinate to uterine relatives. The passage ends with a note that acknowledging a closer relative cancels the contract.

Key Terms
العصبة (al-'asaba): Male agnate relatives who inherit residually.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what happens to the estate of a freed slave if the emancipator died before the client, leaving a son and a daughter?
A) The estate goes to the son only
B) The estate is divided equally between the son and daughter
C) The estate goes to the public treasury
D) The estate goes to the daughter only

Key Takeaway
Inheritance by wala' goes to the nearest 'asaba of the emancipator; wala' itself is not inherited.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
8 min
Wala' al-Muwalah: Rules and Differences
Explanation of wala' al-muwalah (clientage by contract) and its differences from wala' al-'itaqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wala' al-muwalah, list its key rulings, and distinguish it from wala' al-'itaqa.

Core Idea
Wala' bi-al-muwalah is a revocable contract that creates inheritance rights only when stipulated, and it is subordinate to blood relatives.

This lesson introduces wala' al-muwalah, a contract of clientage between two free persons. We examine its definition, essential rulings, and the three major differences from wala' al-'itaqa.

Guided Path
1
Define wala' al-muwalah
It is a contract where a person accepts Islam at the hand of another and agrees that the latter inherits from him and bears blood money liability.
The contract must be explicit.
2
Identify the rulings
The higher inherits from the lower; the lower does not inherit unless stipulated. The contract is revocable. It is subordinate to uterine relatives.
3
List the three differences from wala' al-'itaqa
(1) In 'itaqa, lower does not inherit from higher; in muwalah, they can stipulate mutual inheritance. (2) 'Itaqa is irrevocable; muwalah can be rescinded. (3) 'Itaqa takes precedence over uterine relatives; muwalah comes after them.
4
Understand the effect of acknowledging a relative
If the lower client acknowledges a blood relative, the muwalah contract is rescinded, and that relative inherits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ثم المعتق) لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الولاء لحمة كلحمة النسب وهو آخر العصبات لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لمن أعتق عبداً مات ولم يدع وارثاً كنت عصبة له قال في التعصيب من جهة النسب فهو نوعان: مولى استحقاقه العتاقه ومولى الموالاة. أما الكلام في مولى العتاقة فنقول : تكلم المشايخ في سبب الإرث قال بعضهم شبيه الإعتاق والنص يشهد له قال عليه الصلاة والسلام «الولاء لمن أعتق» وقال بعضهم : شبيه الملك على المعتق وهو الصحيح ألا ترى أن من ورث قريبه حتى عتق عليه وكان ولاؤه له ولا إعتاق ههنا وفي المضمرات: لا يباع الولاء ولا يوهب لأنه ليس بمال. وفي الزيادات ومن الناس من أجاز هبته والصحيح ما قلنا ويكون لأقرب الناس عصبة من المعتق حتى لو مات مولى العتاقة وترك ابنه وبنته ثم المعتق فميراثه لابن المعتق ولا شيء لبنت المعتق، وكذلك إذا مات مولى العتاقة وترك أباً وابناً ثم مات المعتق كان ميراثه لابن المعتق ولا شيء لأبيه لأن الابن أقرب العصبات إليه . فالحاصل أن الولاء نفسه لا يورث بل هو للمعتق على حاله ألا ترى أن المعتق ينسب بالولاء إلى المعتق دون أولاده فيكون استحقاق الإرث بالولاء لمن هو منسوب إليه حقيقة ثم يخلفه فيه أقرب عصبة كما يخلف في ماله فينظر إلى موت المعتق إذ مولى العتاقة لو كان حياً في هذه الحالة ومات من يرثه من عصباته وهو أقرب الناس إليه فيرث ذلك الشخص من المعتق وفي الذخيرة: وهذا الذي ذكرنا أن الولاء لا يورث ظاهر الرواية عن أصحابنا، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يورث ويقسم بين الابن والبنت للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وهكذا روي عن عبد الله بن مسعود في رواية وبه أخذ ابراهيم النخعي وشريح القاضي. وإذا مات المعتق ولم يترك إلا بنت المعتق فلا شيء لها في ظاهر الرواية عن أصحابنا ويكون الميراث لبيت المال وحكي عن بعض مشايخنا أنهم كانوا يفتون في هذه المسألة أن يدفع المال إليها لا بطريق الإرث ولكن لأنها أقرب إلى الميت من بيت المال كيف وأنه ليس في زماننا بيت المال، وإنما كان كذلك في زمن الصحابة. وإذا دفع ذلك إلى سلطان الوقت أو القاضي لا يصرفون إلى مصرفه هكذا كان يفتي القاضي أبو بكر وصدر الشريعة. وذكر الإمام عبد الواحد الشهيد في فرائضه أن الفاضل عن سهام الزوج والزوجة لا يوضع في بيت المال بل يدفع إليهما لأنهما أقرب إلى الميت من جهة النسب وكان الدفع إليهما أولى من غيرهما، وكذلك الابن والابنة من الرضاع إذا لم يكن للميت غيرهما يدفع المال إليهما وعصبة المعتق ترث أما عصبة الورثة لا يرث مثاله: امرأة أعتقت عبداً وماتت وتركت ابناً وزوجاً ثم مات المعتق فالميراث لابن المعتق لأنه عصبتها، ولو كان الابن ثم مات وترك أباه وهو زوج المعتقة لا يرث لأن أب الابن ليس عصبة المعتق. وإذا أعتق الرجل عبداً ثم أعتق المعتق الثاني عبداً ثم مات المعتق الثالث وترك عصبة المعتق الأول لا غير يرث منه. ولو أن امرأة اشترت أباها حتى أعتق عليها ثم مات الأب وترك هذه المشترية وبنتاً أخرى فميراث المعتق أثلاثاً وكان الثلثان بينهما على السوية بحكم الفرض والثلث الآخر للمشتري بحكم الولاء وكثير من هذا الفصل قدم في كتاب الولاء. وأما الكلام في ولاء الموالاة فنقول : تفسير ولاء الموالاة أن يسلم الرجل على يد رجل فيقول للذي أسلم على يديه أو لغيره واليتك على أني إن مت فميراثي لك. وفي شرح الطحاوي: إن مت ولم يكن لي وارث لا من جهة الفريضة ولا من جهة العصبة ولا من جهة ذوي الأرحام فميراثي لك، وإن جنيت فعقلي عليك وعلى عاقلتك وقبل الآخر فهذا هو تفسير ولاء الموالاة، فإذا جنى الأسفل جناية فعقله على عاقلة المولى الأعلى وإذا مات الأسفل يرث منه المولى الأعلى، وإن مات لا يرث منه المولى الأسفل ولا تثبت هذه الأحكام بمجرد الإسلام بدون عبد الموالاة، وإذا مات الأسفل فميراث الأسفل لأقرب الناس عصبة إلى الأعلى كما في ولاء العتاقة، ولكل واحد منهما أن ينقض عقد الموالاة وليس له أن يجعل الولاء إلى غير فإنه لو قال جعلت ولائي لفلان لا يصير له والأسفل له أن يتحول بالولاء إلى غيره فإن له أن يوالي مع آخر مع الأول، وإن والى مع غيره ينتقض الأول، وإن كان الموالاة مع غيره بغيبة الأعلى. وفي الذخيرة ووالي الموالاة يخالف ولاء العتاقة من وجوه : أحدها أن في العتاقة يرث الأعلى من الأسفل ولا يرث الأسفل من الأعلى، وإن شرطوا ذلك في ولاء الموالاة يعتبر شرطهما حتى لو شرطا يرث كل واحد منهما كما شرطا والثاني أن ولاء الموالاة يحتمل النقض وولاء العتاقة لا يحتمل والثالث أن ولاء العتاقة مقدم على ذوي الأرحام ومولى الموالاة مؤخر عن ذوي الأرحام المولى الأسفل إذا أقر بأخ أو ابن عم ثم مات فميراثه المولى وينقض العقد
He (the author) said: (Then the emancipator) because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Clientage is like lineage in flesh.' And it is the last of the 'asaba because of his saying, peace be upon him, to one who emancipated a slave who died leaving no heir: 'You are his 'asaba.' He said concerning 'asabah from the perspective of lineage: it is of two types: a client whose entitlement is by emancipation, and a client by contract. As for the discussion on the client of emancipation, we say: the scholars have spoken about the cause of inheritance. Some said it is similar to emancipation, and the text bears witness to it: he said, peace be upon him, 'Clientage belongs to the one who emancipates.' Some said it is similar to ownership over the emancipated one, and this is correct. Do you not see that one who inherits from his relative until he is emancipated, his clientage belongs to him, and there is no emancipation here? And in al-Mudmarat: clientage cannot be sold nor given as a gift because it is not property. And in al-Ziyadat: some people permitted its gift, but what we have said is correct. And it belongs to the nearest 'asaba of the emancipator. So if a client of emancipation dies leaving a son and a daughter, then the emancipator dies, his inheritance goes to the son of the emancipator, and nothing to the daughter of the emancipator. Similarly, if a client of emancipation dies leaving a father and a son, then the emancipator dies, his inheritance goes to the son of the emancipator, and nothing to his father, because the son is the nearest 'asaba to him. The conclusion is that clientage itself is not inherited, but it remains with the emancipator as it was. Do you not see that the emancipator is attributed by clientage to the emancipated one, not to his children? So entitlement to inheritance by clientage is for the one who is truly attributed to it, then the nearest 'asaba succeeds him in it just as he succeeds in his wealth. So one looks at the death of the emancipator: if the client of emancipation were alive in this situation, and the one who inherits from him among his 'asaba dies, and he is the nearest person to him, then that person inherits from the emancipator. And in al-Dhakhira: what we have mentioned, that clientage is not inherited, is the apparent narration from our companions. And from Abu Yusuf that it is inherited and divided between the son and daughter, with the male taking the share of two females, and thus it was narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud in one narration, and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and Shurayh al-Qadi took it. And if the emancipator dies leaving only the daughter of the client, she gets nothing in the apparent narration from our companions, and the inheritance goes to the public treasury. And it is narrated from some of our scholars that they used to give fatwa in this issue that the money should be given to her, not by way of inheritance, but because she is closer to the deceased than the public treasury, especially since we have no public treasury in our time; it was only like that in the time of the Companions. And if it is given to the ruler of the time or the judge, they do not spend it in its proper channels. This is how Qadi Abu Bakr and Sadr al-Shari'ah used to give fatwa. And Imam Abd al-Wahid al-Shahid mentioned in his Fara'id that the surplus over the shares of the husband and wife is not placed in the public treasury but is given to them because they are closer to the deceased by lineage, and giving it to them is better than to others. Likewise, the son and daughter of breastfeeding, if the deceased has no others, the money is given to them. And the 'asaba of the emancipator inherit, but the 'asaba of the heirs do not inherit. Example: a woman emancipates a slave and dies leaving a son and a husband, then the client dies: the inheritance goes to the son of the emancipator because he is her 'asaba. If the son then dies and leaves his father, who is the husband of the emancipatory woman, he does not inherit because the father of the son is not an 'asaba of the emancipator. And if a man emancipates a slave, then the second emancipated one emancipates another, then the third client dies leaving only the 'asaba of the first emancipator, they inherit from him. And if a woman buys her father until he is emancipated by her, then the father dies leaving this buyer and another daughter, the inheritance of the client is by thirds: two-thirds between them equally by virtue of prescribed share, and the other third for the buyer by virtue of clientage. Much of this section has been presented in the book of Wala'. As for the discussion on clientage by contract: its interpretation is that a man accepts Islam at the hand of another, and says to the one at whose hand he accepted Islam or to another: 'I contract clientage with you on the condition that if I die, my inheritance is for you.' And in Sharh al-Tahawi: 'If I die and I have no heir from the prescribed shares, nor from 'asaba, nor from uterine relatives, then my inheritance is for you; and if I commit a crime, my 'aql (blood money) is upon you and your 'aqila.' And the other accepts. This is the interpretation of clientage by contract. Then if the lower one commits a crime, his 'aql is upon the 'aqila of the higher client. If the lower one dies, the higher client inherits from him. If the higher dies, the lower does not inherit from him. These rulings are not established by mere Islam without the contract of clientage. When the lower dies, his inheritance goes to the nearest 'asaba to the higher, as in clientage of emancipation. Each of them has the right to rescind the contract of clientage. He does not have the right to assign clientage to another; if he says 'I make my clientage for so-and-so,' it does not become his. The lower one has the right to transfer clientage to another; he may contract clientage with another along with the first. If he contracts with another, the first is rescinded, even if the contract with the other was in the absence of the higher. In al-Dhakhira: clientage by contract differs from clientage of emancipation in several ways: first, in emancipation the higher inherits from the lower but the lower does not inherit from the higher; if they stipulate that in clientage by contract, their stipulation is considered, so if they stipulate that each inherits from the other, it is as they stipulated. Second, clientage by contract is subject to rescission, but clientage of emancipation is not. Third, clientage of emancipation takes precedence over uterine relatives, while clientage by contract is delayed after uterine relatives. When the lower client acknowledges a brother or paternal cousin, then dies, his inheritance goes to the client, and the contract is rescinded.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on wala' (clientage) from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It covers two types: wala' al-'itaqa (clientage of emancipation) and wala' al-muwalah (clientage by contract). For wala' al-'itaqa, the author explains the cause of inheritance: some scholars say it is due to emancipation (based on hadith 'al-wala' li-man a'taqa'), but the preferred view is that it is similar to ownership over the emancipated slave, as seen when a relative is inherited and becomes free without direct emancipation. Wala' itself is not property and cannot be sold or given as a gift, per the relied-upon view. The entitlement to inheritance by wala' goes to the nearest 'asaba of the emancipator, not to females of the 'asaba. The passage then discusses the dispute: in Zahir al-Riwayah, wala' is not inherited; Abu Yusuf held that it is inherited and divided between sons and daughters like property, based on a narration from Ibn Mas'ud. However, the fatwa is on the Zahir al-Riwayah position. When the emancipator dies leaving only a daughter of the client, she takes nothing in the dominant view, but later scholars (like Qadi Abu Bakr) gave fatwa to give the money to her due to the absence of a proper public treasury. The passage also provides examples clarifying that the 'asaba of the emancipator inherit, but not the 'asaba of the client's heirs. For wala' al-muwalah, the definition is given: a contract between two free men where one agrees to be the client of the other for inheritance and blood money liability. The rulings differ from emancipation wala': the lower inherits from the higher if stipulated, it is revocable, and it is subordinate to uterine relatives. The passage ends with a note that acknowledging a closer relative cancels the contract.

Key Terms
مولى الموالاة (mawla al-muwalah): A client by contract, where two free persons agree that one inherits from the other and bears blood money liability.
نقض العقد (naqd al-'aqd): Rescission of the contract; each party can rescind muwalah at will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a difference between wala' al-'itaqa and wala' al-muwalah?
A) In 'itaqa, the lower can inherit from the higher; in muwalah, he cannot
B) Muwalah is revocable; 'itaqa is not
C) 'Itaqa is subordinate to uterine relatives; muwalah takes precedence

Key Takeaway
Wala' al-muwalah is a revocable contract distinct from 'itaqa, with flexible inheritance and blood money terms.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
10 min
The Freed Slave's Daughter and the Bayt al-Mal Dilemma
Explains the Hanafi ruling on inheritance of the freed slave's daughter and the later fatwa due to absence of Bayt al-Mal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the inheritance of a freed slave's daughter and evaluate the later fatwa based on the absence of Bayt al-Mal.

Core Idea
When Bayt al-Mal ceases to function, the estate goes to the closest relative, not the treasury.

Inheritance law has fixed rules, but when the underlying rationale disappears, later scholars may adjust the ruling. This lesson examines a classical Hanafi dispute: what happens when a freed slave dies with only a daughter? The authoritative position gives the estate to the public treasury, but later jurists gave it to her because the treasury no longer exists in practice. This is a key example of how Hanafi fiqh adapts to changing circumstances while remaining rooted in the school's methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah
The student must state that according to the core Hanafi transmissions, the daughter of a freed slave inherits nothing, and the entire estate goes to the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury). This is the default position.
The daughter is not an 'asaba nor a Quranic heir in this scenario; she is only the daughter of the freed slave, not of the deceased.
2
Recognize the rationale (illah) for the Bayt al-Mal inheriting
The Bayt al-Mal inherits because it is the ultimate residuary heir when no other heirs exist. This assumes a properly functioning public treasury that distributes the wealth according to Shari'ah.
The illah is the existence of a just and operational Bayt al-Mal.
3
Understand the change in circumstance
Later Hanafi scholars observed that the Bayt al-Mal in their time was not functioning as it did in the era of the Companions. The money would not be spent properly, effectively depriving the closest relatives.
This is a change in the 'illah: the absence of a proper Bayt al-Mal.
4
Trace the later fatwa
Qadi Abu Bakr and Sadr al-Shari'a gave fatwa that the estate should be given to the daughter (or other close relative) because they are closer to the deceased than the dysfunctional treasury. Imam Abd al-Wahid al-Shahid extended this to include surplus from spouses' shares.
The later fatwa is not a contradiction but an adaptation based on istihsan (juristic preference) or consideration of custom.
5
Evaluate the methodological basis
The student should identify that this is an application of the principle that rulings may change when their rationale (illah) disappears. It also shows the role of later mujtahids in updating fatwa without abandoning the madhhab.
This intra-Hanafi dispute is a classic example of 'durar al-madhhab' – the internal evolution of the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخذ إبراهيم النخعي وشريح القاضي. وإذا مات المعتق ولم يترك إلا بنت المعتق فلا شيء لها في ظاهر الرواية عن أصحابنا ويكون الميراث لبيت المال وحكي عن بعض مشايخنا أنهم كانوا يفتون في هذه المسألة أن يدفع المال إليها لا بطريق الإرث ولكن لأنها أقرب إلى الميت من بيت المال كيف وأنه ليس في زماننا بيت المال، وإنما كان كذلك في زمن الصحابة. وإذا دفع ذلك إلى سلطان الوقت أو القاضي لا يصرفون إلى مصرفه هكذا كان يفتي القاضي أبو بكر وصدر الشريعة. وذكر الإمام عبد الواحد الشهيد في فرائضه أن الفاضل عن سهام الزوج والزوجة لا يوضع في بيت المال بل يدفع إليهما لأنهما أقرب إلى الميت من جهة النسب وكان الدفع إليهما أولى من غيرهما
Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and Shurayh al-Qadi took (this position). And if the freed slave dies and leaves only a daughter of the freed slave, she gets nothing in the apparent narration from our companions, and the inheritance goes to the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal). And it is narrated from some of our scholars that they used to give fatwa in this matter that the money should be given to her, not by way of inheritance, but because she is closer to the deceased than the public treasury, especially since there is no public treasury in our time; it only existed in the time of the Companions. And if it is handed over to the ruler of the time or the judge, they do not spend it in its proper channels; thus used to give fatwa Qadi Abu Bakr and Sadr al-Shari'a. And Imam Abd al-Wahid al-Shahid mentioned in his Fara'id that the surplus from the shares of the husband and wife is not placed in the public treasury but is given to them because they are closer to the deceased by lineage, and giving it to them is better than to others.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the inheritance of a freed slave who leaves only a daughter. According to the Zahir al-Riwayah (the authoritative core transmission), the daughter receives nothing because she is not an 'asaba (residuary heir), and the estate goes to the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury). However, later Hanafi scholars like Qadi Abu Bakr and Sadr al-Shari'a gave fatwa that the money should be given to her, not as inheritance but because she is the closest relative, and the Bayt al-Mal no longer exists in our time as it did in the time of the Companions. Imam Abd al-Wahid al-Shahid extended this reasoning to the surplus from husband's and wife's shares, ruling that it should go to them rather than the treasury. The passage illustrates how later Hanafi jurists adapted rulings when the original rationale (existence of a properly functioning Bayt al-Mal) no longer applied.

Key Terms
المعتق (al-mu'tiq): The person who has freed a slave, i.e., the ex-master who now has wala' (patronage) over the freed slave.
بنت المعتق (bint al-mu'tiq): The daughter of the freed slave (i.e., of the person who was freed).
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The most authoritative transmissions of Hanafi rulings from the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
بيت المال (Bayt al-Mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state, which inherits when there are no heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah of the Hanafi school, what happens to the estate of a freed slave who dies leaving only a daughter?
A) The daughter inherits everything as 'asaba.
B) The daughter receives her Quranic share and the remainder goes to Bayt al-Mal.
C) The daughter receives nothing, and the entire estate goes to Bayt al-Mal.
D) The estate is divided equally between the daughter and Bayt al-Mal.

Key Takeaway
In the absence of a functioning Bayt al-Mal, the Hanafi school gives the estate of a freed slave without other heirs to the closest relative, not the treasury.
