Wala' al-Muwalat: Definition and Rulings
Defines Wala' al-Muwalat and contrasts it with Wala' al-'Itaqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Wala' al-Muwalat, state its legal effects (inheritance, blood money, revocation), and distinguish it from Wala' al-'Itaqa in three key respects.

Core Idea
Wala' al-Muwalat is a revocable contract that can include mutual inheritance, and is subordinate to uterine relatives, unlike the stronger Wala' al-'Itaqa.

Clientship (wala') in Islamic law can arise from freeing a slave or from a contract. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq defines Wala' al-Muwalat—a contractual bond between a new Muslim and a patron—and details its rules, including inheritance and blood money liability. The text also highlights three crucial differences between this type of clientship and Wala' al-'Itaqa, giving a complete picture of their legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the opening lines. Identify the key elements: a man accepts Islam at the hand of another, and they contract wala' with explicit wording about inheritance and blood money.
The definition includes a condition: the client must have no heirs by fard, asaba, or dhawi al-arham.
2
Identify the legal effects
Note that the patron (higher) inherits from the client (lower) but not vice versa unless stipulated. The patron's 'aqila is liable for the client's blood money. The contract can be revoked by either party.
These effects do not arise from mere conversion; a specific contract is required.
3
Examine revocability and transfer
Both parties can cancel the contract. The patron cannot assign wala' to another, but the client can contract a new wala' with someone else, which annuls the first.
The client's new contract is valid even without the first patron's knowledge.
4
Analyze the three differences from Wala' al-'Itaqa
Read the 'In al-Dhakhira' section. The differences are: (1) mutual inheritance possible in Muwalat; (2) Muwalat is revocable; (3) Muwalat is postponed behind uterine relatives, while 'Itaqa takes precedence.
These differences have practical effects in inheritance distribution.
5
Apply the differences to a scenario
Consider a client with a uterine brother. Under Muwalat, the brother inherits before the patron. Under 'Itaqa, the patron inherits first. This illustrates the third difference.
Recall that Muwalat does not create a stronger bond than blood ties via the mother.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام في ولاء الموالاة فنقول : تفسير ولاء الموالاة أن يسلم الرجل على يد رجل فيقول للذي أسلم على يديه أو لغيره واليتك على أني إن مت فميراثي لك. وفي شرح الطحاوي: إن مت ولم يكن لي وارث لا من جهة الفريضة ولا من جهة العصبة ولا من جهة ذوي الأرحام فميراثي لك، وإن جنيت فعقلي عليك وعلى عاقلتك وقبل الآخر فهذا هو تفسير ولاء الموالاة، فإذا جنى الأسفل جناية فعقله على عاقلة المولى الأعلى وإذا مات الأسفل يرث منه المولى الأعلى، وإن مات لا يرث منه المولى الأسفل ولا تثبت هذه الأحكام بمجرد الإسلام بدون عبد الموالاة، وإذا مات الأسفل فميراث الأسفل لأقرب الناس عصبة إلى الأعلى كما في ولاء العتاقة، ولكل واحد منهما أن ينقض عقد الموالاة وليس له أن يجعل الولاء إلى غير فإنه لو قال جعلت ولائي لفلان لا يصير له والأسفل له أن يتحول بالولاء إلى غيره فإن له أن يوالي مع آخر مع الأول، وإن والى مع غيره ينتقض الأول، وإن كان الموالاة مع غيره بغيبة الأعلى. وفي الذخيرة ووالي الموالاة يخالف ولاء العتاقة من وجوه : أحدها أن في العتاقة يرث الأعلى من الأسفل ولا يرث الأسفل من الأعلى، وإن شرطوا ذلك في ولاء الموالاة يعتبر شرطهما حتى لو شرطا يرث كل واحد منهما كما شرطا والثاني أن ولاء الموالاة يحتمل النقض وولاء العتاقة لا يحتمل والثالث أن ولاء العتاقة مقدم على ذوي الأرحام ومولى الموالاة مؤخر عن ذوي الأرحام المولى الأسفل إذا أقر بأخ أو ابن عم ثم مات فميراثه المولى وينقض العقد. الموالاة فقد صح منه عقد الموالاة ولم يصح منه الإقرار بالأخ وابن العم
As for the discussion on Wala' al-Muwalat, we say: The interpretation of Wala' al-Muwalat is that a man accepts Islam at the hand of another man and says to the one at whose hand he accepted Islam or to someone else: 'I contract wala' with you, such that if I die, my inheritance is for you.' And in Sharh al-Tahawi: 'If I die and I have no heir either by fixed share (fard), by residuary (asaba), or by uterine relatives (dhawi al-arham), then my inheritance is for you; and if I commit a crime, my blood money ('aql) is upon you and your 'aqila (tribal group).' And the other accepts. So this is the interpretation of Wala' al-Muwalat. So if the lower (freed slave) commits a crime, his 'aql is upon the 'aqila of the higher (patron). And if the lower dies, the higher inherits from him. But if the higher dies, the lower does not inherit from him. And these rulings are not established merely by embracing Islam without a contract of wala'. And if the lower dies, the inheritance of the lower goes to the nearest relative by 'asaba of the higher, just as in Wala' al-'Itaqa. And each of them has the right to cancel the contract of wala'. And he must not assign the wala' to someone else: if he says, 'I have placed my wala' to so-and-so,' it does not become effective. And the lower has the right to transfer his wala' to another: he may contract wala' with another while the first still exists, and if he contracts with another, the first is annulled. And if he contracts with another in the absence of the higher, (it is also annulled). In al-Dhakhira: Wala' al-Muwalat differs from Wala' al-'Itaqa in several respects: one is that in 'Itaqa the higher inherits from the lower but the lower does not inherit from the higher, whereas in Muwalat if they stipulate that each will inherit from the other, their condition is valid, so if they stipulate, each inherits as stipulated. The second is that Wala' al-Muwalat is subject to annulment, whereas Wala' al-'Itaqa is not. The third is that Wala' al-'Itaqa takes precedence over uterine relatives (dhawi al-arham), while Wala' al-Muwalat is postponed behind them. If the lower (i.e., the client) acknowledges a brother or paternal cousin, then dies, his inheritance goes to the patron, and the contract is annulled. (Alternatively:) The wala' (contract) is valid, but the acknowledgment of a brother or paternal cousin is not.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage defines Wala' al-Muwalat (clientship by contract) and outlines its rules. It begins with the definition: a non-Muslim embraces Islam at the hand of a Muslim and contracts wala' such that the Muslim patron inherits from him and pays his blood money. The text clarifies that this relationship is established only through a specific contract, not mere Islam. Key rulings include: the patron (higher) inherits from the client (lower) but not vice versa; the inheritance of the client goes to the nearest 'asaba of the patron. Both parties can revoke the contract, but the patron cannot transfer wala' to another; the client can make a new wala' which annuls the old. The text then contrasts Wala' al-Muwalat with Wala' al-'Itaqa in three points: (1) Mutuality - in Muwalat, both can inherit by stipulation; (2) Revocability - Muwalat can be annulled, but 'Itaqa cannot; (3) Priority over uterine relatives - 'Itaqa takes precedence, while Muwalat is postponed. Finally, if the client acknowledges a brother or cousin, the contract is valid but the acknowledgment is not.

Key Terms
ولاء الموالاة (Wala' al-Muwalat): A contractual clientship established between a new Muslim and a patron, providing for inheritance and blood money liability.
ولاء العتاقة (Wala' al-'Itaqa): Clientship that results from freeing a slave, giving the ex-master inheritance rights.
عقد الموالاة ('Aqd al-Muwalat): The contract of Wala' al-Muwalat between the patron and the client.
عاقلة ('Aqila): The tribal group or clan responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of a member.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is one of the differences between Wala' al-'Itaqa and Wala' al-Muwalat according to the passage?
A) In Wala' al-'Itaqa, both parties can inherit from each other if stipulated.
B) In Wala' al-Muwalat, the lower (client) can inherit from the higher (patron) without stipulation.
C) Wala' al-'Itaqa is subject to annulment, while Wala' al-Muwalat is not.
D) Wala' al-Muwalat is postponed behind uterine relatives, while Wala' al-'Itaqa takes precedence over them.

Key Takeaway
Wala' al-Muwalat is a contractual clientship distinct from Wala' al-'Itaqa in being revocable, allowing mutual inheritance by stipulation, and yielding priority to uterine relatives.

Rules of 'Asaba (Residuary Heirs) and Hijab (Exclusion) in Inheritance
Explains order of 'asaba, women as 'asaba, and hijab rules with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of 'asaba heirs, identify which women become 'asaba, and apply the rules of hijab to determine inheritance shares.

Core Idea
Asaba heirs are ordered by closeness; women become asaba only with brothers; hijab can exclude partial or total.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the rules of residuary heirs ('asaba) and exclusion (hijab). It covers the order of 'asaba for the patron, which women become 'asaba with their brothers, and the principle that one who is connected through another is excluded by that other, with important exceptions. Understanding these rules is essential for accurately distributing the estate when no male descendants exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'asaba order
List the priority: patron's descendants (even if lower), then ancestors, then paternal uncles, then from grandfather's side. Closer degree takes precedence.
The order applies when the deceased is a freed slave with no blood relatives.
2
Recognize women who become 'asaba
Only daughters, sons' daughters, full sisters, and consanguine sisters become 'asaba with their brothers. Others do not. Sons' daughters also become 'asaba with paternal cousins.
Women become 'asaba by others (with brothers) but not by themselves.
3
Apply the hijab rule
A person connected through another is excluded by that other (e.g., grandson excluded by son). Exception: maternal sibling is not excluded by mother. The excluded can still exclude others (e.g., brothers excluded by father still exclude mother from 1/3 to 1/6).
Hijab can be total (hirman) or partial (nuqsan).
4
Compare the Ibn Abbas case
In a case of father, mother, and three maternal sisters, Hanafi gives mother 1/6, father residue; Ibn Abbas gave sisters 1/6 each. Understand why Hanafi rejects this based on Qur'anic verses.
The ikhtilaf is over the interpretation of the hijab verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ثم عصبته على الترتيب) أي عصبة المولى ومعناه إذا لم يكن للمعتق من النسب على الترتيب الذي ذكرنا فعصبته مولاه الذي أعتقه فإن لم يكن مولاه فعصبته عصبة المعتق وهو المولى على الترتيب الذي ذكرناه بأن يكون جزء من المولى أولى وإن سفل، ثم أصوله ثم جزء أبيه ثم جزء جده يقدمون لقوة القرابة عند الاستواء أو بعلو الدرجة عند التفاوت . قال رحمه الله : (واللاتي فرضهن النصف والثلثان يصرن عصبة بإخوتهن لا غير) وهن أربع من النساء : البنات وبنات الابن والأخوات لأب وأم والأخوات لأب وغيرهن لا يصرن عصبة بأخوتهن وقد بيناه في بيان ميراثهن. وقوله بأخوتهن هذا في البنات والأخوات ظاهر لأن عصبتهن تقتصر عليهم، وأما بنات الابن فإنهن يصرن عصبة بأبناء أعمامهن أيضاً وإن سفل كما ذكرنا في مسائل النسب فيكون معناه في حقهن بأخوتهن أو من له حكم إخوتهن، والمصنف ذكر حكم العصبات هنا واستوفاه إلا العصبة مع غيره وهن الأخوات مع البنات، وإنما ترك ذكرهن لأنه ذكرهن فيما يقدم وقد شرحناه هناك فلا نعيده. وإنما جعلهن مع البنات عصبة بغيرهن ومع إخوتهن عصبة لأن ذلك الغير وهو البنات شرط ليصير ورثتهن عصبة ولم يجعلهن لأن نفسهن ليس بعصبة فكيف يجعلن غيرهن عصبة بهن بخلاف ما إذا كن مع إخوتهن لأن الإخوة بأنفسهن عصبة فيصرن به عصبة تبعاً قال رحمه الله : (ومن يدلي بغيره به) أي بذلك الغير سوى ولد الأم فإنه يدلي بالأم ولا تحجبه بل هي . تحجب بالاثنين منهم من الثلث إلى السدس على ما بينا . وإنما لا تحجبه الأم لأنها لا تستحق جميع التركة ولا يرث هو إرثها لأنها ترث بالولادة وهو بالإخوة فلا يتصور الحجب فيه بخلاف الجد حيث يحجب الإخوة والأخوات كلهم لأنه يستحق جميع التركة، وبخلاف الجدة حيث تحجب بالأم لأنها ترث ميراث الأم والأم به أولى منها لأنها أقرب وبخلاف الأب حيث يحجب الجد والجدة والأخوة والأخوات كلهن لأنه يستحق جميع التركة، وكذلك الابن يحجب ابنه لما ذكرنا ويكون الحاجب أقرب كالأعمام يحجبون بالإخوة وبأولادهم وكأولاد الأعمام والأخوة يحجبون بأعلى درجة قال رحمه الله: (والمحجوب يحجب كالإخوين أو الأختين يحجبان الأم من الثلث إلى منهم . السدس مع الأب وهما لا يرثان معه لأن إرث الأخوة مشروط بالكلالة وإرث الأم الثلث مشروط بعدم الاثنين من الإخوة وروي عن ابن عباس في أب وأم وثلاث أخوات للأم السدس وللأخوات السدس وما بقي للأب فجعل للإخوة ما نقص من نصيب الأم، وبيانه آية الكلالة تمنع من ذلك وآية حجب الأم بهم أيضاً لا توجب لهم ما نقص من نصيبهما فيحجبونها من غير أن يحصل لهم شيء.
He (the author) said: 'Then his 'asaba are in order' meaning the 'asaba of the patron. Its meaning is that if the freed slave has no relatives by lineage in the order we have mentioned, then his 'asaba is his patron who freed him. If there is no patron, then his 'asaba is the 'asaba of the patron, i.e., the patron's own 'asaba in the order we have mentioned, such that a part (i.e., descendants) of the patron is given precedence even if lower in grade, then his ancestors, then his father's brothers, then his grandfather's brothers. They are given precedence due to strength of relationship when equal, or due to higher degree when differing. He said: 'And those women whose fixed shares are half and two-thirds become 'asaba only with their brothers' - these are four types of women: daughters, sons' daughters, full sisters, and consanguine sisters. Others do not become 'asaba with their brothers, and we have explained this in the section on their inheritance. His saying 'with their brothers' is obvious in the case of daughters and sisters, because their 'asaba are limited to them. As for sons' daughters, they also become 'asaba with their paternal cousins even if lower, as we mentioned in the section on lineage matters. So the meaning in their case is 'with their brothers or those who have the same status as their brothers.' The author has mentioned here the rulings on 'asaba completely, except for the 'asaba with others, i.e., sisters with daughters. He omitted that because he mentioned it earlier, and we have explained it there, so we do not repeat. He made them (sisters) with daughters 'asaba by others, and with brothers 'asaba by themselves, because that other (daughters) is a condition for them to become residuary heirs. He did not make them (the sisters) as those who can make others 'asaba because they themselves are not 'asaba; so how could they make others 'asaba through them? Contrary to when they are with brothers, because brothers are 'asaba by themselves, so they become 'asaba through them as dependents. He said: 'And one who is connected through another is excluded by that other' meaning by that other, except for the case of a maternal sibling, who is connected through the mother but the mother does not exclude him; rather, she is excluded by two from among them from one-third to one-sixth, as we explained. The mother does not exclude him because she does not inherit the entire estate, and he does not inherit her inheritance because she inherits by birth and he by brotherhood, so hijab is not conceivable. This is unlike the grandfather, who excludes all siblings because he inherits the entire estate; unlike the grandmother, who is excluded by the mother because she inherits the mother's inheritance and the mother is more entitled than her due to being closer; and unlike the father, who excludes the grandfather, grandmother and all siblings because he inherits the entire estate. Similarly, the son excludes his son (grandson) for the reason we mentioned. And the one excluding is the closer relative, like uncles being excluded by brothers and their children, and like children of uncles and brothers being excluded by a higher degree. He said: 'And one who is excluded can exclude others' like two brothers or two sisters exclude the mother from one-third to one-sixth in the presence of the father, and they do not inherit with him because inheritance of siblings is conditional upon kalala (having no children or father), and the mother's inheritance of one-third is conditional upon the absence of two siblings. It is narrated from Ibn Abbas regarding a father, mother, and three maternal sisters: the mother gets one-sixth, the sisters get one-sixth, and the rest goes to the father. He assigned to the siblings what had been reduced from the mother's share. The explanation is that the verse of kalala prevents that, and the verse that excludes the mother by them does not necessitate giving them what reduced from her share; so they exclude her without anything coming to them.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Hidayah's text regarding 'asaba (residuary heirs) and hijab (exclusion). It begins by explaining the order of 'asaba for the freed slave's estate: first the patron's descendants (even if lower), then ancestors, then uncles from the father's side, then from the grandfather's side. Then it discusses which women become 'asaba: daughters, sons' daughters, full sisters, and consanguine sisters become 'asaba only with their brothers (or, for sons' daughters, also with paternal cousins). It clarifies that sisters with daughters become 'asaba by others (the daughters as co-heirs), not by themselves. The commentary then discusses the rule of hijab: a person who is connected through another is excluded by that other (e.g., a nephew excluded by a brother). However, the mother does not exclude her own child (maternal sibling) because she does not inherit the entire estate. The grandfather excludes siblings because he inherits all; the father excludes grandfather and siblings; the son excludes his own son. The final example illustrates that two brothers or two sisters (who themselves are excluded by the father) still exclude the mother from one-third to one-sixth. It also cites a narration from Ibn Abbas that differs from the Hanafi position: in a case with father, mother, and three maternal sisters, he gave the mother one-sixth, the sisters one-sixth, and the father the rest, implying the reduction of the mother's share goes to the siblings, but the Hanafi school rejects this because the verses on kalala and hijab support the standard ruling.

Key Terms
العصبة (al-'Asaba): Residuary heirs who take the remaining estate after fixed shares, typically male paternal relatives.
الحجب (al-Hajb): Exclusion from inheritance, either total (hirman) or partial (nuqsan) caused by a closer heir.
الكلالة (al-Kalala): A deceased who has no descendants or ascendants; it is the condition for siblings to inherit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a deceased leaving only a father, mother, and three maternal sisters, what do the sisters inherit according to the Hanafi school?
A) They each inherit one-sixth of the estate.
B) They inherit one-third of the estate collectively.
C) They inherit nothing because they are excluded by the father.
D) They inherit only if the mother gets less than one-sixth.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi 'asaba and hijab rules ensure that the closest male agnatic relative inherits the residue, and siblings are excluded by the father or grandfather, even if they exclude the mother.

Order of 'Asabah for the Mawla
Commentary expanding the order of 'asabah for the mawla

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi order of 'asabah for the freed slave's inheritance and apply the hierarchy when identifying heirs.

Core Idea
The freed slave's inheritance goes first to his own blood 'asabah, then to his master, then to the master's 'asabah.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the order of residuary heirs ('asabah) when a freed slave dies without any blood relatives. The ruling establishes a clear priority: first the slave's own 'asabah, then the master himself, then the master's 'asabah in a defined sequence. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correctly distributing the estate in these cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary source
Recognize that the passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author expands on the phrase 'then his 'asabah are according to the order'.
2
Understand the three-tier hierarchy
The freed slave's inheritance goes: 1) to the slave's own blood 'asabah (if any); 2) if none, to the master (mawla al-'itaq); 3) if the master is dead, to the master's 'asabah in the order: descendants, then ancestors, then descendants of ancestors.
This mirrors the regular 'asabah order but with the master acting as a substitute.
3
Note the principle of precedence
Within the master's 'asabah, priority is given by closeness of lineage: strength of kinship when degrees are equal, higher degree when degrees differ.
For example, the master's son takes priority over the master's father.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ثم عصبته على الترتيب) أي عصبة المولى ومعناه إذا لم يكن للمعتق من النسب على الترتيب الذي ذكرنا فعصبته مولاه الذي أعتقه فإن لم يكن مولاه فعصبته عصبة المعتق وهو المولى على الترتيب الذي ذكرناه بأن يكون جزء من المولى أولى وإن سفل، ثم أصوله ثم جزء أبيه ثم جزء جده يقدمون لقوة القرابة عند الاستواء أو بعلو الدرجة عند التفاوت .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Then his 'asabah are according to the order' meaning the 'asabah of the mawla (the former master). Its meaning: if the one who freed (the slave) has no relative from the lineage in the order we mentioned, then his 'asabah is his mawla who freed him. If he has no mawla, then his 'asabah is the 'asabah of the manumitter (the mawla himself) in the order we mentioned: that the descendant of the mawla is first, even if lower, then his roots, then the descendant of his father, then the descendant of his grandfather, they are given precedence due to strength of kinship when equal, or due to higher degree when there is disparity.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn followed by its commentary. The passage explains the order of 'asabah for a freed slave's inheritance. When a slave is freed (mu'taq) and dies without any blood relatives ('asabah nasab), his inheritance goes to his former master (mawla al-'itaq) as 'asabah. If the master is deceased, then the inheritance passes to the master's own 'asabah (the manumitter's 'asabah) in the order: first the descendants of the master (even if lower in lineage), then the master's ancestors, then the master's father's descendants, then the master's grandfather's descendants. Precedence is given by strength of kinship (when degrees are equal) or by higher degree (when degrees differ). This follows the general Hanafi principle of 'asabah ordering: first the descendant, then origin, then descendant of origin, with priority given to the closer relative.

Key Terms
عصبة (asabah): Residuary heirs who inherit the remainder after fixed shares are given; in this context, the heirs of the mawla or the manumitter's own 'asabah
المولى (al-mawla): The former master who freed a slave; also refers to the master's estate

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi order, if the freed slave has no blood 'asabah and his former master is deceased, who inherits next?
A) The master's son
B) The master's father
C) The master's daughter
D) The state treasury

Key Takeaway
The inheritance of a freed slave without blood 'asabah follows a specific order: first the master himself, then the master's 'asabah (descendants, then ancestors, then descendants of ancestors).

Women Becoming 'Asabah with Their Brothers
Commentary explaining the limited categories of female 'asabah with brothers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of female heirs who become 'asabah with their brothers and distinguish between 'asabah bi-ghayrihi and 'asabah ma'a ghayrihi.

Core Idea
Only four female heirs become 'asabah with brothers: daughters, son's daughters, full sisters, and paternal half-sisters.

This passage from the sharh elaborates on the matn's statement about women whose prescribed share is half or two-thirds becoming 'asabah with their brothers. It lists the four categories and clarifies the legal reasoning behind their transformation from prescribed share holders to residuary heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the ruling from the matn: women with prescribed shares of half or two-thirds become 'asabah with their brothers.
This is not automatic for all such women; only four categories qualify.
2
List the Four Categories
From the text, extract the four types: daughters, son's daughters, full sisters, and paternal half-sisters.
Maternal half-sisters are excluded.
3
Understand the Exception
Other female heirs (e.g., mother, wife) do not become 'asabah with brothers, even if they have fixed shares.
The text says 'wa ghayruhunna la yasirna'.
4
Distinguish 'Asabah Types
Differentiate between 'asabah bi-ghayrihi (sisters become 'asabah through daughters) and 'asabah ma'a ghayrihi (sisters become 'asabah with brothers).
Brothers are true 'asabah; sisters follow them. Daughters are not 'asabah but enable sisters.
5
Apply the Rationale
Explain why brothers make sisters 'asabah (brothers themselves are 'asabah) while daughters do not (daughters are not 'asabah).
The text contrasts the two scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (واللاتي فرضهن النصف والثلثان يصرن عصبة بإخوتهن لا غير) وهن أربع من النساء : البنات وبنات الابن والأخوات لأب وأم والأخوات لأب وغيرهن لا يصرن عصبة بأخوتهن وقد بيناه في بيان ميراثهن. وقوله بأخوتهن هذا في البنات والأخوات ظاهر لأن عصبتهن تقتصر عليهم، وأما بنات الابن فإنهن يصرن عصبة بأبناء أعمامهن أيضاً وإن سفل كما ذكرنا في مسائل النسب فيكون معناه في حقهن بأخوتهن أو من له حكم إخوتهن، والمصنف ذكر حكم العصبات هنا واستوفاه إلا العصبة مع غيره وهن الأخوات مع البنات، وإنما ترك ذكرهن لأنه ذكرهن فيما يقدم وقد شرحناه هناك فلا نعيده. وإنما جعلهن مع البنات عصبة بغيرهن ومع إخوتهن عصبة لأن ذلك الغير وهو البنات شرط ليصير ورثتهن عصبة ولم يجعلهن لأن نفسهن ليس بعصبة فكيف يجعلن غيرهن عصبة بهن بخلاف ما إذا كن مع إخوتهن لأن الإخوة بأنفسهن عصبة فيصرن به عصبة تبعاً
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: ('And those women whose prescribed share is half or two-thirds become 'asabah with their brothers only') They are four types of women: daughters, son's daughters, full sisters, and half-sisters from the father's side. Other than these do not become 'asabah with their brothers, and we have explained this in the explanation of their inheritance. And his saying 'with their brothers' is apparent for daughters and sisters, because their 'asabah are limited to them. As for son's daughters, they also become 'asabah with the sons of their paternal uncles even if lower, as we mentioned in the chapters on lineage. So its meaning regarding them is: with their brothers or those who have the legal status of their brothers. The author mentioned the ruling of 'asabah here and covered it comprehensively, except for 'asabah with others', i.e., sisters with daughters. He left mentioning them because he mentioned them in what precedes and we explained it there, so we do not repeat. And he made them (the sisters) with daughters 'asabah through others (i.e., through daughters) and with their brothers 'asabah because that other (the daughter) is a condition for them to become 'asabah through them. But he did not treat them (the daughters) as making others 'asabah because they themselves are not 'asabah, so how can they make others 'asabah through them? Unlike when they are with their brothers, because the brothers themselves are 'asabah, so they become 'asabah through them as a follower.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This is a ruling with commentary from the sharh. The matn states that women whose fixed share is half or two-thirds become 'asabah with their brothers, but only for four categories: daughters, son's daughters, full sisters, and paternal half-sisters. Other female heirs do not become 'asabah even with brothers. The author explains that for daughters and sisters, their becoming 'asabah is limited to their brothers. For son's daughters, they can also become 'asabah through more distant male relatives like paternal uncle's sons. However, the author has already covered the case of sisters with daughters (the 'asabah ma'a ghayri) elsewhere. The reasoning: when sisters are with daughters, they become 'asabah through the daughters, but the daughters themselves are not 'asabah (they have fixed shares), so sisters become 'asabah because of the presence of daughters (a condition). In contrast, when sisters are with brothers, the brothers are true 'asabah, so sisters follow them.

Key Terms
عصبة بغيره (asabah bi-ghayrihi): A female heir becoming residuary due to the presence of another female heir (e.g., sisters with daughters)
عصبة مع غيره (asabah ma'a ghayrihi): A female heir becoming residuary alongside a specific female relative (e.g., sisters inheriting the residue with daughters)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Full sisters and paternal half-sisters
B) Daughters and maternal half-sisters
C) Wives and mothers
D) Full sisters only

Key Takeaway
Only four types of female heirs – daughters, son's daughters, full sisters, and paternal half-sisters – become 'asabah with their brothers.

The Rule of Exclusion for One Connected Through Another
Commentary on the exception to the rule of exclusion for one who connects through another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a relative who inherits through another is excluded by that other, identify the exception for the child of the mother, and explain the reasoning.

Core Idea
One who connects through a closer relative is excluded by him—except the child of the mother.

In Hanafi inheritance law, the principle of exclusion (hajb) is intricate. The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key rule: if an heir derives their right of inheritance through an intermediary relative (e.g., a grandson through a son), that intermediary blocks them. However, an important exception exists: the maternal half-sibling (child of the mother) connects through the mother, yet the mother does not exclude them. This lesson unpacks the rule, its exceptions, and comparisons with other heirs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule's formulation
The matn says 'whoever connects through another is excluded by him.' Identify what 'connects through another' means—an heir whose relationship to the deceased passes through a living closer relative.
For example, a grandson connects through his father (the son of the deceased).
2
Recognise the exception
The text exempts the child of the mother (maternal half-sibling). Note the reason: the mother does not take the entire estate, and the child's share is independent.
Contrast with grandfather who excludes siblings because he takes the whole estate.
3
Compare with other cases
The text lists grandfather (excludes all siblings), grandmother (excluded by mother), father (excludes all siblings and ancestors), and son (excludes grandson). Identify the pattern: the excluding relative must be entitled to the entire estate or be the closer connection.
Also note: uncles excluded by brothers, and children of uncles excluded by those of higher degree.
4
Analyse the reasoning for the exception
Why does the mother not exclude her child? Because she does not inherit the whole estate, nor does the child inherit her share; the child inherits as a sibling by fixed share.
Exclusion requires that the excluded's right is derived from the excluding relative or that the excluding relative takes everything.
5
Apply the rule to other relations
Generalise: any relative who is a link excludes those who come through him, unless the link does not take the entire estate and the connection is not by inheritance.
Example: a great-grandson through a son is excluded by the son if the son is alive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ومن يدلي بغيره به) أي بذلك الغير سوى ولد الأم فإنه يدلي بالأم ولا تحجبه بل هي تحجب بالاثنين منهم من الثلث إلى السدس على ما بينا . وإنما لا تحجبه الأم لأنها لا تستحق جميع التركة ولا يرث هو إرثها لأنها ترث بالولادة وهو بالإخوة فلا يتصور الحجب فيه بخلاف الجد حيث يحجب الإخوة والأخوات كلهم لأنه يستحق جميع التركة، وبخلاف الجدة حيث تحجب بالأم لأنها ترث ميراث الأم والأم به أولى منها لأنها أقرب وبخلاف الأب حيث يحجب الجد والجدة والأخوة والأخوات كلهن لأنه يستحق جميع التركة، وكذلك الابن يحجب ابنه لما ذكرنا ويكون الحاجب أقرب كالأعمام يحجبون بالإخوة وبأولادهم وكأولاد الأعمام والأخوة يحجبون بأعلى درجة
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: ('And whoever connects through another is excluded by him') i.e., by that other, except the child of the mother. For he connects through his mother but she does not exclude him; rather, she is excluded by two of them (i.e., two brothers/sisters) from one-third to one-sixth as we have explained. And the mother does not exclude him because she does not deserve the entire estate, nor does he inherit her inheritance because she inherits by birth and he inherits through brotherhood, so exclusion (hajb) is inconceivable. This is unlike the grandfather, who excludes all brothers and sisters because he deserves the entire estate. And unlike the grandmother, who is excluded by the mother because she inherits the mother's inheritance and the mother has priority over her due to being closer. And unlike the father, who excludes the grandfather, grandmother, and all brothers and sisters because he deserves the entire estate. Similarly, the son excludes his son (grandson) for what we mentioned. The excluding relative is the closest, e.g., uncles are excluded by brothers and their children, and children of uncles and brothers are excluded by those of higher degree.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage explains the inheritance rule in Hanafi fiqh: a person who derives their right of inheritance through another relative (i.e., is 'connected through' that relative) is normally excluded (mahjub) by that connecting relative. However, there is an exception: the child of the mother (i.e., maternal half-sibling or uterine sibling) connects through the mother, but the mother does not exclude them. Instead, the mother is herself excluded by two or more of these siblings (reducing her share from one-third to one-sixth). The reason is that the mother does not take the entire estate, and the child of the mother does not inherit her share but rather takes a fixed share by virtue of being a sibling. The text then contrasts this with other cases: the grandfather excludes all brothers and sisters (and their descendants) because he takes the entire estate (as the sole male ascendant). The grandmother is excluded by the mother (since mother is closer). The father excludes grandfather, grandmother, and all siblings because he takes the whole estate. A son excludes his own son (grandson) because the son is closer. In general, the closest relative excludes the more distant one, e.g., brothers and their children exclude uncles, and higher-degree relatives exclude lower-degree ones.

Key Terms
يدلي بغيره (yudlī bi-ghayrihi): To be legally connected to the deceased through an intermediary relative
ولد الأم (walad al-umm): A child of the mother, i.e., a maternal half-sibling (uterine sibling) who inherits as a fixed-share heir (fard) of one-sixth for one, or one-third collectively

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which relative does NOT exclude the person who connects through them?
A) The father excludes the son
B) The mother excludes the child of the mother
C) The grandfather excludes the brothers
D) The son excludes the grandson

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance, a relative who connects through another is excluded by that other, except the child of the mother because the mother does not take the entire estate.

The Excluded Heir Who Still Excludes
Commentary on the principle that a mahjub can still reduce another's share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that a mahjub can reduce another heir's share, and derive the shares in a case involving father, mother, and siblings.

Core Idea
An heir excluded from inheritance can still reduce the share of another heir — exclusion does not neutralise the power to reduce.

This lesson explains a key principle of Hanafi faraid: a person who is himself excluded from inheritance (mahjub) can still cause a reduction in the share of another heir. The classic example is that of siblings who are excluded by the father but still reduce the mother's share from one-third to one-sixth. We will also examine an alternative view attributed to Ibn Abbas and why the Hanafi school rejects it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
State the principle in Arabic and English: 'المحجوب يحجب' — the excluded one excludes. Recognize that exclusion (hajb) can be total (hirman) or partial (nuqsan).
Here, mahjub acts as hajib nuqsan, not hajib hirman.
2
Analyse the Example
Consider a deceased person survived by father, mother, and two brothers (or sisters). Determine that the siblings are excluded by the father (since siblings only inherit in kalalah). The mother's share is reduced from one-third to one-sixth due to the presence of two siblings.
The siblings are mahjub hirman (excluded from inheritance) but still hajib nuqsan for the mother.
3
Understand the Proof
The Quranic verses on inheritance (4:11-12) are interpreted: the verse on kalalah (4:12) conditions sibling inheritance on absence of father, and the verse on hujb of mother (4:11) reduces her share when siblings are present, without giving them a share.
The dalil is the Quran, and the Hanafi interpretation is that the sibling presence triggers reduction but not eligibility.
4
Examine the Ikhtilaf
Contrast with the view of Ibn Abbas who, in a case of father, mother, and three sisters, gave the sisters the amount reduced from the mother's share. The Hanafi school refutes this because the verses do not grant siblings a share in the presence of the father.
The Hanafi position follows the majority of Companions and the apparent meaning of the verses.
5
Derive the Ruling
Conclude that when the father is present, siblings are mahjub from inheritance but still reduce the mother's share. Apply to similar scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (والمحجوب يحجب كالإخوين أو الأختين يحجبان الأم من الثلث إلى السدس مع الأب وهما لا يرثان معه لأن إرث الأخوة مشروط بالكلالة وإرث الأم الثلث مشروط بعدم الاثنين من الإخوة وروي عن ابن عباس في أب وأم وثلاث أخوات للأم السدس وللأخوات السدس وما بقي للأب فجعل للإخوة ما نقص من نصيب الأم، وبيانه آية الكلالة تمنع من ذلك وآية حجب الأم بهم أيضاً لا توجب لهم ما نقص من نصيبهما فيحجبونها من غير أن يحصل لهم شيء.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: ('And the mahjub (excluded person) excludes, like two brothers or two sisters exclude the mother from one-third to one-sixth in the presence of the father, while they do not inherit with him because the inheritance of siblings is conditional upon kalalah (absence of father and child) and the mother's inheritance of one-third is conditional upon the absence of two siblings.' And it is narrated from Ibn 'Abbas regarding a father, mother, and three sisters: the mother gets one-sixth, the sisters get one-sixth, and the remainder goes to the father. So he attributed to the siblings what was reduced from the mother's share. But its explanation: the verse of kalalah prevents that, and the verse of exclusion of the mother through them does not obligate giving them what was reduced from her share. So they exclude her without obtaining anything for themselves.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage explains a key principle: a person who is himself excluded from inheritance (mahjub) can still act as a hajib (excluder) for others. The example given: when the father is alive, two brothers or two sisters of the deceased (full or consanguine siblings) do not inherit at all because they are excluded by the father (since siblings only inherit when there is no father, i.e., in kalalah). However, these same siblings still cause a reduction in the mother's share from one-third to one-sixth. This means that when the father is present, the siblings are mahjub (excluded from inheritance), but they still reduce the mother's share (they are hajib nuqsan). The text then mentions a narration from Ibn 'Abbas in a case involving father, mother, and three sisters: the mother gets one-sixth ( reduced from one-third due to the presence of two or more siblings), the sisters get one-sixth (their fixed share), and the father takes the residue. But it clarifies that the verse of kalalah (Surah an-Nisa, verse 12) prevents siblings from inheriting with the father, and the verse that excludes the mother (Surah an-Nisa, verse 11) does not give them anything. Thus, they exclude the mother without receiving any share themselves.

Key Terms
المحجوب (al-mahjub): A person who is excluded from inheriting due to the presence of a closer heir
يرث بالكلالة (yarithu bi al-kalalah): To inherit as a sibling in the absence of father and child
حجب النقصان (hajb al-nuqsan): Partial exclusion: the heir's share is reduced but not eliminated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the presence of the father, two brothers do not inherit. What effect do they have on the mother's share?
A) They increase her share to half
B) They reduce her share from 1/3 to 1/6
C) They have no effect
D) They exclude her entirely

Key Takeaway
A mahjub can still be a hajib nuqsan, reducing another's share without inheriting themselves.

The Principle: A Deprived Heir (Mahroom) Does Not Exclude Others
Ruling with proof, intra-school dispute, and analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a mahroom does not exclude other heirs, explain the proof by analogy to the deceased, and evaluate the opposing view of Ibn Mas'ud.

Core Idea
A mahroom is legally like a dead person; he neither inherits nor blocks others.

In inheritance law, some individuals are completely ineligible to inherit due to a defect in themselves, such as slavery. These are called mahroom (deprived). A key question is whether such a person can still affect the shares of other heirs by causing a reduction or exclusion. The Hanafi school holds that a mahroom is like the deceased in terms of inheritance: he has no effect on the shares of others. This lesson examines the proof for this position and the rebuttal of a different view attributed to Ibn Mas'ud.

Guided Path
1
Define mahroom and mahjub
Understand that a mahroom is ineligible due to a personal defect (e.g., slavery), whereas a mahjub is eligible but excluded by a closer heir. The difference is crucial for the rule.
The mahroom is treated as non-existent for inheritance; the mahjub is still considered eligible but superseded.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi school says the mahroom does not exclude anyone, whether by total prevention (hajb hirman) or reduction (hajb nuqsan).
This applies to all mahroom, regardless of the cause.
3
Present the opposing view of Ibn Mas'ud
Ibn Mas'ud held that the mahroom can cause hajb nuqsan (reduction) because Quranic texts mention children and siblings absolutely without distinguishing inheriting from deprived ones.
For example, a child who is a slave should still reduce the spouse's share from 1/4 to 1/8.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is an analogy (qiyas): the mahroom is like the deceased because both lack ownership eligibility. Since the deceased does not affect shares, the mahroom also does not.
This analogy is supported by the principle that the mahroom's defect strips him of all inheritance capacity.
5
Respond to Ibn Mas'ud's proof
The Quranic texts about reduction apply only to those heirs who are first mentioned as inheriting, i.e., those eligible. The mahroom is not among those first mentioned, so the absolute texts are restricted.
The mahroom is treated as non-existent, so the texts do not apply to him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (لا) المحروم بالرق والقتل مباشرة واختلاف الدين أو الدار) أي لا يحجب المحروم بهذه الأشياء أحداً وعند ابن مسعود يحجب حجب النقصان كنقص نصيب الزوجين والأم بالولد المحروم بما ذكرنا لأن الله تعالى ذكر الولد مطلقاً ونقص به نصيبهم من غير فصل بين أن يكون وارثاً أو محروماً، وكذا نقص نصيب الأم بالإخوة مطلقاً من غير فصل فيترك على إطلاقه ولا يحجب حجب الحرمان لأنه لو حجب هذا الحجب وهو لا يرث لأدى إلى دفعه إلى مع وجود الوارث أو إلى تضييقه لأن بيت المال أيضاً لا يرث مع الابن أو الإخوة . وجه قول الجمهور أن المحروم في حق الإرث كالميت لأنه حرم لمعنى في نفسه كالميت، ثم إن الميت لا يحجب فكذا المحروم فصار كحجب الحرمان والنصوص التي توجب نقصان إرثهم لا نسلم أنها مطلقة لأن الله تعالى ذكر الأولاد أولاً وأثبت لهم ميراثاً ثم ذكر بعد ذلك حجب فينصرف إلى المذكورين أولاً وهم المتأهلون للإرث ، وهذا لأن المحروم اتصلت به صفة تسلب أهلية الإرث فألحقه بالمعدوم ولا كذلك المحجوب فإنه أهل في نفسه إلا أن حاجبه عليه على إرثه لزيادة قربه فلا يبطل عمله في حق غيره. وإنما ذكر سبب الحرمان بقوله «لا المحروم بالرق الخ ليبين الأسباب المانعة من الإرث فإن الرق يمنع الإرث لأن الرقيق لا يملك شيئاً قال الله تعالى ضرب الله مثلاً عبداً مملوكاً لا يقدر على شيء [النحل : ٧٥] وقال صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يملك العبد إلا الطلاق ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن يكون قناً وهو الذي لم ينعقد له سبب الحرية وبين أن ينعقد له سبب الحرية كالمدبر والمكاتب وأم الولد ومعتق البعض عند أبي حنيفة لأن المعنى يشمل الكل وهو عدم تصور الملك لهم والمكاتب لا يملك الرقبة وهو عبد ما بقي عليه درهم على ما جاء في الخبر لا يكون أهلاً للإرث. والقتل الذي يمنع الإرث هو الذي يتعلق به وجوب القصاص أو الكفارة وما لا يتعلق به واحد منهما كالقتل بسبب أو قصاص لا يوجب الحرمان لأن وجه الإرث عقوبة فتعلق بما تتعلق به العقوبة وهو القصاص والكفاره. والشافعي يعلقه بمطلق القتل حيث لا يرث عنده إذا قتله بقصاص أو رجم أو كان القريب قاضياً فحكم بذلك أو شاهداً فشهد به أو باغياً فقتله أو شهر عليه سيفاً دفعاً كل ذلك يمنع الإرث عنده، وهذا معنى له لأن القاتل أوجب عليه قتله أو جاز له قتله في هذه الصورة فكيف وجب عليه العقوبة بعد ذلك، ولهذا لا يتعلق بسائر القتل سائر العقوبات فكذا الحرمان. والمراد بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «ليس للقاتل شيء من «الميراث هو القتل بالتعدي دل عليه قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «ليس للقاتل ميراث بعد كصاحب البقرة» أي قاتل هو كصاحب البقرة وهو كان متعدياً. واحترز بقوله «مباشرة عن القتل بالتسبب . واختلاف الدين أيضاً يمنع الإرث والمراد به الاختلاف بين الإسلام والكفر بقوله ل و لا يرث المسلم الكافر ولا الكافر المسلم» وأما اختلاف ملل الكفار كالنصرانية واليهودية والمجوسية وعباد الوثن فلا يمنع الإرث حتى يجري الميراث بين اليهودي
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: ('No) the mahroom (deprived) due to slavery, direct killing, and difference of religion or abode' i.e., the one deprived by these things does not exclude anyone. According to Ibn Mas'ud, he does exclude with exclusion of reduction, like reducing the share of the spouses and mother by a deprived child (due to the mentioned reasons), because Allah mentioned the child absolutely and reduced their share by it without distinguishing between an inheriting child and a deprived one. Similarly, the reduction of the mother's share by siblings is absolute without distinction, so it is left upon its absoluteness. But he does not exclude by exclusion of total prevention (hajb hirman) because if he excluded by this exclusion while not inheriting, it would lead to pushing him (giving to him) while there is an heir, or to restricting him, because Bayt al-Mal also does not inherit with the son or siblings. The proof of the majority (including the Hanafis) is that the mahroom regarding inheritance is like the deceased because he is deprived for a reason in himself, like the deceased. Then the deceased does not exclude, so similarly the mahroom does not exclude. So it becomes like exclusion of total prevention. As for the texts that require reduction of their inheritance, we do not accept that they are absolute, because Allah first mentioned children and established inheritance for them, then after that mentioned exclusion, so it refers to those first mentioned who are eligible for inheritance. This is because the mahroom has a quality attached to him that strips him of eligibility for inheritance, so he is treated like a non-existent. Not so the mahjub, for he is eligible in himself, but his excluder prevails over him in inheritance due to greater closeness, so his effect does not become void with regard to others. And he only mentioned the cause of deprivation by his saying 'No, the mahroom due to slavery, etc.' to clarify the preventing causes of inheritance. For slavery prevents inheritance because a slave owns nothing. Allah says: 'Allah sets forth an example a slave owned (by his master) who has no power over anything' (Surah an-Nahl 16:75). And the Prophet (SAW) said: 'The slave owns nothing except divorce.' There is no difference regarding this between a full slave (qinn) and one who has a cause of freedom (like mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and partially manumitted) according to Abu Hanifa, because the meaning includes all: they cannot conceive ownership. And a mukatab does not own the physical body and is a slave as long as even one dirham remains, according to the hadith: 'He is not eligible for inheritance.' Killing that prevents inheritance is that which entails the obligation of qisas or kaffarah. What does not entail either, like killing by cause or in retaliation, does not cause deprivation because the basis of inheritance is a punishment, so it attaches to what entails punishment, i.e., qisas and kaffarah. Al-Shafi'i attaches it to absolute killing: a person does not inherit if he kills by qisas, stoning, or if the relative was a judge and passed that judgment, or a witness who testified, or an aggressor who killed, or one who draws a sword in self-defense—all that prevents inheritance according to him. This has a meaning for him because the killer either made his killing obligatory or it was permissible for him to kill in that situation; how then could punishment be imposed on him? Hence, other punishments are not attached to other types of killing, so similarly deprivation (of inheritance). And the meaning of the Prophet's (SAW) saying 'The killer has nothing of inheritance' is killing by transgression, as indicated by his (SAW) saying: 'The killer has no inheritance after [like the owner of the cow]' i.e., the killer is like the owner of the cow, and he was a transgressor. And he guarded by saying 'directly' against killing by causation. Difference of religion also prevents inheritance; intended is difference between Islam and disbelief, by his saying: 'The Muslim does not inherit from the disbeliever, nor the disbeliever from the Muslim.' As for difference among disbeliever's religions like Christianity, Judaism, Zoroastrianism, and idol worship, it does not prevent inheritance, so inheritance runs between a Jew and...
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This is a ruling with extensive proof and intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab discussion. The passage addresses the principle that a person who is legally deprived (mahroom) of inheritance due to being a slave, a direct murderer, or of a different religion does not exclude other heirs. It first presents the view of Ibn Mas'ud that such a person does cause a reduction in share (like a child who is a slave still reduces the spouse's share), but the Hanafi school and the majority hold that the mahroom does not exclude at all, whether by exclusion of total prevention (hajb hirman) or exclusion of reduction (hajb nuqsan). The reason is that the mahroom is legally like a dead person; he is not eligible for inheritance, so he cannot affect shares of others. The text contrasts the mahroom with the mahjub (who is eligible but excluded by a closer relative) and explains that the mahjub can exclude others because his eligibility is not void. Then the text details each cause: slavery (full or partial) prevents ownership; killing only when it involves qisas or kaffarah (intentional transgression); and difference of religion only between Islam and disbelief, not between different non-Muslim religions. The Hanafi position is compared with al-Shafi'i, who considers any killing (even legal) as a bar to inheritance. Finally, it clarifies that direct killing is the bar, not killing by causation.

Key Terms
المحروم (al-mahroom): An heir who is ineligible to inherit due to a personal defect (slavery, direct killing, difference of religion).
المحجوب (al-mahjub): An heir who is eligible to inherit but is excluded by a closer heir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man dies leaving his wife and a son who is a slave, the wife's share is:
A) 1/4
B) 1/8
C) 1/2
D) 1/6

Key Takeaway
The mahroom is like the deceased; he does not exclude or reduce the shares of other heirs.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
10 min
The Three Causes of Deprivation: Slavery, Killing, and Difference of Religion
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on details

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three causes that make an heir mahroom according to the Hanafi school, state the evidence for each, and distinguish the Hanafi view from the Shafi'i view on killing.

Core Idea
Only slavery, direct intentional killing, and difference between Islam and disbelief deprive an heir; not all killing or religious differences.

The Hanafi school specifies three causes that render an heir mahroom (deprived of inheritance): slavery, direct killing that entails qisas or kaffarah, and difference of religion between Islam and disbelief. This lesson details each cause with its textual and rational proofs, and highlights the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i on killing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three causes
Learn that the mahroom is created by slavery (all types), direct killing with transgression, or difference of religion (only Islam vs. kufr).
These are the only causes; other impediments like apostasy? Actually, apostasy is a form of difference of religion.
2
Examine slavery as a cause
Slavery prevents inheritance because a slave cannot own property. This applies to all types of slaves: full, mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and partially free.
Evidence: Quran 16:75 and hadith 'The slave owns nothing except divorce.'
3
Examine killing as a cause
Only intentional, transgressive killing that entails qisas or kaffarah deprives the killer of inheritance. Killing by cause (e.g., accidental) or in self-defense does not.
Proof: Hadith 'The killer has nothing of inheritance' is restricted to transgression. Shafi'i extends it to all killing.
4
Examine difference of religion
Only difference between Islam and disbelief prevents inheritance. Differences among non-Muslim religions (e.g., Jew vs. Christian) do not.
Evidence: Hadith 'The Muslim does not inherit from the disbeliever, nor the disbeliever from the Muslim.'
5
Distinguish Hanafi from Shafi'i on killing
Shafi'i holds that any killing, even legal (e.g., execution by a judge), bars inheritance. Hanafi restricts it to killing that is a crime (qisas/kaffarah).
The thamarah: in Hanafi, a judge who executes a murderer can still inherit from him; in Shafi'i, he cannot.

Key Terms
القاتل مباشرة (al-qatil mubasharah): A direct killer whose act of killing is immediate, as opposed to killing by causation (tasabbub).
القتل بالتسبب (al-qatl bi al-tasabbub): Killing by causation, e.g., digging a well that causes someone to fall in.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
15 min
The Deprived Heir Does Not Cause Hijab of Deficiency
Ruling with proof on deprived heirs not causing hijab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that a person deprived of inheritance due to slavery, murder, difference of religion, or abode does not cause any reduction in the shares of other heirs, and distinguish this from the view of Ibn Mas'ud.

Core Idea
A person prevented from inheriting is like the dead—neither reduces nor excludes others.

This lesson tackles a subtle but critical principle in Hanafi inheritance law: the legal effect of a person who is disqualified from inheriting (al-mahrum). While intuition might suggest that such a person should still affect the shares of other heirs by their existence, the Hanafi school rules otherwise. We will examine the ruling, its proof, and the opposing view of Ibn Mas'ud, ultimately seeing why the deprived are treated as non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four causes of deprivation
Read the passage to list the four causes that make a person mahrum (deprived of inheritance): slavery, direct murder (qatl bi al-ta'addi), difference of religion (Islam vs. kufr), and difference of abode (Dar al-Islam vs. Dar al-Harb). Note that each cause perpetually bars inheritance.
Hanafis consider these as mawani' (preventers) that strip eligibility entirely.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on their effect
The matn states that the mahrum does not cause hijab for anyone. Understand that this means no hijab of deficiency (nuqsan) and no hijab of exclusion (hirman). They are treated as non-existent in the inheritance calculation.
Contrast with the effect of a living heir who is not deprived, e.g., a son reduces the mother's share.
3
Understand the opposing view of Ibn Mas'ud
Ibn Mas'ud held that the mahrum does cause hijab of deficiency based on the absolute wording of Quranic verses (e.g., Allah mentions children reducing parents' shares without qualification). Consider the potential practical consequence: if the mahrum does not inherit, but reduces others, the share goes to the public treasury, which the Hanafis argue is unintended.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both sides use the same Quranic texts but differ on interpretation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The majority argue by qiyas: the mahrum is like a dead person in inheritance matters—both lack eligibility. Since the dead cause no hijab, the mahrum likewise causes none. Also, the Quranic verses are not absolute; they refer to heirs (those who actually inherit), not the deprived. Identify the two key proofs: the analogy to the dead and the contextual interpretation of the verses.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa).
5
Apply to a hypothetical scenario
Consider a deceased leaving a wife and a son who is a slave. According to the Hanafi view, the wife takes her full 1/8 (unaffected by the slave's existence). According to Ibn Mas'ud, the wife's share would be reduced or the inheritance would go to the public treasury. Apply the principle to another scenario: a mother inheriting with a son who is a murderer.
Ensure the student sees the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الفرائض
قال رحمه الله : (لا) المحروم بالرق والقتل مباشرة واختلاف الدين أو الدار) أي لا يحجب المحروم بهذه الأشياء أحداً وعند ابن مسعود يحجب حجب النقصان كنقص نصيب الزوجين والأم بالولد المحروم بما ذكرنا لأن الله تعالى ذكر الولد مطلقاً ونقص به نصيبهم من غير فصل بين أن يكون وارثاً أو محروماً، وكذا نقص نصيب الأم بالإخوة مطلقاً من غير فصل فيترك على إطلاقه ولا يحجب حجب الحرمان لأنه لو حجب هذا الحجب وهو لا يرث لأدى إلى دفعه إلى مع وجود الوارث أو إلى تضييقه لأن بيت المال أيضاً لا يرث مع الابن أو الإخوة . وجه قول الجمهور أن المحروم في حق الإرث كالميت لأنه حرم لمعنى في نفسه كالميت، ثم إن الميت لا يحجب فكذا المحروم فصار كحجب الحرمان والنصوص التي توجب نقصان إرثهم لا نسلم أنها مطلقة لأن الله تعالى ذكر الأولاد أولاً وأثبت لهم ميراثاً ثم ذكر بعد ذلك حجب فينصرف إلى المذكورين أولاً وهم المتأهلون للإرث ، وهذا لأن المحروم اتصلت به بهم صفة تسلب أهلية الإرث فألحقه بالمعدوم ولا كذلك المحجوب فإنه أهل في نفسه إلا أن حاجبه عليه على إرثه لزيادة قربه فلا يبطل عمله في حق غيره. وإنما ذكر سبب الحرمان بقوله «لا المحروم بالرق الخ ليبين الأسباب المانعة من الإرث فإن الرق يمنع الإرث لأن الرقيق لا يملك شيئاً قال الله تعالى ضرب الله مثلاً عبداً مملوكاً لا يقدر على شيء [النحل : ٧٥] وقال صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يملك العبد إلا الطلاق ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن يكون قناً وهو الذي لم ينعقد له سبب الحرية وبين أن ينعقد له سبب الحرية كالمدبر والمكاتب وأم الولد ومعتق البعض عند أبي حنيفة لأن المعنى يشمل الكل وهو عدم تصور الملك لهم والمكاتب لا يملك الرقبة وهو عبد ما بقي عليه درهم على ما جاء في الخبر لا يكون أهلاً للإرث. والقتل الذي يمنع الإرث هو الذي يتعلق به وجوب القصاص أو الكفارة وما لا يتعلق به واحد منهما كالقتل بسبب أو قصاص لا يوجب الحرمان لأن وجه الإرث عقوبة فتعلق بما تتعلق به العقوبة وهو القصاص والكفاره. والشافعي يعلقه بمطلق القتل حيث لا يرث عنده إذا قتله بقصاص أو رجم أو كان القريب قاضياً فحكم بذلك أو شاهداً فشهد به أو باغياً فقتله أو شهر عليه سيفاً دفعاً كل ذلك يمنع الإرث عنده، وهذا معنی له لأن القاتل أوجب عليه قتله أو جاز له قتله في هذه الصورة فكيف وجب عليه العقوبة بعد ذلك، ولهذا لا يتعلق بسائر القتل سائر العقوبات فكذا الحرمان. والمراد بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «ليس للقاتل شيء من «الميراث هو القتل بالتعدي دل عليه قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «ليس للقاتل ميراث بعد كصاحب البقرة» أي قاتل هو كصاحب البقرة وهو كان متعدياً. واحترز بقوله «مباشرة عن القتل بالتسبب . واختلاف الدين أيضاً يمنع الإرث والمراد به الاختلاف بين الإسلام والكفر بقوله ل و لا يرث المسلم الكافر ولا الكافر المسلم» وأما اختلاف ملل الكفار كالنصرانية واليهودية والمجوسية وعباد الوثن فلا يمنع الإرث حتى يجري الميراث بين اليهودي والنصراني والمجوسي لأن الكفر كله ملة واحدة. وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام «الناس كلهم خير ونحن خير واختلاف الدارين يمنع الإرث والمؤثر هو الاختلاف حكماً حتى لا تعتبر الحقيقة بدونه حتى لا يجري الإرث بين المستأمن والذمي في دارنا ولا في دار الحرب، ويجري بين المستأمن وبين من هو في داره لأن المستأمن إذا دخل إلينا أو إليهم من أهل داره حكماً وإن كان في غيرها حقيقة والدار إنما تختلف باختلاف المنفعة والملك كدار الإسلام ودار الحرب أو دارين مختلفين من دار الحرب باختلاف ملكهم لانقطاع الولاية والتناصر فيما بينهما والإرث يكون بالولاية.
Book of Inheritances. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: '(No) the one prevented [from inheriting] by slavery, direct killing, difference of religion, or difference of abode causes [hijab]' i.e., the one prevented by these things does not cause hijab for anyone. According to Ibn Mas'ud, he does cause hijab of deficiency, like the reduction of the share of the spouses and the mother by a child who is prevented [from inheriting] by what we mentioned, because Allah the Exalted mentioned the child absolutely and reduced their share by it without distinction between whether the child is an heir or prevented. Similarly, the reduction of the mother's share by siblings is absolute without distinction, so it is left upon its absoluteness. He does not cause hijab of exclusion because if he were to cause this hijab while he does not inherit, it would lead to pushing [the inheritance] to the one with the heir or to restricting it, because the public treasury also does not inherit with the son or siblings. The argument of the majority is that the one prevented [from inheriting] is like a dead person with respect to inheritance because he is deprived due to a meaning in himself like the dead, and then the dead does not cause hijab, so similarly the one prevented, so it becomes like hijab of exclusion. The texts that require the reduction of their inheritance we do not accept that they are absolute, because Allah the Exalted mentioned children first and established inheritance for them, then after that mentioned hijab, so it refers to those mentioned first, who are qualified for inheritance. This is because the one prevented is attached to them by a quality that strips the eligibility of inheritance, so he is annexed to the non-existent, unlike the one who is blocked [by a hijab], for he is eligible in himself except that his blocker overcomes his inheritance due to increased proximity, so his effect does not become void with respect to others. He only mentioned the cause of deprivation by saying 'the one prevented by slavery etc.' to explain the preventing causes of inheritance. Slavery prevents inheritance because the slave does not own anything. Allah the Exalted said: 'Allah sets forth an example: a slave owned [by another] who has no power over anything' [Surah an-Nahl: 75]. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'The slave does not own except divorce.' There is no difference in this between a full slave (qinn), who has no cause of freedom attached, and one who has a cause of freedom attached, such as a mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, and a partial freedman according to Abu Hanifa, because the meaning encompasses all: the inability to conceive ownership for them. The mukatab does not own the [slave] body, and he is a slave as long as one dirham remains upon him, as reported in the hadith: he is not eligible for inheritance. The killing that prevents inheritance is that which entails the obligation of qisas or kaffarah, and that which entails neither, like killing by cause or in qisas, does not cause deprivation because the basis of inheritance is a punishment, so it is attached to that which the punishment is attached to, namely qisas and kaffarah. Al-Shafi'i attaches it to absolute killing, so according to him, one does not inherit if he kills him in qisas or stoning, or if the relative was a judge and thus judged [the killing], or a witness and thus testified [to it], or a rebellious party [baghi] and thus killed him, or drew a sword in self-defense—all of that prevents inheritance according to him. This is a meaning because the killer either made his killing obligatory or permissible in these cases, so how could punishment be imposed on him after that? Hence, other punishments are not attached to other types of killing, so likewise the deprivation. The intended meaning of his statement, peace be upon him, 'The killer has nothing of inheritance' is the killing with transgression, as indicated by his statement, peace be upon him, 'The killer has no inheritance, like the owner of the cow'—i.e., a killer who is like the owner of the cow, and he was a transgressor. His statement 'directly' is to exclude killing by cause. Difference of religion also prevents inheritance, and by it is meant the difference between Islam and disbelief by his statement: 'The Muslim does not inherit the disbeliever, nor does the disbeliever inherit the Muslim.' As for the difference among the religions of the disbelievers, like Christianity, Judaism, Magianism, and idol worship, it does not prevent inheritance, so inheritance occurs between the Jew, Christian, and Magian, because all disbelief is one religion. And he, peace be upon him, said: 'All people are [one] and we are [one].' Difference of the two abodes also prevents inheritance, and the effective factor is the difference in legal ruling, so the reality without it is not considered, so inheritance does not occur between a musta'min and a dhimmi in our abode nor in the war abode, and it does occur between a musta'min and one who is in his abode because the musta'min, when he enters to us or to them, is from the people of his abode legally, even if he is outside it physically. The abode only differs by difference of benefit and ownership, like the abode of Islam and the abode of war, or two different abodes of war by difference of their ownership due to the severance of authority and mutual support between them, and inheritance is based on authority.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Kitab al-Faraid of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It addresses the legal effect of a person who is deprived of inheritance (al-mahrum) due to four causes: slavery, direct murder, difference of religion, and difference of abode. The matn states that such a person does not cause hijab (bar) for others. The commentary expands by clarifying that this means they do not cause hijab of deficiency (hajb nuqsan), i.e., they do not reduce the shares of other heirs. The text then presents the opposing view of Ibn Mas'ud, who held that they do cause such reduction, based on the absolute wording of Quranic verses that mention children and siblings reducing shares without qualification. The majority, however, argue that the deprived are like the dead in inheritance matters since they lack eligibility, and the dead do not cause hijab. The verses are interpreted as referring to heirs, not the deprived. The passage then details each cause: slavery prevents inheritance because the slave cannot own property, citing Quran 16:75 and a hadith; murder prevents only if it involves qisas or kaffarah, with a distinction between direct and indirect killing; difference of religion means Islam vs. disbelief, but different disbeliefs are one religion so they inherit from each other; difference of abode prevents inheritance because inheritance is based on mutual authority and support.

Key Terms
المحروم (al-mahrum): A person who is legally prevented from inheriting due to a perpetual bar (e.g., slavery, murder, difference of religion) despite having a relationship that would otherwise entitle them.
حجب النقصان (hajb al-nuqsan): A reduction in the prescribed share of a Quranic heir due to the presence of another heir (e.g., child reduces parents' share). The deprived do not cause this.
القتل بالتعدي (al-qatl bi al-ta'addi): Unlawful killing that is direct and without legal justification, which prevents inheritance.
اختلاف الدين (ikhtilaf al-din): Difference in religion between Islam and kufr; prevents inheritance.
اختلاف الدارين (ikhtilaf al-darayn): Difference of abode (e.g., Dar al-Islam vs. Dar al-Harb) that prevents inheritance due to lack of mutual authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A deceased Muslim leaves a mother and a son who is a slave. According to the Hanafi school, what is the mother's share?
A) 1/3
B) 1/6
C) 1/2
D) 1/8

Key Takeaway
A person disqualified from inheritance due to a perpetual bar is treated as non-existent; they neither inherit nor reduce the shares of other heirs.

Faraid — Inheritance
