Inheritance of Disbelievers: Causes and Multiple Ties
Ruling with proof on kafir inheritance rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rules for disbelievers' inheritance and explain the Hanafi view on multiple kinship ties with the supporting proof.

Core Idea
Disbelievers inherit through valid causes like Muslims; multiple ties combine unless one blocks.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that disbelievers (kuffar) have the same inheritance capacity as Muslims through lineage and cause, but they do not inherit through prohibited marriages. It then addresses a dispute: when a disbeliever has two kinship ties to one person, does he inherit through both or only the stronger? The Hanafi school, following the majority of Companions, gives effect to both causes simultaneously, rejecting the view of Malik and Shafi'i who choose the stronger tie. Understanding this principle is key to calculating shares in cases involving non-Muslims with complex family relations.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the matn's first ruling: disbelievers inherit by lineage and cause like Muslims. Understand the rationale: they are accountable and own property like Muslims.
The dhimma contract puts them on par with Muslims in transactions.
2
Analyze the multiple-ties scenario
When two kinship ties meet in one person (e.g., daughter and granddaughter), determine if one would block the other if separate. If no blocking, Hanafis give both ties effect; if blocking, only the higher tie counts.
The example: Magian marries his mother, son inherits only as son, but daughter takes both shares.
3
Examine the inter-madhhab dispute
Contrast the Hanafi position (both ties effective) with Malik/Shafi'i (only stronger tie). Note the Hanafi proof: you cannot nullify a legal cause without a preventer.
The analogy with a Muslim who inherits by both share and 'asaba.
4
Apply the rule on prohibited marriages
Understand that nikah muharram (e.g., marriage to mahram) does not confer inheritance rights, even if it had some validity in the kafir's original religion.
Abu Hanifa's view: such marriage is like fasid (defective) and not confirmed upon embracing Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والكافر يرث بالنسب والسبب (كالمسلم لأنه مختار مكلف فيملك بالأسباب الموضوعة للملك كالمسلم ولأنه بعقد الذمة التحق بالمسلم في المعاملة فيملك بالأسباب الموضوعة كالمسلم فيكون حكمه في ذلك كحكم المسلم .
قال رحمه الله : : (ولو) حجب أحدهما فبالحاجب) يعني لو اجتمع في الكافر قرابتان لو تفرقا في شخصين يحجب أحدهما الآخر يرث بالحاجب، وإن لم يحجب يرث بالقرابتين كما إذا تزوج المجوسي أمه فولدت له ابناً فهذا الولد ابنها وابن ابنها فيرث منها إذا ماتت على أنه ابن ولا يرث على أنه ابن ابن لأن ابن الابن يحجب بالابن، ولو ولدت بنتاً مكان الولد ترث الثلثين النصف على أنها بنت والسدس على أنها بنت ابن. ولو تزوج بنته فولدت له بنتاً ترث من أمها النصف على أنها بنت وترث الباقي على أنها عصبة لأنها أختها من أبيها وهي عصبة البنت، وإن مات أبوها ترث النصف على أنها بنت ولا ترث على أنها بنت البنت لأنها من ذوي الأرحام فلا ترث مع وجود ذي سهم وعصبة وهو قول عامة الصحابة رضي الله عنهم وبه أخذ أصحابنا، وفي رواية عن ابن مسعود وزيد بن ثابت أنه يرث بأثبت القرابتين أو أكدهما أي أقواهما وبه أخذ مالك والشافعي رحمهما الله، والصحيح الأول لأن فيه إعمال السبب ولا يجوز إبطاله بغير مانع والمانع الحاجب ولم يوجد فيأخذ بالجهتين ألا ترى أن المسلم يرث بالجهتين اتفق له ذلك بأن ماتت المرأة وتركت ابن عمها وهو زوجها أو أخوها من أمها فإنه يأخذ بالفرض والعصوبة فكذا الكافر إذ هو لا يخالف المسلم في سبب الملك كالشراء وغيره بخلاف الأخ من الأب والأم حيث لا يرث إلا بالعصوبة ولا يرث بالفرض على أنه أخ من أم لأنه ليس فيه اختلاف الجهة لأنه يرث بالإخوة وهي جهة واحدة فلا يصلح الاستحقاق بهما إلا للترجيح فقط عند مزاحمة من هو دونه في القوة كالأخ للأب .
قال رحمه الله : (لا بنكاح محرم) أي لا يرث الكافر بنكاح محرم كما إذا تزوج مجوسي بأمه أو غيرها من المحارم لا يرث منها بالنكاح، أما عندهما فظاهر لأن النكاح لا يصح، وأما عند أبي حنيفة فإنه ولو كان له حكم الصحة لكن لا يقر عليه إذا أسلما فكان كالفاسد.
وفي المضمرات: اعلم بأن الكفار يتوارثون فيما بينهم بالأسباب التي يتوارث بها المسلمون من نسب أو سب أو نكاح ولا خلاف أنهم لا يرثون بالأنكحة التي لا تصح
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'And the disbeliever inherits by lineage and cause like the Muslim' because he is a free agent, responsible, so he owns through the means established for ownership like the Muslim, and because by the dhimma contract he joins the Muslim in transactions, so he owns through the established means like the Muslim, thus his ruling in that is like the ruling of the Muslim. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'And if one of them is prevented, then by the preventer' meaning if two kinship ties come together in a disbeliever such that if they were in two persons one would prevent the other, he inherits through the preventer; if not prevented, he inherits through both ties, as when a Magian marries his mother and she bears him a son – this son is her son and her grandson, so he inherits from her when she dies as a son, and does not inherit as a grandson because the grandson is prevented by the son. If she bore a daughter instead of a son, she inherits two-thirds: half as a daughter and one-sixth as a granddaughter. If he married his daughter and she bore him a daughter, she inherits from her mother half as a daughter, and inherits the remainder as an 'asaba because she is her sister from her father and she is an 'asaba of the daughter. If her father dies, she inherits half as a daughter and does not inherit as a granddaughter because she is from dhawu al-arham, so she does not inherit in the presence of a Quranic heir and 'asaba. This is the statement of the general body of Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and our scholars took it. In a narration from Ibn Mas'ud and Zaid ibn Thabit, he inherits by the more established or stronger of the two ties, i.e., the strongest one, and Malik and al-Shafi'i took that. The first is correct because it involves giving effect to the cause, and it is not permissible to nullify it without a preventer, and the preventer is the barrier, which did not exist, so he takes through both aspects. Do you not see that the Muslim inherits through both aspects if that occurs, such as when a woman dies leaving her cousin who is her husband or her brother from her mother? He takes by prescribed share and 'asaba. So too the disbeliever, since he does not differ from the Muslim in the cause of ownership like purchase and others. But the brother from father and mother only inherits by 'asaba and does not inherit by prescribed share as a brother from mother because there is no difference of aspect; he inherits by brotherhood which is one aspect, so entitlement through both is only for tarjih (preference) when facing someone weaker, like the brother from father. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Not through a prohibited marriage' meaning the disbeliever does not inherit through a prohibited marriage, such as when a Magian marries his mother or other mahrams; he does not inherit from her through marriage. According to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] it is apparent because the marriage is invalid. According to Abu Hanifa, although it has a ruling of validity, they are not confirmed upon it if they become Muslim, so it is like a fasid marriage. In al-Mudmarat: Know that disbelievers inherit among themselves through the causes by which Muslims inherit, whether lineage, cause, or marriage, and there is no dispute that they do not inherit through marriages that are invalid.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the inheritance of disbelievers (kuffar) from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The matn states three sub-rulings: (1) The disbeliever inherits through lineage and cause just like a Muslim. The commentary justifies this by noting that disbelievers, like Muslims, are accountable and capable of ownership, and the dhimma contract makes them equal in transactions. (2) If a disbeliever has two potential inheritance claims (e.g., as a son and a grandson from the same person), and one would normally prevent the other, he inherits only through the one that is not prevented; if neither prevents the other, he inherits through both. The text gives detailed examples from Magian marriages (marriage to mother or daughter) which produce children who have dual relationships. The majority of Companions and Hanafis hold that such a person inherits through both relationships if there is no actual barrier, as opposed to the view of Ibn Mas'ud, Zaid, Malik, and Shafi'i who choose the stronger tie. The Hanafi view is preferred because it gives effect to both legal causes. (3) Disbelievers do not inherit through prohibited marriages (nikah muharram), such as a Magian marrying his mahram. Although the marriage may have some validity according to Abu Hanifa, it is not confirmed upon embracing Islam, so it is like a fasid marriage and does not confer inheritance rights. The passage ends with a quote from al-Mudmarat affirming that kafirs inherit through the same valid causes as Muslims, but not through invalid marriages.

Key Terms
الكافر (al-kafir): A non-Muslim who is classified as a disbeliever; inherits among themselves like Muslims through valid causes.
نكاح محرم (nikah muharram): A marriage that is prohibited due to kinship or other permanent reason; such a marriage does not confer inheritance rights even if it has some validity under the kafir's previous religion.
أثبت القرابتين (athbat al-qarabatayn): The more established or stronger of two kinship ties, according to the opposing view preferred by Malik and Shafi'i.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when a disbeliever has two valid kinship ties to the same deceased (e.g., as a daughter and as a granddaughter), and neither tie is a barrier to the other, how does he/she inherit?
A) Only through the stronger tie (e.g., as a daughter).
B) Through both ties, receiving shares from each.
C) Does not inherit because the relationships are from a prohibited marriage.
D) Inherits only through the tie that gives the larger share.

Key Takeaway
Disbelievers inherit through all valid causes of inheritance (lineage and valid marriage) like Muslims, and when multiple causes combine without a barrier, each cause is effective; invalid marriages give no inheritance.

Faraid — Inheritance

Non-Muslims Inherit Through Nasab and Sabab Like Muslims
Sharh of matn stating general rule of inheritance for non-Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that non-Muslims inherit through nasab and sabab like Muslims, and explain the two proofs (legal capacity and dhimma contract) that support it.

Core Idea
The disbeliever inherits through lineage and cause like the Muslim because he is legally responsible and the dhimma contract makes him equal in ownership.

This lesson examines a fundamental principle in Hanafi inheritance law: that non-Muslims inherit from each other through the same causes as Muslims. The text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary by Ibn Nujaym provides two rational proofs for this ruling, based on legal capacity and the contract of dhimma. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly applying faraid in mixed religious contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The text states that the disbeliever (kafir) inherits through lineage (nasab) and cause (sabab, such as marriage or wala) just as the Muslim does. This ruling applies to inheritance among non-Muslims themselves, not between a Muslim and a non-Muslim.
The ruling is general and covers all non-Muslims under Islamic jurisdiction (dhimmi, mu'ahid, etc.).
2
Understand the first proof: legal capacity
The author argues that a non-Muslim is a free, legally responsible person (mukhtar mukallaf). Therefore, he has the capacity to own property through the same legal means as a Muslim, including inheritance. The act of inheriting is a means of ownership, and legal capacity is the basis.
The term 'mukallaf' here refers to civil responsibility, not religious obligation. This is key to the analogy.
3
Understand the second proof: the dhimma contract
Through the contract of dhimma (the pact that grants protected status), non-Muslims are legally annexed to Muslims in all transactional matters (mu'amalat). Since inheritance is a transaction of ownership, they are treated equally.
This proof reinforces the first by grounding the equality in the legal framework of the Islamic state.
4
Apply the ruling to concrete examples
Consider a non-Muslim father and his non-Muslim son. If the father dies, the son inherits like a Muslim son would. Similarly, a non-Muslim husband inherits from his non-Muslim wife. The same rules of exclusion and proportion apply.
Remember: the barrier of difference of religion (ikhtilaf al-din) blocks inheritance between a Muslim and a non-Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والكافر يرث بالنسب والسبب (كالمسلم لأنه مختار مكلف فيملك بالأسباب الموضوعة للملك كالمسلم ولأنه بعقد الذمة التحق بالمسلم في المعاملة فيملك بالأسباب الموضوعة كالمسلم فيكون حكمه في ذلك كحكم المسلم .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (The disbeliever inherits through lineage and cause as the Muslim) because he is a free, responsible person, so he owns through the means established for ownership like the Muslim, and because through the contract of dhimma he is annexed to the Muslim in transactions, so he owns through the established means like the Muslim, thus his ruling in that is like the ruling of the Muslim.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It establishes that non-Muslims (kuffar) inherit from each other through the same two causes that Muslims do: lineage (nasab) and cause (sabab, e.g., marriage, slavery). The commentator provides two rational proofs. First, a non-Muslim is a free, responsible person (mukallaf), so he is legally capable of owning property through the same means that Muslims own property, such as inheritance by lineage or marriage. Second, through the contract of dhimma (‘aqd al-dhimma) that grants them protected status, they are legally annexed to Muslims in transactional matters, so their capacity to own through these means is the same as a Muslim's. Therefore, the ruling for them is identical to that for Muslims in inheritance.

Key Terms
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage: blood relationship that gives right to inheritance, e.g., parent-child, sibling.
السبب (al-sabab): Cause: legal link giving inheritance, such as marriage (nikah) or wala (clientship).
المختار المكلف (al-mukhtar al-mukallaf): The free, legally responsible person: one who has free will and is accountable under Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do non-Muslims inherit from each other through nasab and sabab like Muslims, according to this passage?
A) Because they are Muslims too.
B) Because they are mukallaf and through dhimma they are like Muslims in ownership.
C) Because all non-Muslims are considered Muslims in inheritance.
D) Because they have a special contract that overrides Islamic law.

Key Takeaway
Non-Muslims inherit through nasab and sabab like Muslims because they are legally responsible and the dhimma contract places them on equal footing in ownership matters.

Inheriting through Multiple Causes: Hanafi Position on Non-Muslim Relations
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on non-Muslim inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule for a non-Muslim with two relationships and apply the analogy (qiyas) to a Muslim inheriting through two aspects.

Core Idea
A non-Muslim inherits through both relationships unless one blocks the other—the same as a Muslim inheriting as both spouse and asaba.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a disputed inheritance case: when a non-Muslim (e.g., from Zoroastrian incestuous marriages) has two relationships to the deceased, one of which would block the other. The commentators explain the rule and contrast the Hanafi position with that of Malik and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the issue
Read the matn and commentary: a non-Muslim has two relationships that, if in separate persons, would block each other. The issue is whether the inheritor takes through both or only the stronger.
Focus on the examples: son+grandson, daughter+granddaughter, daughter+sister.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Hanafi: inherits through the blocker if present, otherwise through both. Proof: general principle of giving effect to causes, analogy to Muslim inheriting as spouse and asaba.
The qiyas is jali: clear analogy.
3
Present the opposing view and its proof
Malik and Shafi'i (following Ibn Mas'ud and Zayd): only the stronger relationship counts. They argue that a person cannot inherit twice from the same estate.
This is the competing precedent.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and the qiyas
Hanafi response: nullifying a cause without an impediment is invalid; the Muslim example proves it is possible. The blocker is the only impediment.
Notice the condition: if the two relationships are from the same aspect (e.g., brother from both parents), they do not give double shares.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Practical difference: in cases like a Zoroastrian child from incest, Hanafi gives inheritance based on the blocking relationship (e.g., as son), while the opposing view would give only the stronger share. The text declares the Hanafi position correct ('al-sahih al-awwal').
Tarjih authority: the commentator sides with the Companions majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : : (ولو) حجب أحدهما فبالحاجب) يعني لو اجتمع في الكافر قرابتان لو تفرقا في شخصين يحجب أحدهما الآخر يرث بالحاجب، وإن لم يحجب يرث بالقرابتين كما إذا تزوج المجوسي أمه فولدت له ابناً فهذا الولد ابنها وابن ابنها فيرث منها إذا ماتت على أنه ابن ولا يرث على أنه ابن ابن لأن ابن الابن يحجب بالابن، ولو ولدت بنتاً مكان الولد ترث الثلثين النصف على أنها بنت والسدس على أنها بنت ابن. ولو تزوج بنته فولدت له بنتاً ترث من أمها النصف على أنها بنت وترث الباقي على أنها عصبة لأنها أختها من أبيها وهي عصبة البنت، وإن مات أبوها ترث النصف على أنها بنت ولا ترث على أنها بنت البنت لأنها من ذوي الأرحام فلا ترث مع وجود ذي سهم وعصبة وهو قول عامة الصحابة رضي الله عنهم وبه أخذ أصحابنا، وفي رواية عن ابن مسعود وزيد بن ثابت أنه يرث بأثبت القرابتين أو أكدهما أي أقواهما وبه أخذ مالك والشافعي رحمهما الله، والصحيح الأول لأن فيه إعمال السبب ولا يجوز إبطاله بغير مانع والمانع الحاجب ولم يوجد فيأخذ بالجهتين ألا ترى أن المسلم يرث بالجهتين اتفق له ذلك بأن ماتت المرأة وتركت ابن عمها وهو زوجها أو أخوها من أمها فإنه يأخذ بالفرض والعصوبة فكذا الكافر إذ هو لا يخالف المسلم في سبب الملك كالشراء وغيره بخلاف الأخ من الأب والأم حيث لا يرث إلا بالعصوبة ولا يرث بالفرض على أنه أخ من أم لأنه ليس فيه اختلاف الجهة لأنه يرث بالإخوة وهي جهة واحدة فلا يصلح الاستحقاق بهما إلا للترجيح فقط عند مزاحمة من هو دونه في القوة كالأخ للأب .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if one of them blocks, then through the blocker). Meaning: if in a non-Muslim two relationships accumulate that, if they were in two separate persons, one would block the other, then he inherits through the blocker; but if there is no blocking, he inherits through both relationships. For example, if a Zoroastrian marries his mother and she gives birth to a son, this child is her son and her grandson; he inherits from her when she dies as a son, not as a grandson because the son blocks the grandson. And if she gives birth to a daughter instead, she inherits two-thirds: half as a daughter and a sixth as a granddaughter. And if he marries his daughter and she gives birth to a daughter, she inherits from her mother half as a daughter and the remainder as an asaba, because she is her sister through her father, and she is an asaba of the daughter. And if her father dies, she inherits half as a daughter but not as a daughter of the daughter, because she belongs to dhawu al-arham, so she does not inherit in the presence of a share-holder and an asaba. This is the saying of the generality of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and our companions took it. There is a narration from Ibn Mas'ud and Zayd ibn Thabit that he inherits through the more confirmed or stronger of the two relationships, and Malik and al-Shafi'i took that. The correct is the first, because it involves giving effect to the cause, and it is not permissible to nullify it without an impediment, and the impediment is the blocker, which does not exist here, so he takes through both aspects. Do you not see that a Muslim inherits through both aspects when that occurs, e.g., if a woman dies leaving her cousin who is her husband, or her brother from the mother, then he takes by prescribed share and asaba. Similarly, the non-Muslim, for he does not differ from the Muslim in the cause of ownership like purchase and others. Unlike the brother from father and mother, who only inherits by asaba and not by prescribed share as a brother from the mother, because there is no difference of aspect, as he inherits through brotherhood which is one aspect, so entitlement through both is only for weighting when facing someone weaker, like the brother from the father.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. The matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that if a non-Muslim has two relationships that would block each other (like son and grandson), he inherits through the blocking one (the son). The commentator explains with examples from Zoroastrian marriages. The Hanafi position, following the majority of companions, is that the inheritor takes through both causes if there is no blocker, or only through the blocker if there is one. The opposing view from Ibn Mas'ud and Zayd (followed by Malik and Shafi'i) is that only the stronger relationship counts. The Hanafi response argues that it is more correct to apply both causes because nullifying a cause without an impediment is invalid. They use qiyas to a Muslim inheriting as both spouse and asaba. However, if the two relationships are from the same 'aspect' (like brotherhood from both parents), then only one share is given, and the stronger takes precedence when there is a weaker rival.

Key Terms
الحاجب (al-hajib): The blocker: the relationship that prevents a more distant relative from inheriting, e.g., son blocks grandson.
العصبة (al-asabah): Residuary heir: one who inherits the remainder after prescribed shares, typically male agnates.
ذوو الأرحام (dhawu al-arham): Distant kindred: relatives not qualifying as prescribed sharers or residuaries, e.g., daughter's daughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of a Zoroastrian who marries his mother and has a son, how does the son inherit from his mother?
A) Only as a son.
B) Only as a grandson.
C) As both son and grandson.
D) He does not inherit.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives effect to all valid inheritance causes for non-Muslims, blocking only when an impediment (a closer heir) exists.

Inheritance by Prohibited Marriage Among Non-Muslims: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Sharh of ruling on inheritance by prohibited marriage with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on inheritance by prohibited marriage among non-Muslims and explain how each position derives from the principle of taqrir.

Core Idea
Non-Muslims inherit by valid marriages among themselves, but validity for inheritance depends on confirmation upon conversion.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced area of inheritance law: the effect of prohibited marriages among non-Muslims. While marriages like incest are universally recognized as invalid for inheritance, disputes arise over marriages that are temporarily prohibited among Muslims, such as marriage during iddah or without witnesses. The disagreement among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar hinges on the principle of taqrir—whether such marriages would be confirmed if the couple converted to Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The matn states that a non-Muslim does not inherit by a prohibited marriage (nikah muharram). The commentator gives the example of a Zoroastrian marrying his mother – such a marriage does not confer inheritance.
This is absolute prohibition – incestuous marriages are never valid.
2
Recognize the contested cases
The text then specifies two types of marriages where the ruling is disputed: marriage during the waiting period (iddah) and marriage without witnesses. These are prohibited in Islam but may be practiced among non-Muslims.
3
Map the three Hanafi positions
Zufar: do not inherit in either case. Abu Hanifa: inherit in both. Abu Yusuf: inherit in marriage without witnesses, but not in marriage during iddah.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with practical consequences.
4
Link to taqrir (confirmation upon conversion)
The disagreement stems from whether such marriages would be confirmed (taqrir) if the spouses convert to Islam. Abu Hanifa confirms both, Abu Yusuf confirms only without-witness marriage, Zufar confirms neither.
Taqrir is the key principle underlying the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (لا بنكاح محرم) أي لا يرث الكافر بنكاح محرم كما إذا تزوج مجوسي بأمه أو غيرها من المحارم لا يرث منها بالنكاح، أما عندهما فظاهر لأن النكاح لا يصح، وأما عند أبي حنيفة فإنه ولو كان له حكم الصحة لكن لا يقر عليه إذا أسلما فكان كالفاسد. وفي المضمرات: اعلم بأن الكفار يتوارثون فيما بينهم بالأسباب التي يتوارث بها المسلمون من نسب أو سب أو نكاح ولا خلاف أنهم لا يرثون بالأنكحة التي لا تصح بين المسلمين بحال نحو نكاح المحارم بسبب أو رضاع ونكاح المطلقة قبل التزوج بزوج آخر . واختلفوا في التوريث بحكم النكاح في العدة والنكاح بغير شهود قال زفر : لا يتوارثون. وقال أبو حنيفة : يتوارثون. وقال أبو يوسف : يتوارثون في النكاح بغير شهود ولا يتوارثون بالنكاح في العدة، وهذا بناء على اختلافهم في تقريرهم على هذه الانكحة إذا أسلموا وقد بينا ذلك في النكاح،
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Not by a prohibited marriage). That is, a non-Muslim does not inherit by a prohibited marriage, such as if a Zoroastrian marries his mother or other mahram; he does not inherit from her by marriage. As for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is obvious because the marriage is invalid. As for Abu Hanifa, even though it has a ruling of validity, they are not left upon it if they embrace Islam, so it is like a corrupt marriage. In al-Mudmarat: Know that non-Muslims inherit among themselves through the same causes that Muslims inherit, by lineage, reason, or marriage. There is no disagreement that they do not inherit by marriages that are absolutely invalid among Muslims, such as marriage to mahrams due to lineage or fosterage, and marriage to a divorced woman before remarrying another husband. They differed regarding inheritance by marriage during the waiting period (iddah) and marriage without witnesses. Zufar said: They do not inherit. Abu Hanifa said: They inherit. Abu Yusuf said: They inherit in marriage without witnesses, but do not inherit in marriage during iddah. This is based on their difference regarding confirming them on these marriages if they embrace Islam, and we have explained that in the Book of Nikah.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on inheritance by prohibited marriage among non-Muslims, with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states that a non-Muslim does not inherit by a prohibited marriage (like incestuous marriages). The commentator explains that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold it obvious because such marriages are invalid. Abu Hanifa considers them valid in effect (hukm al-sihha) but since they are not maintained upon conversion to Islam, they are like fasid (corrupt) marriages. The text then quotes al-Mudmarat, which reiterates that non-Muslims inherit by the same causes as Muslims, but not by marriages that are entirely invalid for Muslims. There is an ikhtilaf regarding marriages during iddah and marriages without witnesses: Zufar says they do not inherit; Abu Hanifa says they do; Abu Yusuf distinguishes: inheritance in marriage without witnesses is valid, but not in marriage during iddah. This difference stems from whether such marriages are affirmed upon conversion (taqrir).

Key Terms
المحرم (al-mahram): Mahram: a person with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to lineage, fosterage, or affinity.
النكاح الفاسد (al-nikah al-fasid): Corrupt marriage: a marriage that is temporarily invalid due to a curable defect, e.g., marrying during iddah without knowledge.
تقرير (taqrir): Confirmation: the act of leaving non-Muslims upon their existing marriages after conversion if they are valid in their own law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis of the disagreement between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar regarding inheritance by marriage during iddah?
A) Whether the marriage contract had witnesses.
B) Whether the marriage would be confirmed (taqrir) upon conversion to Islam.
C) Whether the spouses were Muslim at the time of marriage.
D) Whether the marriage was contracted within the iddah period of the wife's previous husband.

Key Takeaway
Non-Muslims do not inherit by absolutely prohibited marriages; for disputed marriages (iddah, no witnesses), inheritance depends on taqrir, leading to three Hanafi positions.

Principle of Inheritance Between Harbi and Dhimmi: Difference of Dar
General principles of inheritance among non-Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance between a harbi and a dhimmi, and explain how the concept of difference of dar (ikhtilaf al-darayn) determines inheritance among non-Muslims.

Core Idea
Difference of dar, not religion, governs inheritance among non-Muslims in the Hanafi school.

This lesson addresses a key principle in Hanafi inheritance law concerning non-Muslims. The passage establishes that a harbi (enemy combatant) does not inherit from a dhimmi (protected citizen), regardless of location. It then explains that dhimmis of all sects inherit from each other because all disbelief is one religion. The crucial concept introduced is the difference of dar (two abodes): inheritance is possible between harbis if they share the same dar, but cut off if they belong to different dar. Understanding this requires grasping the Hanafi definition of dar al-Islam and dar al-harb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage begins by stating that a harbi does not inherit from a dhimmi, whether the harbi is a musta'min in dar al-Islam or in dar al-harb. Note the unanimity among the Hanafi companions on this.
2
Understand the exception for dhimmis
Dhimmis inherit from each other regardless of their specific religion (e.g., Jewish, Christian) because all kufr (disbelief) is considered one religion according to the generality of the Companions.
This is based on ijma.
3
Analyze the rule for harbis
Abu Hanifa and his companions allowed harbis from the same dar to inherit from each other, but not if they are from different dar. The passage defines 'difference of dar' as two separate rulers with separate territories who consider killing each other's subjects.
This is a unique Hanafi criterion.
4
Contrast with Muslims
Within dar al-Islam, even rebels (ahl al-baghy) inherit from just rulers because Islamic law unites them. This contrasts with the situation in dar al-harb, where difference of dar is determined by hostility and separate rule.
The unity of Islamic rulings overrides political differences among Muslims.
5
Apply the musta'min scenario
If non-Muslims from different dar come to dar al-Islam as musta'mins, they are treated as if they remain in the dar they came from, so inheritance between them does not occur unless they become dhimmis or Muslims.
This preserves the original difference of dar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا خلاف بين أصحابنا أن الكافر الحربي لا يرث الذمي سواء كان الحربي مستأمناً في دارنا أو في دار الحرب. وأهل الذمة يرث بعضهم بعضاً وإن اختلفت صورة مللهم عند عامة الصحابة لأن الكفر كله ملة واحدة فجعلوا اليهود والنصارى ملة وكان أبو حنيفة وأصحابه يورثون أهل الحرب بعضهم من بعض إذا كانوا من أهل دار ،واحدة ، وإن اختلفت الدار أن لم يورثوا . تفسيراً اختلاف الدارين أن يكونا ملكين في موضوعين ويرى كل واحد قتل الآخر وإن اتفقت الملل، وهذا بخلافنا فإن أهل العدل مع أهل البغي يتوارثون فيما بينهم لأن دار الإسلام دار الأحكام فباختلاف الملك والمنفعة لا تتغير الدار فيما بين المسلمين لأن أحكام الإسلام تجمعهم، وأما دار الحرب فليست بدار الأحكام بل هي دار قهر وباختلاف الملل تختلف الدار بينهم، واختلاف الدارين يقطع التوارث وكذلك إذا خرجوا الينا بأمان يعني أهل الدارين المختلفين بينهم من أهل الحرب وإن كانوا مستأمنين فيجعل كل واحد منهم في الحكم كأنه في البقعة التي خرج منها بأمان بخلاف ما إذا صاروا ذمة لأهل الإسلام يتوارثون فيما بينهم بعد ذلك كما لو أسلموا فإنه يجري التوارث بعد ما مات بينهم . وإن اختلفت منعتهم في حالة الكفر جئنا إلى المسائل : ذمي مات وخلف ورثة في دار الحرب فماله فيء سواء كانت الورثة في دار الحرب أو في دار الإسلام معاهدين. ولو مات اليهودي وترك ابناً يهودياً في دار الإسلام يؤدي الجزية وابناً له في دار الحرب فالمال كله للابن اليهودي الذي يؤدي الجزية في دار الإسلام ولو مات يهودي من أهل الحرب وهو مستأمن في دار الإسلام وترك ابناً مستأمناً في دار الإسلام وابناً ذمياً وابناً حربياً وابناً مسلماً فالمال على قول أهل العراق بين الابن المعاهد والحربي لأن المعاهد بمنزلة الحربي عندهم فيرث منه الحربي ومن هو مثله وهو المعاهد . ولو مات يهودي من أهل الذمة وخلف ابناً يهودياً وابناً نصرانياً فعلى قول من يورث أهل الذمة بعضهم من بعض، وإن اختلفت صور مللهم المال بينهما نصفان، وعلى قول من يقول بأن اليهود ملة ) والنصارى ملة المال للابن اليهودي.
There is no disagreement among our companions that a harbi disbeliever does not inherit from a dhimmi, whether the harbi is a musta'min in our dar or in dar al-harb. And dhimmis inherit from each other even if their religions differ, according to the generality of the Companions, because all disbelief is one religion; they made Jews and Christians one religion. Abu Hanifa and his companions would make people of war inherit from each other if they were from one dar, but if the dar differed they did not. The interpretation of difference of dar is that they are two rulers in different territories, each considering the killing of the other, even if their religions agree. This is unlike us, for the people of justice and people of rebellion inherit among themselves because dar al-Islam is a dar of rulings; with difference of rule and benefit the dar does not change among Muslims because the rulings of Islam unite them. As for dar al-harb, it is not a dar of rulings but a dar of force; with difference of religions the dar differs among them. Difference of dar cuts off inheritance. Similarly, if they come out to us under aman—meaning the people of two different dar among the people of war, even if they are musta'mins—each one is deemed in ruling as if he is in the place from which he came out under aman, unlike when they become dhimmis to the people of Islam, they inherit among themselves thereafter, just as if they embraced Islam, then inheritance applies after death among them. And if their opponents differ in the state of disbelief, we come to cases: A dhimmi dies leaving heirs in dar al-harb, his property is fay' whether the heirs are in dar al-harb or in dar al-Islam as mu'ahids. If a Jew dies leaving a Jewish son in dar al-Islam who pays jizya and a son of his in dar al-harb, all the wealth goes to the Jewish son who pays jizya in dar al-Islam. If a Jew from the people of war is a musta'min in dar al-Islam and leaves a musta'min son in dar al-Islam, a dhimmi son, a harbi son, and a Muslim son, then according to the people of Iraq, the wealth is between the mu'ahid son and the harbi son because the mu'ahid is like a harbi in their view, so the harbi inherits from him and from one like him, i.e., the mu'ahid. If a Jew from the people of dhimma dies leaving a Jewish son and a Christian son, then according to those who make dhimmis inherit from each other even if their religions differ, the wealth is shared half and half; according to those who say Jews are one religion and Christians another, the wealth goes to the Jewish son.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof covering various scenarios of inheritance between different categories of non-Muslims. It first states that a harbi (enemy combatant) does not inherit from a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim). Dhimmis inherit from each other regardless of their specific sect (Jewish, Christian, etc.) because all disbelief is one religion. Abu Hanifa and his followers also allowed harbis from the same dar to inherit from each other, but not if they are from different dar. The concept of 'difference of dar' (ikhtilaf al-darayn) is defined as two separate rulers with separate territories who consider killing each other's subjects. This is contrasted with Muslims: in dar al-Islam, rebels (ahl al-baghy) inherit from just rulers because Islamic law unites them. The passage then applies these principles to specific cases: a dhimmi's property left in dar al-harb becomes fay' (booty); a Jew's son in dar al-Islam inherits all if another son is in dar al-harb; and a mixed case with multiple sons of different statuses is resolved according to the view that mu'ahid is like harbi. Finally, a case where a Jew and Christian son of a dhimmi parent: those who treat all dhimmi faiths as one religion split equally; those who consider them separate give all to the Jewish son (since the father was Jewish).

Key Terms
الداران (al-daran): The two abodes: dar al-Islam and dar al-harb, distinguished by ruling and security.
المستأمن (al-musta'min): One who enters dar al-Islam with a temporary safe passage (aman).
أهل الذمة (ahl al-dhimma): Non-Muslims living under Muslim protection with a covenant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for dhimmis inheriting from each other despite different religions?
A) Because they all live in dar al-Islam.
B) Because the Companions agreed that all disbelief is one religion.
C) Because they pay jizya.
D) Because they are all considered Muslims.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance, difference of dar (sovereignty/hostility) is the primary factor for harbis, while for dhimmis all religions are treated as one.

Specific Inheritance Cases Among Non-Muslims
Four specific inheritance cases involving non-Muslims with mixed statuses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of inheritance to specific scenarios involving non-Muslims with mixed statuses (dhimmi, harbi, musta'min, mu'ahid).

Core Idea
In specific cases, the Hanafi school treats mu'ahid like harbi, and a son in dar al-Islam inherits exclusively over a son in dar al-harb.

The passage now moves from general principles to concrete cases, testing the rules on inheritance between non-Muslims. Four scenarios are presented: a dhimmi with heirs in dar al-harb (property becomes fay'), a Jew with sons in different dar, a musta'min with multiple sons of different statuses, and a dhimmi with Jewish and Christian sons. Each case illustrates how the concepts of difference of dar, musta'min status, and the unity of disbelief interact to produce specific inheritance outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the first case
A dhimmi dies and leaves heirs in dar al-harb. The passage rules that his property becomes fay' (booty for the state), regardless of whether the heirs are in dar al-harb or dar al-Islam as mu'ahids. This is because the heirs are in different dar from the decedent? Actually, the decedent is a dhimmi in dar al-Islam, but the heirs are in dar al-harb. The rule here is that if a dhimmi's heir is in dar al-harb, the heir does not inherit because of difference of dar? The passage says 'فماله فيء سواء كانت الورثة في دار الحرب أو في دار الإسلام معاهدين.' It seems the property goes to the state. Why? Possibly because the heirs are not in the same dar as the decedent? But it includes mu'ahids in dar al-Islam. The text might be saying that if the heirs are in dar al-harb or are mu'ahids in dar al-Islam, they don't inherit? Actually, a mu'ahid is like a dhimmi? The passage might be indicating that the property becomes fay' because the heirs are not in a position to inherit (perhaps because they are not from the same category?). Let's interpret: The passage earlier stated that dhimmis inherit from each other. But here, the decedent is a dhimmi, and the heirs are in dar al-harb (harbis) or mu'ahids (treaty people) in dar al-Islam. Possibly, the heirs are not dhimmis but harbis or mu'ahids, so they are not of the same status to inherit? The passage says 'معاهدين' which could mean they are not dhimmis but have a treaty. In that case, they might be considered like harbis? The Hanafi view is that a mu'ahid is treated like a harbi for inheritance? The next case gives a clue: a mu'ahid son is treated like a harbi. So in this first case, I think the idea is that if a dhimmi's heir is a harbi or mu'ahid (not a fellow dhimmi), the inheritance does not pass; instead it becomes fay'. The text says 'سواء كانت الورثة في دار الحرب أو في دار الإسلام معاهدين' meaning whether the heirs are in dar al-harb (as harbis) or in dar al-Islam as mu'ahids. So both are excluded. So the step should identify this rule.
The decedent is dhimmi, heirs are not dhimmi but harbi/mu'ahid, so no inheritance.
2
Second case: a Jew with sons in different dar
A Jew dies leaving a Jewish son in dar al-Islam (who pays jizya, so a dhimmi) and another Jewish son in dar al-harb (a harbi). The entire wealth goes to the son in dar al-Islam. This shows that a son in dar al-Islam excludes a son in dar al-harb, even though both are Jewish.
3
Third case: a musta'min with multiple sons
A Jew from dar al-harb is a musta'min in dar al-Islam and dies leaving a musta'min son, a dhimmi son, a harbi son, and a Muslim son. According to the 'people of Iraq' (Hanafis), the wealth is divided between the mu'ahid (dhimmi) son and the harbi son. The musta'min son and the Muslim son are excluded. Why? The text says because the mu'ahid is like a harbi, so they inherit from each other. The musta'min son is also like a harbi? But he is not included because he is from a different dar? Actually, all sons are from the same father who was a musta'min. The text says 'بين الابن المعاهد والحربي' – the mu'ahid (dhimmi) and the harbi inherit. This implies that the musta'min son and the Muslim son do not inherit. The reason: the musta'min son is considered as if he is in the original dar (dar al-harb) along with the father? But if the father was a musta'min in dar al-Islam, and the son is also a musta'min, they are both in dar al-Islam, so difference of dar? Possibly because the father died as a harbi? Actually, the father is described as 'مستأمناً' at death. The sons: one musta'min (same status), one dhimmi (converted to dhimma), one harbi (stayed in dar al-harb), one Muslim. The ruling: only the mu'ahid and the harbi inherit. The musta'min son does not inherit because he is considered as if in the original dar? But the father is also a musta'min, so they share dar? Perhaps the rule is that a musta'min does not inherit from a dhimmi? The text says 'على قول أهل العراق بين الابن المعاهد والحربي' – this is the Hanafi position. The explanation: 'لأن المعاهد بمنزلة الحربي عندهم'. So the mu'ahid (dhimmi) is treated like a harbi for this purpose. This is a specific rule. The Muslim son is excluded because he is of a different religion? Actually, a Muslim does not inherit from a non-Muslim. So that is clear. The musta'min son? He is a musta'min, and the father is also a musta'min. But maybe the ruling is that a musta'min does not inherit from a musta'min? Or maybe the musta'min son is considered as if he is in the original dar, but the father is in dar al-Islam? The text doesn't explicitly exclude the musta'min son; it only says the wealth is between the mu'ahid and the harbi. So the musta'min son is implicitly excluded. Possibly because the musta'min son inherits from the father? Actually, need to check: In the second case, the harbi son was excluded because the other son was in dar al-Islam. Here, the musta'min son is in dar al-Islam, and the father is also in dar al-Islam, but the father is a musta'min, not a dhimmi. The rule might be that a musta'min inherits from his father? But the passage says otherwise. I think the key is that the father died as a musta'min, and his musta'min son is also a musta'min, but they are from different dar? Actually, the father is a musta'min in dar al-Islam, while the musta'min son is also in dar al-Islam. But the father originally came from dar al-harb. The musta'min son may have been born in dar al-Islam? The passage is complex. For the guided path, we should simply state the ruling as given, without overinterpreting.
The Hanafi ruling: only the mu'ahid (dhimmi) and harbi sons inherit; musta'min and Muslim sons are excluded.
4
Fourth case: a dhimmi Jew with Jewish and Christian sons
A dhimmi Jew dies leaving a Jewish son and a Christian son. There is a difference of opinion: those who treat all dhimmi religions as one give each son half; those who treat Jews and Christians as separate religions give all to the Jewish son (since the father was Jewish).
The Hanafi view (people of Iraq) is that they inherit equally because all disbelief is one.

There is no disagreement among our companions that a harbi disbeliever does not inherit from a dhimmi, whether the harbi is a musta'min in our dar or in dar al-harb. And dhimmis inherit from each other even if their religions differ, according to the generality of the Companions, because all disbelief is one religion; they made Jews and Christians one religion. Abu Hanifa and his companions would make people of war inherit from each other if they were from one dar, but if the dar differed they did not. The interpretation of difference of dar is that they are two rulers in different territories, each considering the killing of the other, even if their religions agree. This is unlike us, for the people of justice and people of rebellion inherit among themselves because dar al-Islam is a dar of rulings; with difference of rule and benefit the dar does not change among Muslims because the rulings of Islam unite them. As for dar al-harb, it is not a dar of rulings but a dar of force; with difference of religions the dar differs among them. Difference of dar cuts off inheritance. Similarly, if they come out to us under aman—meaning the people of two different dar among the people of war, even if they are musta'mins—each one is deemed in ruling as if he is in the place from which he came out under aman, unlike when they become dhimmis to the people of Islam, they inherit among themselves thereafter, just as if they embraced Islam, then inheritance applies after death among them. And if their opponents differ in the state of disbelief, we come to cases: A dhimmi dies leaving heirs in dar al-harb, his property is fay' whether the heirs are in dar al-harb or in dar al-Islam as mu'ahids. If a Jew dies leaving a Jewish son in dar al-Islam who pays jizya and a son of his in dar al-harb, all the wealth goes to the Jewish son who pays jizya in dar al-Islam. If a Jew from the people of war is a musta'min in dar al-Islam and leaves a musta'min son in dar al-Islam, a dhimmi son, a harbi son, and a Muslim son, then according to the people of Iraq, the wealth is between the mu'ahid son and the harbi son because the mu'ahid is like a harbi in their view, so the harbi inherits from him and from one like him, i.e., the mu'ahid. If a Jew from the people of dhimma dies leaving a Jewish son and a Christian son, then according to those who make dhimmis inherit from each other even if their religions differ, the wealth is shared half and half; according to those who say Jews are one religion and Christians another, the wealth goes to the Jewish son.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof covering various scenarios of inheritance between different categories of non-Muslims. It first states that a harbi (enemy combatant) does not inherit from a dhimmi (protected non-Muslim). Dhimmis inherit from each other regardless of their specific sect (Jewish, Christian, etc.) because all disbelief is one religion. Abu Hanifa and his followers also allowed harbis from the same dar to inherit from each other, but not if they are from different dar. The concept of 'difference of dar' (ikhtilaf al-darayn) is defined as two separate rulers with separate territories who consider killing each other's subjects. This is contrasted with Muslims: in dar al-Islam, rebels (ahl al-baghy) inherit from just rulers because Islamic law unites them. The passage then applies these principles to specific cases: a dhimmi's property left in dar al-harb becomes fay' (booty); a Jew's son in dar al-Islam inherits all if another son is in dar al-harb; and a mixed case with multiple sons of different statuses is resolved according to the view that mu'ahid is like harbi. Finally, a case where a Jew and Christian son of a dhimmi parent: those who treat all dhimmi faiths as one religion split equally; those who consider them separate give all to the Jewish son (since the father was Jewish).

Key Terms
المعاهد (al-mu'ahid): A dhimmi or one with a treaty; in this context, a dhimmi son treated like a harbi for inheritance.
فيء (fay'): Property that goes to the public treasury, typically from enemies without active warfare.
أهل العراق (ahl al-iraq): Reference to the Hanafi school, centered in Iraq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All wealth goes to the Jewish son.
B) All wealth goes to the Christian son.
C) The wealth is divided equally between them.
D) The wealth goes to the state.

Key Takeaway
Specific cases refine the general rules: a dhimmi's property becomes fay' if heirs are not eligible; a son in dar al-Islam excludes a son in dar al-harb; a musta'min's son who is a musta'min may not inherit; and dhimmis of different sects inherit equally.

Three Principles for Inheritance Among Zoroastrians (Majus)
Definition of three principles for Zoroastrian inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three principles governing inheritance among Zoroastrians and apply them to determine inheritance rights in a given family scenario.

Core Idea
Zoroastrians inherit through valid marriages and through lineage established even by invalid marriages.

In Hanafi inheritance law, Zoroastrians (Majus) have unique rules because their marriage practices may include close-kin unions. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq summarizes three principles that govern how inheritance works among them. These principles are essential for correctly calculating shares in family disputes and distinguishing between valid and invalid marriages upon conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three principles
List the three principles as stated: inheritance only through valid marriages, lineage established through invalid marriages, and inheritance through multiple causes unless blocked.
These principles are axiomatic for all Zoroastrian inheritance cases.
2
Understand the test for valid marriage
A marriage is valid for inheritance if, upon both spouses converting to Islam, they would be allowed to remain in that marriage. If they would be separated, the marriage is invalid for inheritance.
This is the key criterion (al-fasil).
3
Distinguish marriage from lineage
Even if a marriage is invalid for inheritance, the lineage (nasab) from that marriage is still recognized. Children inherit through that lineage, though the spouses do not inherit from each other through the marriage.
Principle 2 separates the two causes.
4
Apply the multiple-causes rule
If a person has two or more connections to the deceased (e.g., as brother and nephew), he inherits through all unless one blocks the other; then only the blocker is considered.
Hajb (blocking) supersedes multiple causes.
5
Work through the example
Consider a Zoroastrian who marries his mother, daughter, or sister. Since this marriage is invalid (they would not be left upon conversion), the spouse does not inherit. However, any children from that marriage still inherit through lineage.
The text cuts off, but the principle is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ميراث المجوسي فيما بينهم يبنى على أصول ثلاثة : أحدها أنهم لا يتوارثون بالأنكحة الفاسدة فيما بينهم وإنما يتوارثون بالأنكحة الصحيحة، والفاصل أن كل نكاح لو أسلما تركا على ذلك فهو نكاح صحيح، ولو أسلما لم يتركا فهو نكاح فاسد والثاني أن النسب فيما بينهم يثبت بالأنكحة الفاسدة ويتوارثون فيما بينهم بذلك النسب وإن كانوا لا يتوارثون بذلك النكاح الثالث أن كل من يدلي إلى الميت بسببين أو ثلاثة فإنه يرث بجميع ذلك إلا إذا كان أحد السببين يحجب الآخر فحينئذ يرث بالحاجب وقد قدمناه . ولو تزوج بأمه أو بابنته أو بأخته فمات أحدهما لا يرث الآخر، وهذا الجواب على .
As for inheritance among Zoroastrians, it is based on three principles: First, they do not inherit from each other through invalid marriages; they only inherit through valid marriages. The distinction is that any marriage which, if they both embrace Islam, they are left upon it, is a valid marriage; if they are not left upon it, it is invalid. Second, lineage among them is established through invalid marriages, and they inherit from each other through that lineage, even though they do not inherit through that marriage. Third, whoever connects to the deceased through two or three causes inherits through all of them, unless one of the two causes blocks the other; in that case he inherits through the blocker, and we have already discussed this. If he marries his mother, daughter, or sister, and one of them dies, the other does not inherit. This answer is on...
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the three principles governing inheritance among Zoroastrians (Majus). It serves as a summary rule. The three principles are: (1) They only inherit through valid marriages; a marriage is considered valid if the couple would be allowed to remain in it upon conversion to Islam. (2) Lineage (nasab) is established through invalid marriages, so inheritance through lineage is allowed even if the marriage itself was invalid. (3) If a person has multiple connections to the deceased (e.g., as son and grandson), he inherits through all, unless one blocks the other; in that case, only the blocker is considered. The passage concludes with an example: if a Zoroastrian marries his mother, daughter, or sister, and one dies, the other does not inherit from the deceased through that marriage. The text appears incomplete, but the principles are fully stated.

Key Terms
المجوسي (al-majusi): Zoroastrian: a follower of the pre-Islamic Persian religion. In Hanafi law, they have special rules for inheritance due to their incestuous marriage practices.
النكاح الصحيح (al-nikah al-sahih): Valid marriage: one that Islamic law would uphold if the couple converted, i.e., not incestuous and otherwise lawful in Islam.
الحجب (al-hajb): Blocking: the exclusion of one heir by another from inheritance. In Principle 3, it applies when multiple causes conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only the daughter inherits through lineage.
C) Both husband and daughter inherit.
D) Neither inherits because the marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Zoroastrian inheritance separates marriage and lineage: invalid marriages block spousal inheritance but recognize children's rights.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Inheritance from Invalid Marriages and the Milla Principle
Intra-Hanafi dispute and milla principle for Dhimmi inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on inheritance from marriage during 'idda and without witnesses, and explain why Dhimmis inherit from each other regardless of sect.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa validates inheritance from both invalid marriages; Zufar denies both; Abu Yusuf distinguishes.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether non-Muslims who marry during the waiting period or without witnesses can inherit from each other if they later become Muslim. It then establishes the foundational rule that all Dhimmis inherit from one another, regardless of sect, because all kufr is considered one milla according to the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Note the three opinions: Zufar (no inheritance), Abu Hanifa (inheritance from both), and Abu Yusuf (inheritance from marriage without witnesses only).
2
Understand the basis of the dispute
The disagreement is rooted in whether such marriages are confirmed (taqrir) when the couple becomes Muslim. This was discussed earlier in the book of marriage.
The reasoning is that if they continue the marriage upon conversion, it is treated as valid retroactively for inheritance.
3
State the consensus on Harbi and Dhimmi
There is no disagreement that a Harbi does not inherit from a Dhimmi, even if the Harbi is a Musta'min in Dar al-Islam.
This establishes a clear boundary between enemy and protected non-Muslims.
4
Explain the milla principle
Dhimmis inherit from each other even if they belong to different religious sects (e.g., Jew and Christian) because all kufr is one milla according to the general body of Companions.
This is the position of the Hanafi school and the common view of the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وباختلاف الملل تختلف الدار بينهم، واختلاف الدارين يقطع التوارث وكذلك إذا خرجوا الينا بأمان يعني أهل الدارين المختلفين بينهم من أهل الحرب وإن كانوا مستأمنين فيجعل كل واحد منهم في الحكم كأنه في البقعة التي خرج منها بأمان بخلاف ما إذا صاروا ذمة لأهل الإسلام يتوارثون فيما بينهم بعد ذلك كما لو أسلموا فإنه يجري التوارث بعد ما مات بينهم . وإن اختلفت منعتهم في حالة الكفر جئنا إلى المسائل : ذمي مات وخلف ورثة في دار الحرب فماله فيء سواء كانت الورثة في دار الحرب أو في دار الإسلام معاهدين. ولو مات اليهودي وترك ابناً يهودياً في دار الإسلام يؤدي الجزية وابناً له في دار الحرب فالمال كله للابن اليهودي الذي يؤدي الجزية في دار الإسلام ولو مات يهودي من أهل الحرب وهو مستأمن في دار الإسلام وترك ابناً مستأمناً في دار الإسلام وابناً ذمياً وابناً حربياً وابناً مسلماً فالمال على قول أهل العراق بين الابن المعاهد والحربي لأن المعاهد بمنزلة الحربي عندهم فيرث منه الحربي ومن هو مثله وهو المعاهد . ولو مات يهودي من أهل الذمة وخلف ابناً يهودياً وابناً نصرانياً فعلى قول من يورث أهل الذمة بعضهم من بعض، وإن اختلفت صور مللهم المال بينهما نصفان، وعلى قول من يقول بأن اليهود ملة) والنصارى ملة المال للابن اليهودي.
Among Muslims, by way of marriage to prohibited women due to lineage or fosterage, and marriage to a divorced woman before remarrying another husband. And they differed regarding inheritance based on marriage during the waiting period and marriage without witnesses: Zufar said: They do not inherit. Abu Hanifa said: They inherit. Abu Yusuf said: They inherit in marriage without witnesses but do not inherit in marriage during the waiting period. And this is based on their disagreement about confirming these marriages when they become Muslim, and we have explained that in the book of marriage. And there is no disagreement among our companions that the Harbi infidel does not inherit from the Dhimmi, whether the Harbi is a Musta'min in our territory or in the territory of war. And the people of the covenant (Dhimmis) inherit from each other even if their sects differ, according to the general body of the Companions, because all disbelief is one religion, so they considered Jews and Christians as one religion. And Abu Hanifa and his companions made people of war inherit from each other if they are from one territory, but if the territories differ, they do not inherit. The interpretation of the difference of territories is that they are two kingdoms in two locations, and each considers it permissible to kill the other, even if their religions agree. And this is contrary to our case, for the people of justice (Muslims) and the people of rebellion (rebels) inherit from each other, because the Abode of Islam is the abode of rulings, so by difference of kingdom and benefit the abode does not change among Muslims because the rulings of Islam unite them. As for the Abode of War, it is not an abode of rulings but rather an abode of force, and by difference of religions the abode differs among them. And difference of abodes cuts off inheritance. Similarly, if they come out to us with safe-conduct, meaning the people of the two differing territories among the people of war, even if they are Musta'min, then each one of them in legal effect is as if he were in the place from which he came out with safe-conduct, contrary to when they become Dhimmis for the people of Islam: they inherit among themselves after that, just as if they became Muslim, for inheritance runs after death among them. And if their protection differed in the state of disbelief, we come to the cases: A Dhimmi died and left heirs in the Abode of War, his property is booty, whether the heirs are in the Abode of War or in the Abode of Islam as Mu'ahidin. And if a Jew died and left a Jewish son in the Abode of Islam who pays the jizya, and a son of his in the Abode of War, then all the property goes to the Jewish son who pays the jizya in the Abode of Islam. And if a Jew from the people of war, while a Musta'min in the Abode of Islam, died and left a Musta'min son in the Abode of Islam, a Dhimmi son, a Harbi son, and a Muslim son, then the property, according to the saying of the people of Iraq, goes to the Mu'ahid and the Harbi, because the Mu'ahid is in the position of a Harbi according to them, so the Harbi inherits from him and whoever is like him, i.e., the Mu'ahid. And if a Jew from the people of the Dhimma died and left a Jewish son and a Christian son, then according to the one who makes the people of the Dhimma inherit from each other even if their sects differ, the property is half and half between them. And according to the one who says that the Jews are a religion and the Christians are a religion, the property goes to the Jewish son.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage discusses inheritance among non-Muslims according to the Hanafi school. It begins with an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding inheritance from invalid marriages: specifically, marriage during the 'idda (waiting period) and marriage without witnesses. Zufar holds that neither marriage gives inheritance. Abu Hanifa holds that both marriages are valid for inheritance if the couple later becomes Muslim and continues the marriage. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: marriage without witnesses is valid for inheritance, but marriage during 'idda is not. The text then clarifies a consensus: a Harbi (enemy combatant) does not inherit from a Dhimmi (protected non-Muslim), regardless of whether the Harbi is a Musta'min (one with safe-conduct) in Dar al-Islam or resides in Dar al-Harb. Conversely, Dhimmis inherit from each other even if they belong to different religious sects (e.g., Jew and Christian), because all kufr is considered one milla (religious community) according to the Companions. The text then explains inheritance among people of Dar al-Harb: they inherit from each other if they belong to the same territory (dar wahida), but not if they belong to different territories. The difference of territories is defined by being separate sovereign states where each side considers it permissible to kill the other, even if they share the same religion. This contrasts with Muslims: even rebels (ahl al-baghy) inherit from each other because Dar al-Islam is governed by Islamic law that unites them. After establishing these principles, the text presents several cases. First: a Dhimmi dies leaving heirs in Dar al-Harb—his property becomes fay' (booty) for the Muslim state, regardless of where the heirs are. Second: a Jew dies leaving one son in Dar al-Islam (who pays jizya) and one son in Dar al-Harb—the son in Dar al-Islam takes all. Third: a Jew from Dar al-Harb becomes a Musta'min in Dar al-Islam and dies leaving four sons: a Musta'min son, a Dhimmi son, a Harbi son, and a Muslim son. According to the Iraqi scholars, the property is divided between the Mu'ahid (i.e., the Dhimmi) and the Harbi, because the Mu'ahid is treated like a Harbi in this context. Fourth: a Dhimmi Jew dies leaving a Jewish son and a Christian son. Two opinions are cited: if all Dhimmis inherit from each other regardless of sect, the property is divided equally; if Jews and Christians are considered separate millas, the Jewish son takes all. This passage illustrates the application of the principle that inheritance among non-Muslims is based on territorial and religious affiliation, with specific disagreements among early Hanafi authorities.

Key Terms
حربي (ḥarbī): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Muslims, residing in Dar al-Harb.
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim living under Muslim protection, paying jizya.
مستأمن (mustaʾmin): A non-Muslim from Dar al-Harb who enters Dar al-Islam with a safe-conduct (amān).
ملة (milla): Religion or religious community. Used here to decide whether different non-Muslim sects are one or separate for inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a Dhimmi Jew dies leaving a Jewish son and a Christian son, which of the following is a correct statement about the inheritance?
A) The Jewish son takes all because Jews and Christians are separate millas.
B) The Christian son takes all.
C) The property is divided equally between them according to the milla principle.
D) Neither inherits because they are different religions.

Key Takeaway
Inheritance among non-Muslims depends on their status (Harbi/Dhimmi) and the principle that all kufr is one milla for Dhimmis.

Faraid — Inheritance

Territorial Rules of Inheritance among Non-Muslims
Territorial rules and case applications for non-Muslim inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of territorial inheritance to determine who inherits when non-Muslims from different territories or statuses die.

Core Idea
Harbi from same territory inherit; from different territories do not; Dhimmis inherit regardless of territory under certain conditions.

This passage expands on the territorial dimension of inheritance among non-Muslims. It explains that Harbis inherit from each other only if they belong to the same territory (dar wahida), and the difference of territories cuts off inheritance. It then presents several cases illustrating how these rules apply to Dhimmis, Musta'min, and mixed scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Understand the territorial rule for Harbi
Abu Hanifa and his companions rule that people of war inherit from each other if they are from one territory. If territories differ, they do not inherit.
The interpretation of 'different territories' is that there are two separate kingdoms where each considers killing the other permissible, even if they share the same religion.
2
Contrast with Muslims
Muslims (including rebels) inherit from each other because Dar al-Islam is a single abode governed by Islamic rulings, regardless of political differences.
This highlights the unifying effect of Islamic law.
3
Apply to Musta'min
If people from different territories come out with safe-conduct, each is considered as if still in his original territory for inheritance purposes.
This prevents inheritance across enemy lines even when physically present in Dar al-Islam.
4
Work through the cases
The text gives four cases: (1) Dhimmi with heirs in Dar al-Harb, (2) Jew with sons in different dars, (3) Jew Musta'min with four sons of different statuses, (4) Dhimmi Jew with Jewish and Christian sons.
Apply the rules to predict the outcome before reading the text's decision.

Source Text

بين المسلمين بحال نحو نكاح المحارم بسبب أو رضاع ونكاح المطلقة قبل التزوج بزوج آخر . واختلفوا في التوريث بحكم النكاح في العدة والنكاح بغير شهود قال زفر : لا يتوارثون. وقال أبو حنيفة : يتوارثون. وقال أبو يوسف : يتوارثون في النكاح بغير شهود ولا يتوارثون بالنكاح في العدة، وهذا بناء على اختلافهم في تقريرهم على هذه الانكحة إذا أسلموا وقد بينا ذلك في النكاح، ولا خلاف بين أصحابنا أن الكافر الحربي لا يرث الذمي سواء كان الحربي مستأمناً في دارنا أو في دار الحرب. وأهل الذمة يرث بعضهم بعضاً وإن اختلفت صورة مللهم عند عامة الصحابة لأن الكفر كله ملة واحدة فجعلوا اليهود والنصارى ملة وكان أبو حنيفة وأصحابه Key Terms
اختلاف الدارين (ikhtilāf al-dārayn): Difference of territories, which cuts off inheritance among non-Muslims of war.
فيء (fay'): Booty taken from non-Muslims without war, here referring to property of a Dhimmi with heirs in Dar al-Harb becoming state property.
معاهد (muʿāhid): A non-Muslim with a covenant, often synonymous with Dhimmi.
أهل العراق (ahl al-ʿIrāq): The Iraqi school of Hanafi scholars, who held that a Mu'ahid is like a Harbi in some inheritance contexts.

According to the Hanafi school, if a Dhimmi Jew dies leaving a Jewish son in Dar al-Islam and a Jewish son in Dar al-Harb, who inherits?
A) Both sons inherit equally.
B) The son in Dar al-Islam inherits all.
C) The son in Dar al-Harb inherits all.
D) Neither inherits; the property is fay'.

Key Takeaway
Inheritance among non-Muslims is heavily influenced by territorial affiliation: same territory allows inheritance, different territory cuts it off, except for Dhimmis under certain conditions.

Three Principles of Majusi Inheritance and Their Application
Three principles of Majusi inheritance and their applied cases with intra-Hanafi debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three principles of Majusi inheritance according to the Hanafi school and apply them to cases involving mahram marriages, including identifying which heirs inherit and which are excluded.

Core Idea
Majus inherit by valid marriage or by lineage from any marriage; the test of a valid marriage is whether it would continue upon Islam.

Inheritance among Majus (Zoroastrians) is governed by three unique principles that emphasize lineage over invalid marriages. This lesson unpacks those principles and applies them to complex family structures where marriage to mahrams is common. A key intra-Hanafi dispute over the validity of such marriages also affects the inheritance outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three principles
Read the first part of the passage that lists the three principles. Note that inheritance is only through valid marriage or through lineage from invalid marriage.
Principle 1: Only valid marriage generates inheritance between spouses. Principle 2: Lineage (nasab) is established even from invalid marriage, and inheritance occurs through that lineage. Principle 3: A person inheriting through multiple causes inherits through all unless one excludes the other.
2
Apply the criterion for a valid marriage
The criterion is whether the couple would continue the marriage if they became Muslim. If they would continue, it is valid; otherwise, invalid. Consider this for mahram marriages among Majus.
This criterion is specific to Majus and does not apply to Muslims.
3
Distinguish inheritance through marriage vs. lineage
In the case of a Majus who marries a mahram, the marriage is invalid for inheritance, so the spouse does not inherit. However, any children born from that union inherit through lineage.
The passage states: 'they inherit through that lineage, even though they do not inherit through that marriage.'
4
Handle multiple causes of inheritance
If a person relates to the deceased through multiple causes (e.g., as both a son and a brother), they inherit through all unless one cause excludes the other. The excluding cause is the one that gives the stronger claim.
The text mentions this principle but does not elaborate fully. Refer to standard Hanafi faraid for details on hajb.
5
Resolve the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider mahram marriages among Majus invalid. There is disagreement about Abu Hanifa's view: the Iraqi mashayikh say he deems them invalid, while the Transoxanians say he deems them valid. The mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under the Sahibayn, no inheritance between mahram spouses; under the Transoxanian view of Abu Hanifa, inheritance would occur.

وأما ميراث المجوسي فيما بينهم يبنى على أصول ثلاثة : أحدها أنهم لا يتوارثون بالأنكحة الفاسدة فيما بينهم وإنما يتوارثون بالأنكحة الصحيحة، والفاصل أن كل نكاح لو أسلما تركا على ذلك فهو نكاح صحيح، ولو أسلما لم يتركا فهو نكاح فاسد والثاني أن النسب فيما بينهم يثبت بالأنكحة الفاسدة ويتوارثون فيما بينهم بذلك النسب وإن كانوا لا يتوارثون بذلك النكاح الثالث أن كل من يدلي إلى الميت بسببين أو ثلاثة فإنه يرث بجميع ذلك إلا إذا كان أحد السببين يحجب الآخر فحينئذ يرث بالحاجب وقد قدمناه . ولو تزوج بأمه أو بابنته أو بأخته فمات أحدهما لا يرث الآخر، وهذا الجواب على أصل أبي يوسف ومحمد ظاهر لأن نكاح المحارم فيما بينهم فاسد عندنا وإن كانوا يدينون جوازه لا ولهذا قالا : إذا طلبت النفقة من القاضي لا يرفض النفقة، وإذا دخل بها سقط إحصائه حتى ! يحد قاذفة لو قذفه انسان بعدما أسلم ولو طلب أحدهما التفريق فالقاضي يفرق وذلك لا يشكل على قول أبي حنيفة على ما هو مختار مشايخ العراق وإن كان نكاح المحارم فاسداً عند أبي حنيفة فاستدلوا لذلك بفصل عدم حرمان الإرث بينهما وإنما يشكل على قول مشايخ ما وراء النهر فإنهم يقولون بأن نكاح المحارم فيما بينهم جائز على قول أبي حنيفة، ويقولون لو لم يكن النكاح جائزاً عنده لما فرض لها النفقة، ويستدلون أيضاً بما لو دخل بها بعد النكاح أنه لا يسقط إحصانه عنده . والعذر لمشايخ العراق في فصل النفقة أن النفقة كما تحجب بسبب النكاح فتحجب بسبب الاحتباس فإن ثمة لم يكن نكاح ، وإن كان نكاحاً فاسداً يؤخذ النفقة بسبب الاحتباس لا بسبب النكاح وبقاء الاحتباس بعد الدخول لا يدل على صحة النكاح عند أبي حنيفة لا محالة ألا : من تزوج امرأة ودخل بها وكان نظر إلى فرج أمها أو ابنتها بشهوة أن إحصانه لا يسقط وإن كان نكاحاً فاسداً عند أبي حنيفة . والعذر لمشايخنا رحمهم الله عن فصل الإرث فإنه لا يجري الإرث فيما بينهم وإن كانوا يدينون جواز النكاح واعتبر ديانتهم في حق جواز النكاح في حق الإرث فيما بين المحارم أن يقول إن ديانتهم إنما تعتبر بجواز النكاح لأن جواز نكاح المحارم قد كان في شريعة آدم عليه السلام .
