وفي الذخيرة : ثم فرقوا بين نكاح المحارم فيما بينهم وبين النسب الثابت في النكاح فقالوا : إذا تزوج المجوسي بمحارمة ثم مات أحدهما لا يرثه الباقي، فأما إذا حدث بينهما ولد فإنه يثبت النسب ويتوارثون بذلك النسب فيما بينهم. تزوج مجوسي فولدت منه ابناً وبنتاً ثم مات المجوسي فقد مات عن ابن وبنت وزوجة فيقسم المال بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين يورثون بالنسب ويسقط اعتبار النكاح لأنه فاسد يثبت به النسب فيما بينهم ولا يتوارثون به فلهذا قال يسقط اعتبار النكاح ويرثون بالنسب. ولو مات الابن بعد ذلك فقد مات أخت لأب وأم وعن أخت لأب هي أمه فللأخت لأب السدس بحكم الأموية والسدس بحكم الأختية والنصف للأخت لأب وأم والباقي للعصبة إن كانت وإلا فيرد عليهما وعلى سهامهما . ولو لم يمت الابن بعد موت المجوسي ولكن ماتت البنت التي هي زوجته فقد عن ابن هو أخوها لأبيها وعن بنت هي أختها لأبيها ويرثون بالبنوة والبنتية ويقسم المال بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين. ولو لم تمت الابنة التي هي زوج المجوسي ولكن ماتت الابنة الأخرى فقد ماتت عن أخ لأب وأم عن أخت لأب وأم وعن أخت لأب هي أمها فيكون للأم السدس والباقي لأخ للأب وأم فيسقط اعتبار الأختية لأن قرابة والأخت لأب ساقطة الاعتبار لقرابة الأخ لأب وأم . وإنما كان للأم السدس في هذه الصورة لأن للميت أخاً وأختاً والأخت من أهل الاستحقاق إلا أنها صارت محجوبة بهذا السبب العارض ولهذا اسقط فرض الأم عن ماتت
As for the inheritance of the Majus among themselves, it is based on three principles: First, they do not inherit through invalid marriages among themselves, but they inherit through valid marriages. The criterion is that every marriage which, if they became Muslim, they would continue with it is a valid marriage, and if they became Muslim they would not continue with it is an invalid marriage. Second, lineage among them is established through invalid marriages, and they inherit among themselves through that lineage, even though they do not inherit through that marriage. Third, whoever connects to the deceased through two or three causes inherits through all of them, except if one of the two causes excludes the other, then he inherits through the excluding cause, and we have already mentioned this. And if one marries his mother, or his daughter, or his sister, and one of them dies, the other does not inherit. This answer is apparently based on the principle of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, because marriage to mahrams among them is invalid according to us, even if they believe in its permissibility. Hence they said: if she demands maintenance from the judge, he does not refuse maintenance; and if he consummates with her, his ihsan (chastity) is not removed, so that his slanderer would be subject to hadd if someone slandered him after he became Muslim; and if one of them seeks separation, the judge separates them. And this does not pose a problem according to the statement of Abu Hanifa as chosen by the mashayikh of Iraq, even though marriage to mahrams is invalid according to Abu Hanifa. They used as evidence the fact that inheritance is not prevented between them. But it poses a problem according to the statement of the mashayikh of Transoxiana, who say that marriage to mahrams among them is permissible according to Abu Hanifa, and they say: if the marriage were not permissible in his view, he would not have prescribed maintenance for her. They also use as evidence that if he consummates after the marriage, his ihsan is not removed according to him. The excuse of the mashayikh of Iraq regarding the issue of maintenance is that maintenance, just as it is established due to marriage, is also established due to being confined (ihtibas). For here there is no marriage, and if it were an invalid marriage, maintenance is taken due to confinement, not due to marriage. And the continuation of confinement after consummation does not necessarily indicate the validity of the marriage according to Abu Hanifa. Indeed, if a man marries a woman and consummates, and he had looked at the private part of her mother or her daughter with desire, his ihsan is not removed, even if the marriage is invalid according to Abu Hanifa. And the excuse of our mashayikh (may Allah have mercy on them) regarding the issue of inheritance is that inheritance does not occur among them even if they believe in the permissibility of the marriage, and their belief is considered regarding the permissibility of marriage, but regarding inheritance among mahrams, it may be said that their belief is only considered regarding the permissibility of marriage because the permissibility of marrying mahrams existed in the law of Adam (peace be upon him). 
[Corrupted line: 'عن تری أن هذا بابنة له' - meaning unclear]
And in al-Dhakhira: Then they distinguished between marriage to mahrams among them and the lineage established through marriage, and said: If a Majus marries a mahram, then one of them dies, the surviving spouse does not inherit. But if a child is born between them, lineage is established and they inherit through that lineage among themselves. A Majus married and she gave birth to a son and a daughter, then the Majus died, leaving a son, a daughter, and a wife. The property is divided among them with the male taking the share of two females; they inherit through lineage, and the marriage is disregarded because it is invalid, lineage is established through it among them, and they do not inherit through it. Hence it is said: the marriage is disregarded and they inherit through lineage. And if the son died after that, he dies leaving a sister (paternal and maternal) and a sister (paternal) who is his mother. So the sister (paternal) gets one-sixth by virtue of motherhood and one-sixth by virtue of sororal relation, and half goes to the sister (paternal and maternal), and the remainder goes to the asaba if any, otherwise it is returned to them and to their shares. And if the son did not die after the death of the Majus, but the daughter who is his wife died, then she leaves a son who is her brother (paternal), and a daughter who is her sister (paternal), and they inherit by virtue of sonship and daughter-ship, and the property is divided among them with the male taking the share of two females. And if the daughter who is the wife of the Majus did not die, but the other daughter died, then she dies leaving a brother (paternal and maternal), a sister (paternal and maternal), and a sister (paternal) who is her mother. So the mother gets one-sixth, and the remainder goes to the brother (paternal and maternal), and the sororal relation is disregarded because the relationship of the sister (paternal) is excluded due to the relationship of the brother (paternal and maternal). The mother is given one-sixth in this scenario because the deceased has a brother and a sister, and the sister is eligible, but she is excluded by this incidental cause, and hence the mother's prescribed share was reduced from [text incomplete]

Commentary
This passage explains the inheritance rules for Majus (Zoroastrians) based on three foundational principles. The first principle distinguishes between valid and invalid marriages: any marriage that the couple would continue if they became Muslim is considered valid for inheritance; otherwise it is invalid. Second, lineage (nasab) is established even through invalid marriages, and inheritance can occur through that lineage, even though the marriage itself does not entitle inheritance. Third, a heir who relates to the deceased through multiple causes inherits through all of them unless one cause excludes another, in which case he inherits through the excluding cause. The text then applies these principles to cases involving marriage to mahrams (close relatives). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, such marriages are invalid, so no inheritance occurs between spouses. The text then records a dispute between the mashayikh of Iraq and those of Transoxiana regarding Abu Hanifa's view on the validity of mahram marriages among Majus. The Iraqis hold that Abu Hanifa considers them invalid, while the Transoxanians hold that he considers them valid. Each side presents evidence: the Iraqis point to the fact that no inheritance occurs, while the Transoxanians use the rulings on maintenance and ihsan. The text provides excuses for both positions. Further cases from al-Dhakhira illustrate: if a Majus marries a mahram and has children, inheritance is through lineage, not marriage. The passage then works through several inheritance scenarios involving a Majus who dies leaving a wife (who is also his daughter or sister) and children, calculating shares based on the principle that lineage takes precedence and the marriage is disregarded. For example, if a Majus dies leaving a son, daughter, and wife, the property is divided among the son and daughter according to the rule 'for the male the share of two females', and the wife does not inherit because the marriage is invalid. If the son later dies, leaving a sister (who is also his mother) and another sister, shares are calculated accordingly. The text explains how certain heirs are excluded due to stronger relationships. The passage ends mid-sentence on page 389, possibly continuing on the next page.

Key Terms
مجوسي (majūsī): A Zoroastrian, a follower of the Magian religion. The text discusses their inheritance rules.
نكاح فاسد (nikāḥ fāsid): An invalid marriage in Islamic law. For Majus, the criterion is whether they would continue the marriage if they became Muslim.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or parentage. For Majus, lineage is established even through invalid marriages.
احتباس (iḥtibās): Confinement or retention of the woman in the husband's home, which can be a basis for maintenance even if the marriage is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the first principle of Majusi inheritance, how is a valid marriage distinguished from an invalid one?
A) By whether the marriage was conducted with witnesses.
B) By whether the marriage is between mahrams or not.
C) By whether the couple would continue the marriage if they became Muslim.
D) By whether the marriage was consummated.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, Majus inherit through valid marriage or lineage from any marriage, and the test for a valid marriage is whether the couple would continue it upon embracing Islam.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Zoroastrian Mahram Marriage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effects of invalid Zoroastrian mahram marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the differences between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the legal effects of Zoroastrian mahram marriage, and identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Core Idea
Zoroastrian mahram marriage is invalid in Hanafi fiqh, but the Sahibayn recognize certain practical effects while Abu Hanifa limits them.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. The core issue is not whether the marriage is valid (all agree it is fasid), but what consequences follow. The Sahibayn grant maintenance, remove ihsan, and allow judicial separation, while Abu Hanifa (according to Iraqi scholars) restricts these effects. Transoxianan scholars attribute a different view to Abu Hanifa, which is rejected by the later tradition. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists balance principle with practical reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common ground
All Hanafi scholars agree that Zoroastrian marriages to mahrams are fasid (invalid). This is the starting point for all discussions.
Invalidity is based on clear Quranic prohibition of incest.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and their proofs
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that certain effects follow: the wife can seek maintenance, consummation removes ihsan, and the judge can separate the couple. Their proof is that Zoroastrians consider it permissible, so to avoid hardship, these effects are recognized.
They rely on the practical circumstance of the couple's belief.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position according to Iraqi scholars
Abu Hanifa, as understood by the Iraqi scholars, denies these effects because the marriage is fundamentally invalid. Maintenance is due only to confinement (ihtibas), not marriage; removal of ihsan is not automatic; and separation is not required.
The key distinction: maintenance is tied to de facto cohabitation, not the invalid marriage contract.
4
Analyze the Transoxianan counter-claim
Some Transoxianan scholars attribute to Abu Hanifa the view that the marriage is valid for Zoroastrians, arguing that he would not have imposed maintenance otherwise and that he does not remove ihsan. The Iraqi rebuttal is that maintenance is from confinement, and there are analogous cases where an invalid marriage does not remove ihsan.
This attribution is rejected by the majority of later Hanafi authorities.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the Iraqi scholars: the marriage is invalid, maintenance may be due from confinement, removal of ihsan is not automatic, and the judge may separate the couple if requested. Inheritance never flows through the marriage.
This is the view of al-Marghinani and the later tradition.

كتاب الفرائض
أصل أبي يوسف ومحمد ظاهر لأن نكاح المحارم فيما بينهم فاسد عندنا وإن كانوا يدينون جوازه لا ولهذا قالا : إذا طلبت النفقة من القاضي لا يرفض النفقة، وإذا دخل بها سقط إحصائه حتى ! يحد قاذفة لو قذفه انسان بعدما أسلم ولو طلب أحدهما التفريق فالقاضي يفرق وذلك لا يشكل على قول أبي حنيفة على ما هو مختار مشايخ العراق وإن كان نكاح المحارم فاسداً عند أبي حنيفة فاستدلوا لذلك بفصل عدم حرمان الإرث بينهما وإنما يشكل على قول مشايخ ما وراء النهر فإنهم يقولون بأن نكاح المحارم فيما بينهم جائز على قول أبي حنيفة، ويقولون لو لم يكن النكاح جائزاً عنده لما فرض لها النفقة، ويستدلون أيضاً بما لو دخل بها بعد النكاح أنه لا يسقط إحصانه عنده . والعذر لمشايخ العراق في فصل النفقة أن النفقة كما تحجب بسبب النكاح فتحجب بسبب الاحتباس فإن ثمة لم يكن نكاح ، وإن كان نكاحاً فاسداً يؤخذ النفقة بسبب الاحتباس لا بسبب النكاح وبقاء الاحتباس بعد الدخول لا يدل على صحة النكاح عند أبي حنيفة لا محالة ألا : من تزوج امرأة ودخل بها وكان نظر إلى فرج أمها أو ابنتها بشهوة أن إحصانه لا يسقط وإن كان نكاحاً فاسداً عند أبي حنيفة . والعذر لمشايخنا رحمهم الله عن فصل الإرث فإنه لا يجري الإرث فيما بينهم وإن كانوا يدينون جواز النكاح واعتبر ديانتهم في حق جواز النكاح في حق الإرث فيما بين المحارم أن يقول إن ديانتهم إنما تعتبر بجواز النكاح لأن جواز نكاح المحارم قد كان في شريعة آدم عليه السلام .

وفي الذخيرة : ثم فرقوا بين نكاح المحارم فيما بينهم وبين النسب الثابت في النكاح فقالوا : إذا تزوج المجوسي بمحارمة ثم مات أحدهما لا يرثه الباقي، فأما إذا حدث بينهما ولد فإنه يثبت النسب ويتوارثون بذلك النسب فيما بينهم. تزوج مجوسي فولدت منه ابناً وبنتاً ثم مات المجوسي فقد مات عن ابن وبنت وزوجة فيقسم المال بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين يورثون بالنسب ويسقط اعتبار النكاح لأنه فاسد يثبت به النسب فيما بينهم ولا يتوارثون به فلهذا قال يسقط اعتبار النكاح ويرثون بالنسب. ولو مات الابن بعد ذلك فقد مات أخت لأب وأم وعن أخت لأب هي أمه فللأخت لأب السدس بحكم الأموية والسدس بحكم الأختية والنصف للأخت لأب وأم والباقي للعصبة إن كانت وإلا فيرد عليهما وعلى سهامهما . ولو لم يمت الابن بعد موت المجوسي ولكن ماتت البنت التي هي زوجته فقد عن ابن هو أخوها لأبيها وعن بنت هي أختها لأبيها ويرثون بالبنوة والبنتية ويقسم المال بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين. ولو لم تمت الابنة التي هي زوج المجوسي ولكن ماتت الابنة الأخرى فقد ماتت عن أخ لأب وأم عن أخت لأب وأم وعن أخت لأب هي أمها فيكون للأم السدس والباقي لأخ للأب وأم فيسقط اعتبار الأختية لأن قرابة والأخت لأب ساقطة الاعتبار لقرابة الأخ لأب وأم . وإنما كان للأم السدس في هذه الصورة لأن للميت أخاً وأختاً والأخت من أهل الاستحقاق إلا أنها صارت محجوبة بهذا السبب العارض ولهذا اسقط فرض الأم عن الثلث إلى السدس.
Book of Inheritances. The position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is manifest because marriage to mahrams among them is invalid in our school, even though they believe it permissible. Therefore they said: When she seeks maintenance from the judge, he does not refuse maintenance, and when he consummates, his ihsan is removed so that if someone slanders him after he converts, the slanderer is hadded. And if one of them seeks separation, the judge separates them, and that is not problematic according to Abu Hanifa's opinion as chosen by the Iraqi scholars. Even though marriage to mahrams is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, they argued using the case of non-deprivation of inheritance between them. It is only problematic according to the scholars of Transoxiana, who say that marriage to mahrams among them is permissible according to Abu Hanifa, and they say: if the marriage were not permissible in his view, he would not have imposed maintenance for her. They also argue using the case that if he consummates after marriage, his ihsan is not removed in his view. The excuse of the Iraqi scholars regarding maintenance is that maintenance, just as it is established due to marriage, is also established due to confinement [ihtibas]. For there is no marriage, and if it is an invalid marriage, maintenance is taken due to confinement, not due to marriage. The continuation of confinement after consummation does not indicate the validity of marriage according to Abu Hanifa inevitably. Indeed: if one marries a woman and consummates, and had looked at the private part of her mother or daughter with desire, his ihsan is not removed, even if the marriage is invalid according to Abu Hanifa. The excuse of our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, regarding inheritance: inheritance does not flow between them even if they believe the marriage permissible. And they considered their belief regarding the permissibility of marriage in the matter of inheritance among mahrams that they say: their belief is only considered for the permissibility of marriage, because the permissibility of marriage to mahrams existed in the law of Adam, peace be upon him. ... [unclear text] ... In al-Dhakhirah: Then they distinguished between marriage to mahrams among themselves and lineage established through marriage. They said: When a Zoroastrian marries his mahram, then one of them dies, the other does not inherit from him. But if a child occurs between them, lineage is established, and they inherit through that lineage among themselves. A Zoroastrian married and had from him a son and a daughter, then the Zoroastrian died, leaving a son, a daughter, and a wife. The property is divided among them with the male getting the share of two females. They inherit through lineage, and consideration of marriage is dropped because it is invalid. Lineage is established through it among them, but they do not inherit through it. Therefore he said: consideration of marriage is dropped, and they inherit through lineage. And if the son died after that, he left a sister for [paternal] father and mother, and a sister for [paternal] father who is his mother. So for the sister for [paternal] father, one-sixth by virtue of motherhood, and one-sixth by virtue of sisterhood, and half for the sister for [paternal] father and mother, and the remainder goes to the agnates if any, otherwise it is returned to them upon their shares. And if the son did not die after the death of the Zoroastrian, but the daughter who is his wife died, he left a son who is her brother by father, and a daughter who is her sister by father. They inherit by sonship and daughterhood, and the property is divided among them with the male getting the share of two females. And if the daughter who is the Zoroastrian's wife did not die, but the other daughter died, she left a brother for father and mother, a sister for father and mother, and a sister for father who is her mother. So for the mother one-sixth, and the remainder for the brother for father and mother. Consideration of sisterhood is dropped because the kinship of the sister for father is dropped due to the kinship of the brother for father and mother. The mother gets one-sixth in this case because the deceased has a brother and a sister, and the sister is entitled but becomes prevented by this accidental cause, hence the mother's share is reduced from one-third to one-sixth.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of Zoroastrian (Magian) marriages to close relatives (mahram). The Hanafi school holds such marriages as invalid (fasid) because they violate the prohibition of incest. However, there is a difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa and his students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), as well as between scholars of Iraq and Transoxiana, regarding certain practical consequences. The Sahibayn argue that since Zoroastrians believe these marriages are permissible, certain effects follow: if the wife seeks maintenance, the judge grants it; if the husband consummates, he loses his status of ihsan (which would otherwise protect him from hadd for slander); and if one party seeks divorce, the judge separates them. Abu Hanifa, according to the Iraqi scholars, also considers the marriage invalid but disagrees on some of these points. The Transoxianan scholars attribute to Abu Hanifa the view that such marriages are actually valid for Zoroastrians, citing that he would not have imposed maintenance otherwise, and that consummation does not remove ihsan. The Iraqi scholars rebut by explaining that maintenance is due to confinement (ihtibas), not marriage, and that the removal of ihsan does not necessarily indicate validity. On inheritance, all agree that the invalid marriage does not create inheritable ties; inheritance flows only through blood lineage (nasab) from children born of such unions. The passage then provides examples from al-Dhakhirah, illustrating how inheritance is calculated among Zoroastrians who have married mahrams, always relying on lineage rather than marriage. For instance, if a Zoroastrian dies leaving a wife (who is also his mahram), a son, and a daughter, the wife does not inherit due to the invalid marriage; the estate goes to the son and daughter with the son taking double the daughter's share. Subsequent deaths among the heirs are handled according to standard inheritance rules based on blood relationships.

Key Terms
المحارم (al-mahārim): Close blood relatives whom marriage is permanently prohibited (e.g., mother, daughter, sister).
الإحصان (al-iḥṣān): The status of being married and having consummated, which protects one from certain hadd punishments (e.g., slander) and raises the punishment for zina to stoning.
الاحتباس (al-iḥtibās): Confinement or detention; here refers to the fact that the woman remains with the man under a de facto relationship, which can give rise to maintenance obligations even if the marriage is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an effect that the Sahibayn attribute to a Zoroastrian mahram marriage?
A) The wife can seek maintenance from the judge.
B) The husband's ihsan is removed upon consummation.
C) The couple inherits from each other upon death.
D) The judge can separate them if either party requests.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school unanimously holds Zoroastrian mahram marriage invalid, but the Sahibayn recognize some pragmatic effects while Abu Hanifa (Iraqi view) limits them; the relied-upon position is the Iraqi one.

Inheritance of a Zoroastrian with Invalid Marriages
Inheritance calculation for a Zoroastrian with multiple marriages and deaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate inheritance shares in a complex family tree involving multiple invalid marriages, correctly applying hajb and 'usubah rules.

Core Idea
Inheritance is based on blood lineage, not invalid marriages; distant heirs are excluded by closer ones.

This faraid case from al-Dhakhirah presents a complex family tree resulting from invalid Zoroastrian marriages between mahrams. Despite the marital irregularities, inheritance is determined solely by blood relationships (nasab). We will work through the initial scenario and several subsequent deaths to see how shares shift when different heirs predecease the original deceased.

Guided Path
1
Identify the family tree
Map out the relationships: a Zoroastrian man marries his mother, has a son and a daughter. Then he divorces her and marries that daughter, who bears him a granddaughter. He dies leaving his mother, his son (from the first marriage), his daughter (his sister/wife from first marriage), and his granddaughter.
The mother is also his ex-wife, but inheritance treats her as mother.
2
Apply hajb hirman (total exclusion)
Determine which heirs are excluded. The granddaughter (bint al-ibn) is totally excluded by the son (her uncle) because the son is a closer agnatic heir.
Other heirs (mother, son, daughter) are not excluded.
3
Assign shares to fard heirs
The mother gets her Quranic share of 1/6 as a mother. There is no other fard heir; the son and daughter inherit as 'asabah.
The daughter is also the man's sister, but she inherits as a daughter from the father? Actually she is his daughter from his mother. Yes, she is his daughter.
4
Distribute the residue to 'asabah
After the mother's 1/6, the residue (5/6) goes to the son and daughter as agnatic heirs, with the son taking double the daughter's share (li al-dhakari mithlu hazz al-unthayayn).
The son and daughter are full siblings (same father and mother).
5
Calculate final shares
Base = 6. Mother = 1. Residue = 5. Son : Daughter = 2:1, so son gets 10/3? Actually 5/6 split 2:1 gives son 2/3 of 5/6 = 10/18 = 5/9, daughter 1/3 of 5/6 = 5/18. After adjusting base to 18: mother 3/18, son 10/18, daughter 5/18.
No awl or radd needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة: مجوسي تزوج بأمه فولدت بنتاً وابناً ثم فارقها وتزوج ابنته فولدت له ابنة ثم مات المجوسي فقط مات عن أم وابن وابنة بنت ابن فيكون للأم السدس باعتبار الأمومة والباقي بين الابن والبنت للذكر مثل خط الأنثيين ولا شيء لبنت الابن، فإن مات الابن بعد فإنما مات عن زوجة هي جدته أم أبيه وهي أمة وعن أخت لأمة وأبيه فلا شيء للأم بالزوجية ولا بكونها جدة لأن الجدة لا ترث مع الأم ولكن لها السدس بالأمومة والابنة النصف بالبينة ولا شيء لها بالأختية لأم، فإن لم يمت الابن ولكن ماتت الابنة الكبرى فقد ماتت عن أم هي جدتها أم أبيها وعن أخ لأب وأم وعن ابنة هي أختها لأمها فللأم السدس بالأمومة لأن معها أخاً لأم وأختاً وهما يردان الأم من ثلث إلى السدس وللابنة السدس بالأختية لأم والباقي للأخ لأب وأم بالعصوبة، فإن كانت الابنة التي ماتت هي الصغرى فقد ماتت عن أم وعن جدتها لأبيها وعن أبيها وعن عمة هي أختها لأبيها وعن ابن هو أخوها لأمها فللأم السدس والباقي للأب لأن الإخوة والاخوات لا يرثون مع الأب شيئاً. ولو لم تمت الابنة ولكن ماتت الأم فإنما ماتت عمن هو زوجها وهو ابن ابنها وعن ابنة ابن هي أختها فلا شيء للابن بالزوجية ولكن المال بين الابن والأنثى للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين فلا شيء للذكر باعتبار أنه ابن ابن ولا الأنثى باعتبار أنها ابنة الابن.
In al-Dhakhirah: A Zoroastrian married his mother, and she gave birth to a daughter and a son. Then he separated from her and married his daughter, and she gave birth to a daughter for him. Then the Zoroastrian died only, leaving a mother, a son, and a daughter of the son (granddaughter). So for the mother one-sixth by virtue of motherhood, and the remainder between the son and the daughter [who is his sister?] with the male getting the share of two females, and nothing for the granddaughter. Then if the son died after that, then he died leaving a wife who is his grandmother (his father's mother) and she is his mother, and a sister for mother and father. So for the mother there is nothing by virtue of marriage nor by being a grandmother, because the grandmother does not inherit with the mother, but she gets one-sixth by motherhood, and the daughter gets half by daughterhood, and nothing for her by sisterhood for mother. Then if the son did not die, but the elder daughter died, then she died leaving a mother who is her grandmother (her father's mother), a brother for father and mother, and a daughter who is her sister for mother. So for the mother one-sixth by motherhood because with her is a brother for mother and a sister, and they reduce the mother from one-third to one-sixth. For the daughter one-sixth by sisterhood for mother, and the remainder for the brother for father and mother by 'usubah. Then if the daughter who died is the younger one, then she died leaving a mother, a grandmother for father, a father, a paternal aunt who is her sister for father, and a son who is her brother for mother. So for the mother one-sixth, and the remainder for the father, because brothers and sisters do not inherit anything with the father. And if the daughter did not die, but the mother died, then she died leaving those: her husband who is her grandson, and a granddaughter who is her sister. So for the son [i.e., the husband] nothing by virtue of marriage, but the property between the son and the female with the male getting the share of two females. So nothing for the male by virtue of being a grandson, nor for the female by virtue of being a granddaughter.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This faraid case from al-Dhakhirah involves a complex family tree due to Zoroastrian marriage between mahrams. The original deceased is a Zoroastrian who married his mother, then divorced her and married his daughter. He has a son and a daughter from his mother, and a daughter from his daughter. The case then explores various scenarios of deaths among these heirs. The key principle is that inheritance is based on blood lineage (nasab), not invalid marriage. Hence, the mother gets her Quranic share (1/6) because she is a mother by blood, but she does not inherit as a wife. The son and daughter from the mother get shares as brother and sister. The granddaughter (daughter of the daughter) does not inherit because she is a distant relative (dhawi arham) when closer heirs exist. Subsequent scenarios illustrate how shares change when different heirs die. For example, when the son dies, his heirs include his grandmother (who is also his mother) and sisters, with specific rules about grandmothers being excluded by mothers, and sisterhood versus motherhood. The calculations follow standard Hanafi inheritance rules but applied to this unusual family structure.

Key Terms
الرد (ar-radd): Return of surplus to sharers when there is no residuary heir; not applicable here because there are 'asabah heirs.
حجب (hajb): Exclusion of an heir from inheritance either totally (hirman) or partially (nuqsan).
عصوبة ('usubah): Agnatic heir who takes the residue after fard shares, with males taking double females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because she is not a blood relative.
B) Because she is excluded by the son (her uncle) who is a closer heir.
C) Because she is a female and females do not inherit.
D) Because the marriage to the daughter is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Inheritance is based on blood lineage (nasab); invalid marriages do not confer inheritance rights.

First Distribution: Death of the Zoroastrian
First inheritance calculation after the Zoroastrian's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the inheritance shares when the deceased leaves a mother (who is also wife), two daughters, and a granddaughter, applying hajb and radd.

Core Idea
Only blood relations count; invalid marriages confer no inheritance rights.

This passage from al-Dhakhirah presents a challenging faraid case involving multiple invalid mahram marriages among Zoroastrians. The first scenario is the death of a man who married his own mother and then his daughter. The heirs are tangled, but only certain relationships are recognized for inheritance. We will work through the distribution step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify all living relatives at the first death
List every person who could potentially inherit: the mother (also wife), three daughters (one from mother? actually two from mother and one from daughter), and the granddaughter from the daughter.
The marriage relationships are invalid in Islam, so they confer no inheritance rights.
2
Apply hajb to exclude distant heirs
The granddaughter is a dhawi arham and is excluded by the presence of closer heirs (the daughters). Also, the mother as wife does not inherit because the marriage is invalid.
3
Assign Quranic shares to eligible sharers
The mother (as mother) receives 1/6 because there are children (the daughters). The two daughters collectively receive 2/3 (each 1/3) since there is no son.
The mother's share is reduced from 1/3 to 1/6 due to the presence of children.
4
Calculate the residue and determine its distribution
Add the shares: 1/6 + 4/6 = 5/6, leaving 1/6. If there are any agnates (e.g., a brother), they take the residue. Otherwise, the surplus is returned (radd) to the mother and daughters proportionally.
This case assumes no agnates, so radd applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجوسي تزوج أمه فولدت له ابنتين فتزوج ابنته فولدت له ابنة ثم مات المجوسي فقد مات عن أم هي زوجة وثلاث بنات إحداهن زوجة وبنتان أختان لأم وإحداهن ابنة ابن فلا شيء للزوجة منهن بالزوجية ولا للأختين لأم بالأختية ولا للثالثة بكونها ابنة ابن ولكن الباقي للعصبة إن كانت وإن لم تكن فهو رد على أم والبنات على مقدار حقهن، فإن ماتت بعدها الابنة التي هي زوجته فقد ماتت عن ابنة هي أخت لأب وأم فللابنة النصف والباقي للعصبة، وإن لم تمت هذه ولكن ماتت الابنة السفلى فإن ماتت عن أمها وهي أختها لأبيها وعن أخت أيضاً فيكون للأم السدس بالأمية وللأختين الثلثان بالأختية والباقي للعصبة .
A Zoroastrian married his mother, and she gave birth to two daughters for him. Then he married his daughter, and she gave birth to a daughter for him. Then the Zoroastrian died, leaving a mother who is his wife, and three daughters: one of them is his wife, and two sisters for mother, and one of them is a granddaughter. So nothing for the wife among them by virtue of marriage, nor for the two sisters for mother by sisterhood, nor for the third by being a granddaughter. But the remainder goes to the agnates if any, and if not, then it is a return (radd) to the mother and the daughters in proportion to their shares. Then if after that the daughter who is his wife dies, she leaves a daughter who is a sister for [paternal] father and mother. So for the daughter half, and the remainder for the agnates. And if this one does not die, but the lower daughter dies, then she leaves her mother who is her sister for father, and also a sister. So for the mother one-sixth by motherhood, and for the two sisters two-thirds by sisterhood, and the remainder for the agnates.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is another faraid case from al-Dhakhirah involving a Zoroastrian who marries his mother and then his daughter, creating a complex web of relationships. The heirs at the first death (the Zoroastrian) include: his mother (also his wife), three daughters: one from the mother (also his wife? Actually from the mother: two daughters, and one from the daughter: a granddaughter). The text says 'three daughters: one of them is wife, and two sisters for mother, and one of them is a granddaughter.' This is confusing; likely it means: there is the mother (who is also wife), and there are three daughters: two from the mother (who are also sisters of each other? Actually they are sisters from mother? Wait, the two from the mother are his daughters and also his sisters? That is due to the marriage to mother: the daughters are both his daughters and his sisters (since his mother is also their mother? Actually if he marries his mother and has daughters, those daughters are both his daughters and his sisters? This is the complexity. The inheritance calculation: none of the women inherit due to invalid marriage (the wife) or due to being distant relatives (the granddaughter). The agnates take the residue, but if no agnates, the estate is returned (radd) to the mother and the daughters in proportion to their Quranic shares. The text then gives subcases where a daughter dies, and the new heirs inherit according to standard rules: a daughter gets half, sisters get two-thirds, etc. This illustrates the application of inheritance rules in cases of multiple invalid relationships.

Key Terms
الرد (ar-radd): Return of surplus inheritance to Quranic sharers when there is no residuary heir.

Inheritance When a Single Heir Has Multiple Relationships: The Zoroastrian Marriage Case
Inheritance calculation with multiple relationship ties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi faraid principle that a single heir inherits only once based on the strongest relationship, even when they have multiple legal ties to the deceased.

Core Idea
A single heir inherits only once; multiple relationships do not multiply shares.

This faraid case from al-Dhakhirah presents a challenging inheritance scenario: a Zoroastrian marries his own daughter, and they have two daughters. After the father dies, one of these daughters dies, leaving as heirs her mother (who is also her half-sister) and a full sister. The question arises: does the mother inherit as both mother and sister, taking two shares? The Hanafi ruling clarifies that a single heir cannot inherit through multiple relationships simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
Identify all relationships of each heir
List every relationship each living person has to the deceased. Here, the deceased daughter's mother is also her half-sister (since the mother is the daughter of the deceased Zoroastrian). The other heir is a full sister.
The mother has two distinct relationships: motherhood and sisterhood (half-sister through father).
2
Determine the primary relationship for inheritance
For each heir, determine which relationship gives them a share and whether that share is excluded by another heir. In Hanafi faraid, a person inherits through the relationship that is not blocked and usually through the one that gives the largest share.
As a mother, she gets 1/6 (Quranic share when deceased has children). As a sister, she would be a half-sister and could inherit only if no brother/father/child? Actually full sister gets 1/2 if no children or father. But here mother is not a full sister; she is a half-sister? The text says 'sister for father' (half-sister) but also 'sister for father and mother' (full sister) exists separately. The mother is a half-sister. Half-sisters inherit only when no full siblings or children? But there is a full sister, so half-sister is excluded. So even if she could claim as sister, she'd be excluded. So her only viable share is as mother.
3
Assign shares without duplication
Assign each heir exactly one share based on their primary relationship. The full sister gets 1/2, the mother gets 1/6. The mother does not get an additional share as a sister.
The principle: no double inheritance.
4
Apply hajb if needed
Check if any heir is excluded by a closer one. Here, the full sister is not excluded; the mother as a half-sister would be excluded by the full sister, but she already inherits as mother, so the exclusion is moot.
The text notes that some scholars mistakenly gave the mother an extra 1/6 as sister, but the correct view is the first (mother only 1/6 as mother).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل مجوسي تزوج بابنته فولدت ابنتين فمات المجوسي ثم ماتت إحدى البنتين فإنما ماتت . أم هي أخت لأب وعن أخت لأب وأم أيضاً فقد ذكر بعض المشايخ أن للأم السدس بالآية وللأخت لأب وأم النصف وللأم السدس بالأختية والأول أصح .
A Zoroastrian man married his daughter, and she gave birth to two daughters. Then the Zoroastrian died, then one of the two daughters died. Then she died leaving a mother who is a sister for father, and a sister for father and mother. Some scholars said: for the mother one-sixth by the verse, and for the sister for father and mother half, and for the mother one-sixth by sisterhood, but the first is more correct.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This faraid case from al-Dhakhirah deals with a Zoroastrian who marries his daughter, producing two daughters. After the father dies, one of his daughters from this union dies. The deceased daughter's heirs are: her mother (who is also her sister by father? Actually the mother is the daughter-wife of the deceased Zoroastrian, so she is both mother and half-sister of the deceased daughter? The relationships are confusing: the deceased daughter's mother is also her father's daughter, making her the half-sister of the deceased daughter? This is a consequence of the incestuous marriage. The text mentions two views: some scholars assign the mother 1/6 as mother (by Quranic verse) and the full sister 1/2, and also assign the mother 1/6 as sister? That would double count. The 'first' opinion (the mother gets 1/6 as mother, full sister gets 1/2, and the mother does not get anything as sister) is considered more correct. This illustrates that when a person has multiple relationships to the deceased, they should take only one share, usually the highest or the one that is not excluded. In standard Hanafi inheritance, a mother cannot also inherit as a sister if she is already taking a share as mother, because the sister's share would be blocked by the brother? Actually here there is no brother, so the full sister would get half, but the mother is also a sister, but the sister's share is not given because inheritance is not by all relationships simultaneously; one inherits only once.

Key Terms
بالآية (bil-āyah): By the Quranic verse; here referring to the verse (Q4:11) that gives the mother 1/6 when the deceased has children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because sisters are excluded by mothers.

Key Takeaway

Inheritance of a Captive (Asir) in Hanafi Fiqh
A concise ruling on captive's inheritance from al-Sirajiyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the inheritance status of a Muslim captive and identify the condition that changes it to that of a missing person.

Core Idea
Captivity alone does not affect inheritance; only apostasy alters the captive's status.

Inheritance law often raises questions about individuals in unusual circumstances, such as prisoners of war. The Hanafi school provides clear guidance: as long as a captive remains a Muslim, he is treated exactly like any other Muslim heir. However, if he abandons Islam, his status shifts to that of a missing person (mafqud), with the estate held until his fate is determined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal rule
Read the passage and extract the general rule: the captive's inheritance is identical to that of any other Muslim.
2
Recognise the condition
Note the condition attached: 'as long as he does not leave his religion.' This is the key factor that maintains normal inheritance rights.
Leaving Islam means apostasy (riddah).
3
Understand the consequence of apostasy
If the captive apostatises, his ruling becomes that of a missing person (mafqud). Consider what mafqud means: an heir whose life or death is uncertain.
The estate is not distributed immediately; it is held until the mafqud's status is resolved.
4
Differentiate from common misconceptions
Contrast with false assumptions: captivity alone does not cut off inheritance rights, nor does it automatically make the captive a missing person.
Only apostasy triggers the change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السراجية : حكم الأسير كحكم سائر المسلمين في الميراث ما لم يفارق دينه ، فإن فارق دينه فحكمه حكم المفقود.
In al-Sirajiyyah: The ruling of the captive is like the ruling of other Muslims in inheritance as long as he does not leave his religion. If he leaves his religion, his ruling is like the ruling of the missing person (mafqud).
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This concise ruling from al-Sirajiyyah states the legal status of a captive (asir) in inheritance law. As long as the captive remains a Muslim, he is considered like any other free Muslim for purposes of inheritance: he can inherit from and be inherited from normally. However, if he renounces Islam while in captivity, his status changes to that of a mafqud (missing person), meaning his estate is held in abeyance until his death is confirmed or a certain period passes. This ruling highlights that captivity alone does not affect inheritance rights; only apostasy does.

Key Terms
الأسير (al-asīr): A captive taken during war; here specifically a Muslim captive in enemy hands.
المفقود (al-mafqūd): A missing person whose whereabouts and status (alive or dead) are unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Sirajiyyah, what happens to the inheritance of a Muslim captive who apostatises?
A) He loses all inheritance rights permanently.
B) He is treated like a missing person (mafqud).
C) He inherits normally but his share is given to charity.
D) His inheritance is postponed until he returns to Islam.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim captive retains full inheritance rights as long as he remains Muslim; apostasy changes his status to that of a missing person.
